Clarifying 'or from other than it' – Nullification from Each Passage
Critical commentary on the phrase 'or from other than it'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that nullification of wudu occurs from each of the two passages individually, and apply it to distinguish which bodily excretions (worms, wind) nullify wudu.

Core Idea
Nullification occurs from each passage individually, not from both together.

This lesson examines a critical commentary on the phrase 'or from other than it' in the context of wudu nullifiers. The commentator refines the interpretation to emphasize that a nullifier is effective when it exits from any one of the two passages individually, not necessarily both. This grammatical nuance has direct practical implications for determining what breaks wudu, such as worms from the anus or urethra.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic issue
The phrase 'or from other than it' contains a pronoun 'it' that refers back to something. Depending on the manuscript, it may refer to 'one of the two passages' or to the dual 'two passages'. Clarify the referent.
The pronoun resolution affects whether nullification requires excretion from both passages or just one.
2
Understand the two interpretations
Does 'from the two passages' mean from both collectively or from each individually? The text states that the nullifier is not what comes out from both together, but from each one individually.
3
Apply to specific cases: worms and wind
Apply the rule: a worm exiting from the anus or the urethra, or wind from the anus, each nullifies wudu because they exit from one passage individually.
4
Recognize the correction of a claim
The commentator refutes the claim that nullification by a vaginal worm is unanimous; the disagreement about wind and certain worms is acknowledged.
This shows that even within Hanafi commentaries, ikhtilaf exists.
5
Note the grammatical resolution
The preferred interpretation is that 'what came out of the two passages' means from each one individually, solving the pronoun problem.

Book of Purification: Ablution. 'or from other than it' page 313. ('or from other than it' [1] From 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj' and 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' (1) from 'al-Jawhara al-Nayyira': That the nullification by a vaginal worm is unanimous is not correct. It is also permissible to refer to both the worm and the two winds. As for the disagreement regarding the two winds, it is clear. As for the worm, it is due to considering some of its individuals, namely the worm of the vulva and the penis. The second: Regarding his specification of the aforementioned worm in the commentary as exiting from the anus, for the apparent meaning is general, including it and the worm of the vulva and the penis; rather, if the word 'exiting' were made an adjective for both the worm and the wind, it would not be far-fetched. The third: Regarding his statement 'lest repetition occur', for such repetition, where the ruling is mentioned explicitly in one place and implicitly in another, is not objectionable. The fourth: That the repetition he fled from is necessary according to his choice of interpreting the worm as the anal worm as well, for the commentator will explicitly state its ruling with his words 'As for that which exits from the anus, it nullifies'... etc. The fifth: Regarding his statement 'and also it necessitates'... etc., for that which is necessary is accepted as you have known. The sixth: Regarding his statement 'and the commentator will explicitly state'... etc., it is an incorrect reference, for the commentator's statement in what follows does not indicate the negation of disagreement about it. [1] His statement: 'or from other than it' means that which exits from other than the two passages, so it is conjoined to his statement 'one of the two passages', and its pronoun refers back to 'one'. This is according to some copies. As for according to others which omit the word 'one', referring the pronoun of 'other' to the dual is problematic. The examiners have adopted cold interpretations to resolve this, which we have mentioned their falsity in 'al-Si'ayah' (3). The most appropriate is to say: the meaning of 'what came out of the two passages' is what came out from each one of the two passages, because it is apparent that the nullifier is not what came out from both together, but rather what came out from each one individually. Then there is no problem in referring the pronoun.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (hāshiyah) on a previous explanation of the phrase 'or from other than it' in the context of nullifiers of wudū'. The commentator addresses six points of objection or clarification. First, he cites a claim from 'al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj' and 'al-Durr al-Mukhtār' that the nullification by a vaginal worm is unanimous, but he rejects this as incorrect, noting that the disagreement about wind is obvious and about worms concerns specific types. Second, he argues that the commentator's specification of the worm as coming from the anus is too narrow; the general meaning includes worms from the vulva and penis, and even wind. Third, he defends the possibility of repetition in the ruling without it being problematic. Fourth, he points out that the repetition the commentator tried to avoid is actually necessary given his own interpretation. Fifth, he dismisses an objection about necessity. Sixth, he criticizes the commentator's reference to a later passage as not actually resolving the ikhtilāf. Then he explains the phrase 'or from other than it' as referring to what exits from other than the two passages, clarifying grammatical issues with the pronoun and endorsing the interpretation that what nullifies is what comes out from each passage individually, not both together.

Key Terms
السبيلين (al-sabīlayn): The two passages: the anterior (front) and posterior (anus) for elimination.
الناقض (al-nāqiḍ): That which invalidates wudū'; the text discusses whether certain excretions are nullifiers.
الدودة (al-dūdah): Worm; a type of impurity that may exit from the body and is subject to differing rulings on nullification.
الريحين (al-rīḥayn): The two winds: wind exiting from the front (urethra) and back (anus).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the correct interpretation of 'what came out of the two passages'?
A) Both passages together must exit something for nullification.
B) Nothing nullifies because 'two passages' is ambiguous.
C) Exiting from one passage individually is sufficient to nullify.
D) Only wind from the anus nullifies, not worms.

Key Takeaway
Nullification of wudu occurs from excretion out of each of the two passages individually, not from both together.

Conditions for Nullification from Non-Passages
Explanation of three conditions for nullification by non-passage impurities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for nullification of wudu by substances from non-passages and apply the limb classification to determine if nullification occurs.

Core Idea
A substance from non-passages nullifies wudu only if najis, flowing, and reaches a limb that requires purification.

In the Hanafi school, not every impurity exiting the body breaks wudu. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that for substances coming from other than the two passages (i.e., not from the privy parts), three conditions must be met: the substance must be impure (najis), it must be flowing (sā'il), and it must reach a place that requires purification (mā yutahhar). The place is further categorized into three types of limbs, which determines the ruling. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the rules of taharah in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
From the passage, extract that the exiting substance must be najis, flowing, and reach a place requiring purification. Note the objection that 'flowing' is redundant but explained as a descriptive attribute.
The commentator defends the inclusion of 'flowing' as completing the intended meaning.
2
Classify the limbs into three types
Read the detail about three limb categories: (1) internal from all sides (e.g., heart, brain), (2) external from all sides (e.g., hand, face), (3) internal from one aspect and external from another (e.g., mouth, nose). Understand which require purification in wudu and/or ghusl.
Type 1 requires no purification; type 2 requires purification in both; type 3 requires purification in ghusl only.
3
Apply the examples
Work through the examples: blood reaching the nasal passage (type 3) nullifies wudu; urine or blood reaching the urethra (which is itself a passage) does not nullify because it is not a limb requiring purification.
The ruling is based on the destination limb, not the source of the impurity.
4
Address the objection about 'wudu'
The commentator anticipates that mentioning 'wudu' might be superfluous since no limb requires purification only in wudu. However, the term is used for completeness in the explanation of limb types.
The phrase 'mā yutahhar' covers both wudu and ghusl needs.

الغسل.

"If it is najis flowing" to what it purifies. "If it is najis (1) flowing to what it purifies": i.e., to a place that must be purified in general, either in wudu or in ghusl. [1] His statement: "If it is" refers to what exits from other than the two passages; for what exits from the two passages is a nullifier without restriction, and the condition that the exiting substance be najis and flowing to what purifies is only required for what exits from other than them. [2] His statement: "flowing" from flow. It was objected that its mention is superfluous because the exiting substance is only described as najis after flowing, not before, so non-flowing is not najis. Thus his statement "najis" suffices for mentioning flow. It was answered: that it is a descriptive attribute mentioned to complete the intended meaning. [3] His statement: "either in wudu or in ghusl"; its detail: limbs are of three types: a type that does not require purification either in ghusl or wudu, which are the internal from every aspect, like the heart, brain matter, cavities of veins, and the like. Flow of blood and similar to such places does not nullify wudu, for blood flows from one place to another inside the body and does not affect anything. A type that requires purification in both wudu and ghusl, like the hand and face, which are external from every aspect. A type that requires purification in ghusl but not in wudu, like the mouth and nose: these are limbs internal from one aspect and external from another, as will be discussed in the 'section on ghusl' if Allah wills. Flow of najis to these two types nullifies wudu. Hence they said: if blood exits to the nasal passage, wudu is nullified; but if urine or blood exits from a place to the passage of the penis, wudu is not nullified. From this we know that 'amma' and 'aw' in the commentary are for preventing vacancy, not for preventing combination. If you say: the mention of wudu is superfluous, since there is no limb that requires purification in wudu but not in ghusl. (1) His statement 'najis' is a precaution against saliva and the like, for their exit does not nullify. See: 'Hawashi Multaqatat ala al-Niqayah' (4).
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage expands on the condition for nullification of wudū' by substances exiting from other than the two passages. The commentator explains that the condition is threefold: the substance must be najis (impure), it must be flowing (sā'il), and it must reach a place that requires purification (mā yutahhar). The place that requires purification is divided into three categories: limbs that are internal from all sides (like heart, brain) — no purification required in wudu or ghusl, so flow there does not nullify; limbs that are external from all sides (like hand, face) — purification required in both wudu and ghusl; and limbs that are internal from one aspect and external from another (like mouth, nose) — purification required in ghusl but not in wudu. The nullification only occurs when the najis reaches the second or third category. Examples are given: blood reaching the nasal passage nullifies wudu because the nose is of the third type; urine or blood reaching the urethra (penis passage) does not nullify because it is a passage itself. The objection that 'wudu' is superfluously mentioned is anticipated and answered by noting that the categories cover cases where purification is needed in wudu only? Actually, the commentary states that there is no limb requiring purification in wudu alone; but the mention is necessary to explain the combination. The final note clarifies that saliva does not nullify because it is not najis.

Key Terms
سائل (sā'il): Flowing; a condition for nullification that the exiting impurity must be liquid and flow to another place.
ما يطهر (mā yutahhar): A place that requires purification; either in wudu or ghusl; limbs are categorized based on this.
نجس (najis): Impure substance; saliva is excluded as it is not najis.

A) Wudu is not nullified because the arm is not a passage.
B) Wudu is nullified because it reaches a limb that requires purification in ghusl but not wudu.
C) Wudu is nullified only if the blood is flowing.
D) Wudu is nullified because it is najis and reaches an external limb.

Key Takeaway
For substances from non-passages, wudu is nullified only when the impurity is najis, flowing, and reaches a limb that requires purification (external or mixed, not purely internal).

Taharah — Purification

Conditions for Nullification of Wudu by Impurities
Expounds conditions for wudu nullification by impure discharges

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which an impure discharge from a non-private part nullifies wudu in the Hanafi school and identify the primary evidence from the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh.

Core Idea
Only flowing impurity reaching a washable part nullifies wudu — not every discharge from the body.

Not every impurity that exits the body breaks wudu. According to the Hanafi school, discharges from the two passages (front and back) always nullify wudu regardless of flow. But for all other discharges — like blood from a wound — two conditions must be met: the impurity must be flowing (sā'il) and it must reach a body part that requires purification (ilā mā yuṭahhar). This lesson unpacks these conditions and the evidence behind them, including the authentic hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh about istihadah (chronic uterine bleeding).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an impure discharge (e.g., blood, pus) from a non-private part. The ruling in the Hanafi school: it nullifies wudu only if two conditions are met — the impurity is flowing (sā'il) and it reaches a place that must be purified in wudu or ghusl.
Discharges from the two passages always nullify wudu regardless of these conditions.
2
Locate the primary evidence (dalil)
The strong evidence is the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh, reported by al-Bukhari and others, in which the Prophet instructed her to perform wudu for each prayer due to chronic blood (istihadah). This indicates that such impure discharge requires wudu, i.e., it nullifies the previous wudu.
The hadith is authentic (sahih) and directly addresses a discharge from a non-private part (the uterus, which is not one of the two passages).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (legal import)
The command 'توضئي لكل صلاة' (perform wudu for each prayer) shows that the istihadah blood necessitates wudu. In Hanafi usul, a command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is a contrary indicator. Thus, the blood nullifies wudu when it reaches a place that must be washed in wudu.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the blood being flowing (sā'il) and reaching a washable area; the hadith implies both conditions.
4
Note the inter-madhhab dispute (ikhtilaf)
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that no discharge from non-private parts nullifies wudu. His evidence includes hadith that the Prophet cupped and prayed without wudu, and that he vomited and performed wudu voluntarily (not as obligation).
The Hanafi response is that those hadith are weak (da'if) and cannot outweigh the authentic hadith of Fatima.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preponderance) and practical consequence
The Hanafi position is muftā bihi (relied upon for fatwa). The practical difference: In the Hanafi school, if blood flows from a wound to the hand (a washable area), wudu is nullified. In the Shafi'i school, wudu remains valid in that case.
The stronger dalil and the principle of taking the authentic hadith over weak ones supports the Hanafi view.

Source Text

إنّما هو إغناء الأوّل عن الثانى، لا إغناء الثاني عن الأول. فإن قلت : فلم لم يكتف على ذكر الغسل فقط. قلت : توضيحاً وتتميماً، لا سيما والمقام مقام الوضوء. قوله : لا ينقض الوضوء؛ لما أخرجه الدارقطني في «سننه» عن أنس قال : احتجم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فصلى ولم يتوضأ ولم يزد على غسل محاجمه، وأخرج أيضاً عن ثوبان : «أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قاء فتوضا، فقلت : أفريضة الوضوء من القيء، قال: «لو Its meaning: to a place that must be purified generally, either in wudu or in ghusl. He said: 'if it is' meaning the discharge from other than the two passages; because discharge from the two passages nullifies without restriction, but the condition that the discharge be impure and flowing to what must be purified is only required for what exits from other than them. He said: 'flowed' from flow; objection was raised that its mention is redundant because the discharge is only described as impure after flow, not before, so non-flowing is not impure, thus his word 'impure' suffices for mentioning flow. And it was answered: it is a clarifying attribute mentioned to complete the objective. He said: 'either in wudu or in ghusl'; its detail: the body parts are three categories: a part that must not be purified in either ghusl or wudu, which are the internal from every aspect like the heart, brain matter, cavities of veins and the like, and flow of blood etc. into such places does not nullify wudu, for blood flows from place to place inside the body and does not harm anything. And a part that must be purified in both wudu and ghusl, like the hand and face, which are external from every aspect. And a part that must be purified in ghusl but not in wudu, like the mouth and nose: they are parts internal from one aspect and external from another as will come in the discussion of ghusl, and flow of impurity into these two parts nullifies wudu; hence they said: if blood exits to the nasal cavity wudu is nullified, but if urine or blood exits from a place to the urethral meatus wudu is not nullified. By this it is known that 'amma' and 'aw' in the commentary are for mutual exclusion, not for conjunction. If you say: mentioning wudu is superfluous since there is no part that must be purified in wudu but not in ghusl. I say: the superfluity would only be necessary if mentioning wudu came after ghusl, but if it came before then no, for the defect is only making the first sufficient for the second, not the second for the first. If you say: why did he not suffice with mentioning only ghusl? I say: for clarification and completion, especially since the context is about wudu. He said: 'it does not nullify wudu' because of what al-Daraqutni narrated in his Sunan from Anas: the Prophet cupped and prayed without performing wudu and did no more than wash the cupping sites; and also from Thawban: the Prophet vomited and performed wudu, so I said: is wudu from vomiting obligatory? He said: if it were obligatory, you would find it in the Quran; both chains are weak, and there are other hadith we mentioned with their strengths and weaknesses along with the evidence of our scholars in al-Sa'ayah. The strongest evidence of our scholars is what al-Bukhari and the authors of the Sunan and others narrated that Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh said: O Messenger of Allah, I have istihadah and do not become pure, should I abandon prayer? He said: no, it is only a vein - i.e. blood from a vein, not menstruation - so when the menstruation comes, leave prayer, and when it ends, wash the blood from yourself, pray, and perform wudu for each prayer until that time comes.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on what nullifies wudu. The base text from al-Wiqayah states: 'if it is impure and flows to what is to be purified'. The commentator explains that this condition applies only to discharges from other than the two passages (front and back), because discharges from the two passages always nullify wudu regardless of these conditions. He addresses an objection that mentioning 'flow' is redundant because impurity is only established after flow, so the word 'impure' already implies flow. The response is that it is a clarifying attribute. He then divides body parts into three categories: (1) internal parts that require no purification in wudu or ghusl, where flow does not nullify wudu; (2) external parts that require purification in both, where flow does nullify; (3) semi-internal parts like mouth and nose that require purification only in ghusl, where flow also nullifies wudu because they are external in one aspect. He gives examples: blood reaching the nasal cavity nullifies wudu, but urine or blood reaching the urethral meatus does not, because the urethra is internal and does not require purification. He then addresses a potential objection about why wudu is mentioned separately from ghusl, explaining that both are necessary for completeness. Finally, he presents the inter-madhhab dispute: al-Shafi'i holds that discharges from non-private parts never nullify wudu. The commentator cites hadith evidence, including the well-known hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh, where the Prophet instructed her to perform wudu for each prayer due to istihadah (chronic blood discharge). This hadith is the strongest proof for the Hanafi position that impure discharges do nullify wudu when they flow to a washable area.

Key Terms
نجس (najis): Impure substance; specifically, here it refers to any liquid impurity (e.g., blood, urine) that exits from non-private parts.
سائل (sā'il): Flowing liquid; the impurity must be fluid enough to flow from its origin to another location.
إلى ما يطهر (ilā mā yuṭahhar): To a place that must be purified, i.e., a body part that requires washing in wudu or ghusl, such as the hands, face, mouth, nose, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does blood from a wound on the arm nullify wudu?
A) Always, if it is impure.
B) Only if it flows to a place that must be washed in wudu or ghusl.
C) Only if it flows to any part of the body.
D) Never, because it is from a non-private part.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, only a flowing impurity reaching a place that requires purification nullifies wudu; not every discharge from the body.

Cupping Blood and Wudu Nullification: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Presents Hanafi ruling with proof and Shafi'i objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between cupping blood and other blood regarding wudu nullification, and state the evidence for the exception.

Core Idea
Cupping blood does not nullify wudu — but other blood does — because the Prophet's practice creates a specific exception.

This passage presents a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over what nullifies wudu. While both agree that cupping blood does not break wudu, they differ on the underlying principle. The Hanafi school argues that all impure substances exiting the body nullify wudu except where there is explicit prophetic exemption, as in the case of cupping. The Shafi'i school holds the general rule that only exits from the two private parts nullify. Understanding this dispute reveals how madhhab reasoning uses specific hadith to refine general rules.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its apparent exception
The Hanafi school rules that cupping blood does not nullify wudu, but other blood (e.g., from wounds or nosebleeds) does. Identify why cupping is treated differently.
The exception is based on a specific prophetic practice.
2
Identify the Shafi'i general rule
The Shafi'i school holds that only exits from the two private parts (front and back) nullify wudu. Therefore, cupping blood is not a nullifier by default, not because of an exception.
Their general principle is that any substance exiting from other than the two passages does not nullify.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof: the cupping hadith
Analyze the hadith of Anas: the Prophet was cupped and prayed without wudu. This demonstrates that cupping blood does not nullify. Note that the chain is considered weak by some, but is supported by other evidence.
This hadith establishes the exception for cupping.
4
Examine the Hanafi's stronger proof: the istihadah hadith
The hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh regarding chronic uterine bleeding shows that blood from a vein (istihadah) does nullify wudu because the Prophet ordered her to perform wudu for each prayer. This proves that blood from other than the two passages can nullify, which is the basis for the general Hanafi rule.
This hadith is authentic and refutes the Shafi'i generalization.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference
The practical consequence: according to Hanafis, any flowing blood (except cupping) nullifies wudu; according to Shafi'is, only exits from the two passages nullify. Thus, a nosebleed would break wudu for Hanafis, but not for Shafi'is.
This difference affects daily practice in acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند الشافعي له الخارج من غير السبيلين لا ينقض الوضوء. قوله : لا ينقض الوضوء ; لما أخرجه الدارقطني في «سننه» عن أنس قال : احتجم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فصلى ولم يتوضأ ولم يزد على غسل محاجمه، وأخرج أيضاً عن ثوبان : «أن رسول الله لقاء فتوضا ، فقلت : أفريضة الوضوء من القيء، قال: «لو كان فريضة لوجدته في القرآن» وسندهما ضعيف، وفي الباب أخبار أخر ذكرناها مع ما لها وما عليها مع أدلة أصحابنا في «السعاية». وأقوى وجوه أصحابنا ما أخرجه البخاري وأصحاب السنن وغيرهم أن فاطمة بنت أبي حبيش رضي الله عنها قالت : يا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إني استحاض فلا أطهر، أفادعُ الصلاة؟ قال : لا إنّما ذلك عرق - أي دم عرق وليست بالحيضة – فإذا أقبلت الحيضة فدعي الصلاة، وإذا أدبرت فاغسلي عنك الدم وصلي وتوضئي لكل صلاة حتى يجيء ذلك الوقت.
And according to al-Shafi'i, what exits from other than the two passages does not nullify wudu. His statement: It does not nullify wudu, based on what al-Daraqutni narrated in his Sunan from Anas who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was cupped and then prayed without performing wudu and did not do more than washing the cupping sites. He also narrated from Thawban: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) vomited and then performed wudu, so I said: Is wudu obligatory from vomiting? He said: If it were obligatory, it would have been found in the Quran.' Their chains are weak, and there are other reports in this chapter which we have mentioned along with their strengths and weaknesses and the proofs of our companions in 'al-Sa'ayah'. And the strongest proof of our companions is what al-Bukhari and the authors of the Sunan and others narrated: that Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh (may Allah be pleased with her) said: O Messenger of Allah, I am a woman who experiences istihadah and do not become pure, should I abandon prayer? He said: No, that is only a vein - meaning blood from a vein, not menstruation - so when the menstruation comes, leave the prayer, and when it ends, wash the blood from yourself and pray, and perform wudu for every prayer until that time comes.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i regarding what nullifies wudu. The author first states the Shafi'i position: anything exiting from other than the two private parts (front and back) does not nullify wudu. Then he presents the Hanafi ruling that cupping blood does not nullify wudu, as stated in the matn. To support this, he cites two hadiths: first, the hadith of Anas that the Prophet was cupped and prayed without wudu, indicating that cupping blood does not nullify wudu. Second, the hadith of Thawban about vomiting, though its chain is weak. The strongest Hanafi evidence is the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh regarding istihadah (chronic uterine bleeding). In that hadith, the Prophet commands her to perform wudu for each prayer, implying that the bleeding (which is from a vein, not the private parts) does nullify wudu. This is a key Hanafi proof that blood exiting from other than the private parts can nullify wudu, but cupping blood is an exception based on explicit prophetic practice. The difference between the schools is that al-Shafi'i holds a general rule that only exits from the two passages nullify wudu, while the Hanafis hold that any impure substance exiting the body nullifies except where there is a specific exemption. The thamarah (practical consequence) is that according to the Hanafis, blood from a wound or nosebleed nullifies wudu if it flows, while according to al-Shafi'i it does not.

Key Terms
الخارج (al-kharij): Anything that exits from the body, such as blood, pus, etc.
السبيلين (al-sabilayn): The two passages: the front (urethra/vagina) and the back (anus).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following does NOT nullify wudu?
A) Blood from a nosebleed
B) Blood from cupping
C) Blood from a wound that flows
D) Pus from a boil

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between cupping blood (excepted) and other blood (nullifier) based on prophetic practice, while the Shafi'i school applies a general rule that only exits from the two passages nullify wudu.

Distinguishing Najas from Nijis in Hanafi Terminology
Defines the term najas and its variant readings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between najas and nijis in Hanafi terminology and correctly apply each term to substances and contaminated objects.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, najas is the impurity itself; nijis is the quality of being impure.

The Arabic term for impurity carries a precise distinction in Hanafi legal usage. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that the word najas (with fatha) refers to the actual substance of impurity, while nijis (with kasra) describes something that has become impure. Understanding this difference is essential for properly applying rules of purification (taharah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term
Locate the phrase in the passage that introduces the definition: 'الرواية النَّجَس... هو عين النَّجاسة' and 'بكسر الجيم فما لا يكون طاهراً'.
Focus on the contrast between the two vowellings.
2
Note the variant vowellings
The author explains that the narration uses 'al-najas' with a fatha on the jim, and contrasts it with 'al-nijis' with kasra. This vowel difference signals a different meaning.
The vowel is not arbitrary; it distinguishes substance from description.
3
Differentiate substance from attribute
Understand that najas (fatha) is the 'ayn (essence) of impurity, like urine or blood. Nijis (kasra) is an adjective meaning 'not pure,' applied to objects that have been contaminated.
Think: najas = the impurity itself; nijis = the condition of carrying impurity.
4
Compare technical and linguistic meanings
The passage gives the linguistic root (najisa yanjasu) and notes that in language, both najis and najas are used as adjectives. But in juristic terminology, the distinction is fixed.
The technical usage overrides the general linguistic one.
5
Apply the distinction to a practical example
Classify a substance like blood as najas. Classify a garment stained with blood as nijis. State why the precision matters for rulings on purification.
The same substance can cause different rulings depending on whether it is the source or the material it touches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والروايةُ النَّجَس ، بفتح قلت : إنما يلزم الاستدراك لو كان ذكرُ الوضوء بعد الغسل، وأما إذا كان قبله فكلاً ; فإن المعيوب إنّما هو إغناء الأوّل عن الثانى ، لا إغناء الثاني عن الأول. فإن قلت : فلم لم يكتف على ذكر الغسل فقط. قلت : توضيحاً وتتميماً، لا سيما والمقام مقام الوضوء. الجيم : وهو عينُ النَّجاسة ، وأما بكسر الجيم، فما لا يكون طاهراً، هذا في اصطلاح الفقهاء. وأَمَّا فِي اللُّغةِ فيقال : نَحِسَ الشَّيءُ يَنْجُس، فهو نَحِسٌ ونَجَس.
And the narration [has] 'al-najas' with a fatha [on the jim]. I say: The correction is only necessary if wudu were mentioned after ghusl, but if it was before, then no; for the defect is only the sufficiency of the first over the second, not of the second over the first. If you say: Why was it not sufficient to mention only ghusl? I say: For clarification and completion, especially since the context is about wudu. The word with the jim: it is the essence of impurity. As for with the kasra of the jim, it is what is not pure. This is the terminology of the jurists. As for in language, it is said: a thing became impure (najisa), so it is impure (najis and najas).
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a definition and parsing exercise of the term 'najas' (impurity). The author first addresses the variant reading of the word in the matn: 'al-najas' with a fatha on the jim. He then explains the reason why the matn mentions both wudu and ghusl in its discussion: if wudu had been mentioned after ghusl, a correction might be needed, but since it is mentioned first, no correction is required. The author then provides the technical definition used by the Hanafi jurists: 'al-najas' (with fatha) refers to the actual substance of impurity, such as urine or blood, while 'al-nijis' (with kasra) refers to something that is not pure, such as a garment stained with impurity. In linguistic terms, the verb najisa (an-najasu) means to become impure, and the adjective can be najis or najas. The Hanafi school maintains this distinction to ensure precise legal application regarding purity and impurity.

Key Terms
النَّجَس (بفتح الجيم) (al-najas (with fatha on jim)): The actual substance of impurity, such as urine, blood, or wine.
النَّجِس (بكسر الجيم) (al-nijis (with kasra on jim)): A description meaning 'not pure,' applicable to objects that have been contaminated with impurity, like a soiled garment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi terminology, what does 'al-najas' (with fatha) refer to?
A) A garment that is impure
B) The actual substance of impurity like urine
C) Any object that is not pure
D) A person in a state of impurity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes najas (substantive impurity) from nijis (the attribute of being impure), a distinction that affects the rulings of purification.

Nullification of Wudu by Squeezed Wound Blood: The Hanafi Position
States the condition of flow and ruling on squeezed wound blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nullification of wudu by blood from a squeezed boil and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Zufar.

Core Idea
Wudu is not nullified by blood that exits only due to external force, such as squeezing, if the blood would not have flowed on its own.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between spontaneous blood flow (which nullifies wudu) and blood that exits only due to external pressure (which does not). This lesson explores the ruling on squeezing a boil, where the blood would not have come out without squeezing. The author uses the istihadah hadith and qiyas to cupping to support the position that forced extraction does not nullify wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The author states that if blood does not flow beyond the orifice, wudu is not nullified. Then he addresses squeezing: if the boil would not have released without squeezing, wudu remains intact even if the blood flows.
Note the condition: 'if he had not squeezed it, it would not have exceeded'.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar holds that any exit of blood, even without flow, nullifies wudu. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) disagree.
Zufar's view is not mufta bihi.
3
Analyze the proof from the istihadah hadith
The Prophet ﷺ said of istihadah blood: 'That is only a vein.' This indicates that blood from a vein that flows spontaneously nullifies, but forced extraction is like cupping and does not nullify.
The hadith is ahad, hence zanni al-thubut, but sufficient for the ruling.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority view is relied upon for fatwa, as it is based on stronger analogy and textual indications.
The author implicitly prefers this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قال : سال ; لأنَّهُ إذا لم يتجاوز المخرج لا ينقض الوضوء عندنا ، وينقض عندَ زُفَر له. وكذا إذا عَصَرَ القُرْحةَ فتجاوز، وكان بحال لو لم يعصر لم يتجاوزه. فقوله : إنما ذلك عرق مشير إلى أن كونه من العرق له تأثير في نقض الطهارة. [١] قوله : عين النجاسة ; أي ذاتُ النجاسة من حيث هي نجاسة : كالبول والدم المسفوح ، فالنسبة بينه وبين المعنى الثاني عموم وخصوص مطلق ; فإنّ كل نجاسة يصدق عليه أنه ليس بطاهر، وقد يكون غير الطاهر غير ذات نجاسة : كالثوب النجس ، ولا تصغ إلى ما في حل المشكلات من أن بينهما عموماً من وجه. [٢] قوله : وكذا إذا عَصَرَ القُرْحة ; بضم القافِ وجاء بالفتح أيضاً ; صرح به في «المصباح المنير ، وهو بالفارسية آبله وعصرها : إخراج ما فيها باليد ونحوها ، ويقال للخارج بالعصر : العصارة. وحاصله : أنه إذا كانت القرحة بحال لو لم يعصره أحد لم يخرج منه شيء فعصرها بالأصبع فخرج منها دم أو صديد وتجاوز المخرج لم ينتقض وضوؤه ; لأنه [متعلق]
And he only said 'flowed' because if it does not go beyond the exit, it does not nullify wudu according to us, but it does according to Zufar. Likewise, if one squeezed a boil and it exceeded [the exit], and it was in a state that if he had not squeezed it would not have exceeded, then [wudu is not nullified]. So his statement 'that is only a vein' indicates that being from a vein has an effect on nullifying purification. [1] His statement: 'essence of impurity' means the very substance of impurity in terms of being impure, like urine and flowing blood. So the relationship between it and the second meaning is general and specific in an absolute sense; for every impurity is true that it is not pure, and sometimes that which is not pure is not the essence of impurity, like the impure garment. And do not listen to what is in 'Hall al-Mushkilat' that there is a general and specific from one perspective. [2] His statement: 'Likewise, if he squeezed the boil' – with damm on the qaf, and it is also read with fatha; it is stated in al-Misbah al-Munir, and it is in Persian 'ābila'. Squeezing it means extracting what is in it with the hand or similar means, and what is extracted by squeezing is called 'asara'. The summary: if the boil was in a state that if no one squeezed it, nothing would come out, so he squeezed it with his finger and blood or pus came out and exceeded the exit, his wudu is not nullified because...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the condition for nullification of wudu by exiting blood. The author explains why the matn used the word 'saala' (flowed): because if blood or other substance does not flow beyond the exit point (i.e., it remains at the orifice), wudu is not nullified according to the Hanafis, but Zufar holds that it does nullify even if it does not flow. The author then addresses the case of squeezing a boil: if a person squeezes a boil and the blood or pus exits and flows beyond the exit, but it was of such a nature that it would not have come out without squeezing, then wudu is not nullified. The reason is that the exit in this case is due to his own action (squeezing), and the ruling is based on the principle that what exits due to an external cause does not nullify wudu, unlike spontaneous flow. The author connects this to the istihadah hadith, where the Prophet said 'that is only a vein,' indicating that blood from a vein has an effect in nullifying purification. The key distinction is whether the blood would have exited naturally without external pressure; if it would not, then wudu remains intact. This is a specific ruling in the Hanafi school, and Zufar's opposing view is noted but not adopted.

Key Terms
القُرْحة (al-qur'hah): A boil or sore on the skin that contains pus or blood.
العصارة (al-'asārah): The substance extracted by squeezing, such as blood or pus from a boil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wudu is nullified because blood has exited
B) Wudu remains valid because the blood exited due to squeezing
C) Wudu is nullified only if the blood is from the private parts
D) Wudu is nullified according to all scholars

Key Takeaway
Wudu is not nullified by blood or pus that exits due to squeezing if the substance would not have flowed on its own.

Condition of Flow for Wudu Nullification from Wounds
Clarifies the condition of flow for wudu nullification when impurities exit a wound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that wudu is not nullified by impurities from wounds unless they flow beyond the exit, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar.

Core Idea
Wudu is not nullified by blood from a wound unless it flows; the nullifier is spontaneous exit, not induced extraction.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key detail in Hanafi taharah: when does blood or pus from a wound break wudu? The school requires the impurity to flow beyond the exit point. The commentator details the ruling, contrasts it with Zufar's view, and applies it to everyday actions like biting food or blowing the nose. Understanding this condition prevents unnecessary wudu renewal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the emergence of impurity (blood/pus) from a wound. The ruling according to Hanafis: wudu is not nullified if the impurity does not flow beyond the exit.
The nullification of wudu is tied to the condition of flow (sailan).
2
State the Hanafi condition and its proof
Hanafis require the impurity to flow (sala) beyond the wound opening. This is based on a hadith of a wounded companion and the analogy that such blood is like sweat, not like urine or feces from the two passages.
The distinction between khuruj (spontaneous exit) and ikhraj (induced exit) is central.
3
Explain the dispute with Zufar
Zufar argues that any exit of impurity nullifies wudu, analogizing all body emissions to the two passages. Hanafis respond that wound impurities are not habitual and that the nullifier is only spontaneous exit, not induced extraction.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Zufar was a student of Abu Hanifa).
4
Analyze the squeezed pustule case
If a pustule is squeezed and the content flows, early Hanafi texts said wudu is not nullified because it is ikhraj. Later authorities (al-Kafi, al-Nihaya, etc.) ruled nullification because the exit is a necessary consequence of squeezing, and the nullifier is the exit itself regardless of how it occurs.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is nullification when squeezing causes flow.
5
Apply the rule to everyday scenarios
Biting food, picking teeth, inserting a finger in the nose, blowing nose, spitting, or a needle prick: if blood appears but does not flow, wudu remains valid. This applies unless the exit is induced and the flow is a necessary outcome (like squeezing).
Contrary to Zufar, Hanafis maintain validity in these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجيم : وهو عين النجاسة ، وأما بكسر الجيم، فما لا يكون طاهراً، هذا في اصطلاح الفقهاء). وأما في اللغة فيقال : نَحِسَ الشيء يَنْجُس، فهو نَحِسٌ ونَجَس. وإنما قال : سال ؛ لأنَّهُ إذا لم يتجاوز المخرج لا ينقض الوضوء عندنا ، وينقض عندَ زفر له. وكذا إذا عصر القرحة فتجاوز، وكان بحال لو لم يعصر لم يتجاوزه. فقوله : إنما ذلك عرق مشير إلى أن كونه من العرق له تأثير في نقض الطهارة (٢). [١] قوله : عين النجاسة ؛ أي ذات النجاسة من حيث هي نجاسة : كالبول والدم المسفوح ، فالنسبة بينه وبين المعنى الثاني عموم وخصوص مطلق ؛ فإن كل نجاسة يصدق عليه أنه ليس بطاهر، وقد يكون غير الطاهر غير ذات نجاسة : كالثوب النجس ، ولا تصغ إلى ما في حل المشكلات من أن بينهما عموماً من وجه. [٢] قوله : وكذا إذا عصر القرحة ؛ بضم القاف وجاء بالفتح أيضاً ؛ صرح به في «المصباح المنير»، وهو بالفارسية آبله وعصرها : إخراج ما فيها باليد ونحوها ، ويقال للخارج بالعصر : العصارة. وحاصله : أنه إذا كانت القرحة بحال لو لم يعصره أحد لم يخرج منه شيء فعصرها بالأصبع فخرج منها دم أو صديد وتجاوز المخرج لم ينتقض وضوؤه؛ لأنه إخراج، والناقض إنما هو الخروج، وهذا عند بعض المشايخ منهم «الهداية» (١). والأصح على ما اختاره في «الكافي»، و«النهاية»، و«غاية البيان»، و«فتح القدير» (٢)، و«البزازية» (٣)، وغيرها (4) : هو النقض في هذه الصورة؛ لكون الخروج لازماً للإخراج، والناقض إنما هو الخروج مطلقاً لا الخروج بنفسه فحسب. [١] قوله : وكذا؛ أي لا ينقض الوضوء عندنا بناءً على اشتراط السيلان. إذا عض: أي أخذ شيئاً بأسنانه، يقال: عضضت اللقمة: إذا أمسكتها بالأسنان. أو خلل: أي أدخل الخلال في أسنانه فرأى أثر الدم في الخلال أو ذلك الشيء المعضوض. أو أدخل إصبعه بكسر الهمزة في أنفه، فرأى أثر الدم في الإصبع وغيره. أو استنثر: أي أخرج ما في أنفه مخاطاً كان أو غيره، فخرج من أنفه عَلَقاً، - عَلَقاً بفتحتين يقال للدم الغليظ المنجمد مثل العَدَس بفتحتين: حب معروف –. وكذا إذا بزق فرأى في بزاقه شيئاً قليلا من أثر الدم. وكذا إذا غُرزت إبرة أو شوكة في عضو فأُخْرِجَ منه، فظهر منه الدم، ولم يسل، ففي هذه الصور وأمثالها لا ينتقض الوضوء عندنا، خلافاً لزفر. ووجهه : إن خروج النجاسة مؤثر في زوال الطهارة كالسبيلين.
Al-Jim: It is the substance of impurity. But with kasrah of the jim, it is that which is not pure; this is in the terminology of jurists. As for language, it is said: the thing is najis (impure), so it is najis and najas. And he [the author] only said: 'it flowed' because if it does not go beyond the exit, it does not nullify wudu according to us, but it does nullify according to Zufar. Likewise, if he squeezed a pustule and it exceeded, in a state that if he had not squeezed it would not have exceeded. So his statement: 'that is only sweat' indicates that being from sweat has an effect on nullifying purity (2). [1] His statement: 'substance of impurity' meaning the essence of impurity insofar as it is impurity, like urine and poured blood. So the relationship between it and the second meaning is general and specific in an absolute sense; for every impurity applies to it that it is not pure, and sometimes that which is not pure is not a substance of impurity, like a soiled garment. Do not listen to what is in Hal al-Mushkilat that between them there is general and specific from one aspect. [2] His statement: 'Likewise, if he squeezed the pustule' with dammah of the qaf and it also occurs with fatha; it is stated in al-Misbah al-Munir, and it is in Persian 'ābla' and squeezing it: extracting what is in it by hand and the like. The extract is called 'usara. The summary: if the pustule is in a state that if no one squeezed it, nothing would come out, so he squeezed it with his finger and blood or pus came out and exceeded the exit, his wudu is not nullified; because it is extraction (ikhraj), and the nullifier is only the exit (khuruj). This is according to some scholars, among them the author of al-Hidayah (1). But the correct according to what is chosen in al-Kafi, al-Nihaya, Ghayat al-Bayan, Fath al-Qadir (2), al-Bazzaziyya (3), and others (4): is nullification in this scenario, because the exit is necessary for the extraction, and the nullifier is only the exit absolutely, not the exit by itself alone. [1] His statement: 'Likewise' means: wudu is not nullified according to us, based on the condition of flow. If he bit (i.e., took something with his teeth), it is said: I bit the morsel: if I held it with the teeth. Or picked his teeth (i.e., inserted a toothpick into his teeth and saw a trace of blood on the toothpick or on that bitten thing). Or inserted his finger (with kasrah of the hamza) into his nose and saw a trace of blood on the finger or elsewhere. Or blew his nose (i.e., expelled what is in his nose, whether mucus or other), and blood came out of his nose clotted (with two fatha, meaning thick congealed blood, like lentils (with two fatha: a well-known grain)). Likewise, if he spat and saw in his saliva a small amount of blood trace. Likewise, if a needle or thorn was inserted into a limb and then removed, and blood appeared but did not flow. So in these and similar cases, wudu is not nullified according to us, contrary to Zufar. And his proof: that the exit of impurity has an effect on removing purity like the two passages.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the text of Al-Wiqayah, explaining the conditions for wudu nullification when impurities exit from a wound. The central issue is the requirement of flow (sailan): according to the Hanafi school, if blood or pus emerges from a wound but does not flow beyond the exit point, wudu remains valid. This is based on a hadith where a wounded companion prayed without repeating wudu, and the impurity is analogized to sweat rather than urine or feces. The commentator notes a dispute with Zufar, who holds that any exit of impurity nullifies wudu by analogy to the two passages. The commentator then discusses the case of squeezing a pustule: if squeezing causes flow, some early scholars (like the author of al-Hidayah) ruled that wudu is not nullified because the nullifier is spontaneous exit, not induced exit. However, the relied-upon position according to later authorities (al-Kafi, al-Nihaya, etc.) is that it does nullify because the exit is a necessary consequence of the squeezing, and the nullifier is the exit itself regardless of how it occurs. The commentator then lists various everyday situations—biting food, picking teeth, inserting a finger into the nose, blowing the nose, spitting, pricking with a needle—and rules that in all these, if blood appears but does not flow, wudu is not nullified, contrary to Zufar. Zufar's counterargument is that any impurity exiting the body nullifies purity by analogy to the two natural passages. The Hanafi response emphasizes the distinction between the cause (ikhrāj) and the actual exiting (khurūj), and the requirement that the impurity must exit spontaneously and flow to nullify wudu.

Key Terms
السيلان (as-sailān): The condition of flowing beyond the exit point; required for wudu nullification from wounds.
الخروج (al-khurūj): The act of exiting (impurity emerging from the body). In this text, it is distinguished from 'ikhrāj' (causing to exit).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fiqh discussed in this passage, when does blood from a wound nullify wudu?
A) Always, regardless of amount.
B) Only if it flows beyond the wound exit.
C) Only if it is intentionally squeezed out.
D) Never, blood does not nullify wudu.

Key Takeaway
Wudu is not nullified by blood or pus from a wound unless it flows beyond the exit; the Hanafi condition of sailan distinguishes incidental impurities from habitual ones.

Non-Flowing Blood and Wudu' Nullification: The Hanafi Flow Condition
Intra-Hanafi dispute on blood nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for blood to nullify wudu', distinguish between 'kharuj' and 'bad', and explain Zufar's opposing argument and the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Non-flowing blood does not nullify wudu' because exit requires flow; mere appearance (bad) is not exit.

The Hanafi school places a precise condition on when blood nullifies wudu': the blood must flow (sayalan). Mere appearance of blood, such as from a bite or toothpick, is not considered true exit. This lesson examines the legal basis for this ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar, who analogizes to the two excretory passages.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its condition
Identify the main Hanafi position: non-flowing blood does not nullify wudu'. The key condition is that the blood must flow (sailan) from its original place to be considered exit (kharuj).
This is based on the principle that nullification only occurs with true exit, not mere appearance (bad).
2
Identify Zufar's opposing view and his qiyas
Zufar holds that any exit of impurity, regardless of flow, nullifies wudu' by analogy (qiyas) to the two excretory passages.
The common illa is the exit of impurity; Zufar argues flow is irrelevant.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by distinguishing between kharuj (exit with flow) and bad (mere appearance). The body is filled with impurities under the skin; only when impurity moves from its original place (flow) is it considered exit.
The response rejects Zufar's premise that the blood has exited.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance (al-asahh)
Later Hanafi authorities (al-Kafi, al-Nihayah, etc.) hold that in cases where exit is necessary (e.g., biting, picking), nullification occurs because the blood's exit is unavoidable. This is considered the most correct view (al-asahh).
This modifies the classic Zahir al-Riwayah position that non-flowing blood never nullifies.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference affects whether one must repeat wudu' after seeing non-flowing blood from a bite, toothpick, etc. According to the classic view, wudu' remains valid; according to Zufar and the later asahh view, it is nullified.
The mufta bihi today often follows the asahh view when exit is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا إذا عَضَ شيئاً ، أو خلل أسنانه، أو أدخلَ أُصْبَعَهُ في أنفه فرأى أثر الدم، أو استنثر فخرج من أنفِهِ الدَّمُ عَلَقاً مثل العدس، لا ينقض الوضوء عندنا، خلافاً لزُفَرَه، ووجهه : إن خروج النجاسةِ مؤثر في زوال الطهارة كالسبيلين. إخراج، والناقض إنّما هو الخروج، وهذا عند بعض المشايخ منهم «الهداية». والأصح على ما اختاره في «الكافي»، و«النهاية»، و«غاية البيان»، و«فتح القدير»، و«البزازية»، وغيرها : هو النقض في هذه الصورة؛ لكون الخروج لازماً للإخراج، والناقض إنّما هو الخروج مطلقاً لا الخروج بنفسه فحسب. [١] قوله : وكذا؛ أي لا ينقض الوضوء عندنا بناءً على اشتراط السيلان إذا عض: أي أخذ شيئاً بأسنانه، يقال : عضضتُ اللقمة : إذا أمسكتها بالأسنان. أو خلل: أي أدخل الخلالَ في أسنانِهِ فرأى أثر الدم في الخلال أو ذلك الشيء المعضوض. أو أدخل إصبعه بكسر الهمزة في أنفه، فرأى أثر الدم في الإصبع وغيره. أو استنثر: أي أخرج ما في أنفه مخاطاً كان أو غيره، فخرج من أنفه عَلَقاً - بفتحتين يقال للدم الغليظ المنجمد مثل العَدَس بفتحتين : حب معروف. وكذا إذا بزق فرأى في بزاقه شيئا قليلا من أثر الدم. وكذا إذا غُرزت إبرة أو شوكة في عضو فأُخْرِجَ منه، فظهر منه الدم، ولم يسل، ففي هذه الصور وأمثالها لا ينتقض الوضوء عندنا، خلافاً لزفر. (٢) قوله : إن خروج النجاسة ... الخ؛ يحتمل تقرير كلامه على وجهين : الأول : إن خروج النجاسةِ علةٌ لزوال الطهارة، وكلما وجدت العلة وجد المعلول، فكلما وُجد خروجُ النَّجَسِ وُجِدَ زوالُ الطهارة، لكن خروج النجس موجود فيما نحن فيه، فلا بد من الحكم بالانتقاض. وحينئذ تقرير الجواب : أنا لا نُسلّم صحة وضع المقدم، فإنّ القليل الغير السائل ليس بخارج، بل هو باد؛ أي ظاهر، وذلك لأنّ البدن مملوء من النجاسات باطناً، فتحت كل جلد نجاسة، فإذا زالت القشرة ظهرت تلك النجاسة الكائنة، وإنما يحكمُ بالخروج إذا تعدى عن موضعه الأصلي، وهو لا يتحقق إلا بالسيلان، فالخروج والسيلان متلازمان. الثاني : إن القليل نجس خارج، وكلّ نجس خارج ناقض، فينتج أن القليل أيضاً ناقض، وحينئذ فتقرير الجواب : إنا أيضاً لا نُسلّم الصغرى؛ لأنّ القليل ليس بخارج. وهاهنا وجوه أخر أيضاً بيناها في «السعاية». [١] قوله : والنجاسة؛ هذه مقدّمة، لا احتياج لها في الجواب، فإنه قد تم على قوله: «لا خارج»، وإنما أوردها تأكيداً وتأييداً للجواب، ويمكن أن يكون جواباً آخر مستقلاً عن استدلال زفر على سبيل المعارضة، بأن يقال : إن القليل نجاسة مستقرة في معدنها؛ أي موضعها، وكلّ ما هو كذلك فهو غير ناقض. [٢] قوله : هذا الدليل؛ إشارة إلى قوله : «والنجاسة المستقرة في معدنها لا تنقض»، أو إلى قوله : «لكن القليل باد» أو إلى كليهما. وحاصله : أنّ هذا الذي ذكروه جواباً عن قول زفر دليل غير تام؛ لعدم شموله جميع صور غير السائل، فإنّه إذا غرزت الإبرة - وهو الذي يخاط به - أو رأسه – أي أدخلت وركزت في موضع - فارتفع الدم من موضعه، وظهر على رأس الموضع المغروز
And likewise, if one bites something, or picks his teeth, or inserts his finger into his nose and sees a trace of blood, or sniffs and blood comes out of his nose as a clot like a lentil, it does not nullify wudu' according to us, contrary to Zufar. His reasoning: indeed the exit of impurity is effective in removing purity, like the two passages. The nullifier is only the exit, and this is according to some teachers among them, al-Hidayah. The most correct, based on what was chosen in al-Kafi, al-Nihayah, Ghayat al-Bayan, Fath al-Qadir, al-Bazzaziyyah, and others, is that nullification occurs in this case because exit is necessary for extraction, and the nullifier is only exit absolutely, not exit by itself alone. [1] His saying: 'And likewise' meaning it does not nullify wudu' according to us based on the condition of flow. 'Bites' meaning he took something with his teeth. 'Or picks' meaning he inserted a toothpick into his teeth and saw a trace of blood on the toothpick or the bitten thing. 'Or inserts his finger' with kasra on the hamza into his nose, and sees a trace of blood on the finger or elsewhere. 'Or sniffs' meaning he expels what is in his nose, mucus or otherwise, and blood comes out as a clot - with two fatha, meaning thick clotted blood like lentils - the well-known grain. And likewise if he spits and sees in his saliva a small amount of trace of blood. And likewise if a needle or thorn is pricked into a limb and then extracted, and blood appears from it but does not flow, then in these and similar cases wudu' is not nullified according to us, contrary to Zufar. (2) His saying: 'Indeed the exit of impurity...' is open to two interpretations: First: That the exit of impurity is the cause for removal of purity, and whenever the cause exists the effect exists, so whenever the exit of impurity is found, the removal of purity is found, but the exit of impurity exists in our case, so nullification must be ruled. At that point the response: We do not accept the validity of the premise, because the small amount that does not flow is not exiting, rather it is apparent only, because the body is filled with impurities internally, under every skin is impurity, so when the skin is removed that impurity appears, and exit is only judged when it moves from its original place, which is not realized except with flow, so exit and flow are inseparable. Second: That the small amount is impure and exiting, and every exiting impure thing nullifies, so it results that the small amount also nullifies. At that point the response: We also do not accept the minor premise because the small amount is not exiting. And there are other aspects we have explained in al-Si'ayah. [1] His saying: 'And the impurity' is a premise not needed in the response; it is complete with his saying 'not exiting', and he only brought it as emphasis and support. It could be an independent response to Zufar's argument via counterargument, saying: The small amount is an impurity settled in its mine, i.e., its place, and everything that is like that does not nullify. [2] His saying: 'This proof' refers to his saying 'the impurity settled in its place does not nullify' or to his saying 'but the small amount is apparent' or both. The summary is that what they mentioned as a response to Zufar's statement is an incomplete proof because it does not encompass all cases of non-flowing, such as when a needle is pricked - which is sewn with - or his head - meaning it was inserted and embedded in a spot - and the blood rises from its place and appears on the head of the pricked spot
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether the appearance of blood without flowing nullifies wudu'. The author states the Hanafi position: non-flowing blood (e.g., from a bite, toothpick, nose, spit, or needle prick) does not nullify wudu', contrary to the view of Zufar. The key condition is 'sayalan' (flow). Zufar analogizes the exit of impurity to the two excretory passages, arguing that any exit of impurity nullifies purity. The Hanafi response distinguishes between 'kharuj' (exit) and 'bad' (apparent). The body internally contains impurities; mere appearance without movement from its original place is not considered exit. True exit requires flowing (sailan). The text then explores two formulations of Zufar's argument and the Hanafi rebuttals. The relied-upon position (as in al-Kafi, al-Nihayah, etc.) is that nullification occurs only if the blood exits with flow, but some earlier authorities (like the author of al-Hidayah) held non-nullification in these cases. The final commentary criticizes the Hanafi response for not covering all cases, e.g., when blood rises to the surface of a needle prick but does not flow. The passage overall reinforces that the Hanafi school conditions flowing for nullification by blood, distinguishing it from the two excretory passages where any exit nullifies.

Key Terms
الخروج (al-kharuj): The exit of impurity from the body; in this context, only considered to have occurred if the impurity flows (sailan) from its original place.
السيلان (al-sayalan): Flowing or running of blood or other impurity from a wound; a condition for nullification of wudu' according to the Hanafi school.
الباد (al-bad): Something that appears on the surface but does not exit from its original place; not considered true exit that nullifies wudu'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position described in the text, what is the key condition for blood to nullify wudu'?
A) The blood must be from a wound
B) The blood must flow (sailan)
C) The blood must be more than a dirham
D) The blood must be from the two excretory passages

Key Takeaway
Blood nullifies wudu' only when it flows from its place; mere appearance without flow does not break wudu'.

The Ruling on Non-Flowing Blood and Its Effect on Wudu
Debate on whether a small amount of blood that does not flow nullifies wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a small amount of non-flowing blood does not nullify wudu, and distinguish between the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the definition of 'flow'.

Core Idea
Non-flowing blood is apparent but not exiting, so it does not nullify wudu.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi legal debate: does a small amount of blood that appears but does not flow break wudu? The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, answers no, but there is an intra-school difference between Zufar (who says yes) and the majority. We will explore the key concepts of 'exit' (khuruj), 'flow' (sayalan), and the nature of impurity, as well as the practical consequence for daily worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question and the act in question
Determine what the dispute is about: a small amount of blood that appears on the skin but does not flow (ghayr sa'il).
Focus on the definition of 'small amount' (qalil) in this context.
2
State the mainstream Hanafi position and Zufar's opposing view
The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa) says non-flowing blood does not nullify wudu; Zufar says it does.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof: bad vs. kharij
Non-flowing blood is considered 'apparent' (bad) not 'exiting' (kharij). True exit requires movement from its place, which only occurs with flow.
The body is filled with internal impurities; mere appearance upon skin removal is not exit.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on the definition of flow
Imam Muhammad defines flow as rising and descending (ʿuluww wa inhidar); Abu Yusuf defines it as moving away from the exit (zualan ʿan al-makhraj). This affects the needle prick case.
According to Muhammad, needle prick blood does not flow; according to Abu Yusuf, it does if it moves from the site.
5
Consider the alternative argument: impurity status of the blood
Only shed blood (dam masfuh) is impure. Non-flowing blood is not shed, so even if exit is conceded, there is no impurity to nullify.
This is a second independent answer to Zufar.
6
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The majority position (mufta bihi) is that non-flowing blood does not nullify; one may pray without repeating wudu. Zufar's view would require renewal.
This affects daily practice, e.g., cuts or pricks during prayer time.

ونحن نقول : نعم ؛ لكن القليل بادٍ لا خارج، والنجاسة المستقرة في موضعها لا تنقض. قلت : هذا الدليل غير تام ؛ لأنه لا يشمل ما إذا غُرِزَتْ إبرةٌ فارتقى الدَّمُ على رأس الجرح لكن لم يسل عن رأس الجرح فإنَّ الخروج هناك محسوس الأول : إن خروج النجاسةِ علةٌ لزوال الطهارة، وكلما وجدت العلة وجد المعلول، فكلما وُجد خروجُ النَّجَسِ وُجِدَ زوالُ الطهارة، لكن خروج النجس موجود فيما نحن فيه ، فلا بد من الحكم بالانتقاض. وحينئذ تقرير الجواب : أنا لا نُسلّم صحة وضع المقدم، فإنّ القليل الغير السائل ليس بخارج ، بل هو باد؛ أي ظاهر، وذلك لأنّ البدن مملوء من النجاسات باطناً ، فتحت كل جلد نجاسة، فإذا زالت القشرة ظهرت تلك النجاسة الكائنة ، وإنما يحكمُ بالخروج إذا تعدى عن موضعه الأصلي، وهو لا يتحقق إلا بالسيلان، فالخروج والسيلان متلازمان. الثاني : إن القليل نجس خارج ، وكلّ نجس خارج ناقض، فينتج أن القليل أيضاً ناقض، وحينئذ فتقرير الجواب : إنا أيضاً لا نُسلّم الصغرى ؛ لأنّ القليل ليس بخارج، وهاهنا وجوه أخر أيضاً بيناها في «السعاية). [١]قوله : والنجاسة؛ هذه مقدّمة، لا احتياج لها في الجواب، فإنه قد تم على قوله: «لا خارج»، وإنما أوردها تأكيداً وتأييداً للجواب، ويمكن أن يكون جواباً آخر مستقلاً عن استدلال زفر على سبيل المعارضة، بأن يقال : إن القليل نجاسة مستقرة في معدنها ؛ أي موضعها ، وكلّ ما هو كذلك فهو غير ناقض. [٢] قوله : هذا الدليل ؛ إشارة إلى قوله : «والنجاسة المستقرة في معدنها لا تنقض»، أو إلى قوله : «لكن القليل باد» أو إلى كليهما. وحاصله : أنّ هذا الذي ذكروه جواباً عن قول زفر دليل غير تام ؛ لعدم شموله جميع صور غير السائل، فإنّه إذا غرزت الإبرة - وهو الذي يخـاط بـه - أو رأسه – أي أدخلت وركزت في موضع - فارتفع الدم من موضعه، وظهر على رأس الموضع المغروزِ ومع ذلك لا ينقض عندنا ، وقد خطر ببالي وجه حسن " : وهو أنه لم يتحقق خروج النجاسة ؛ لأنَّ هذا الدَّمَ غيرُ نجس فيه، ولم يسل إلى ما وراءه لا يصدق عليه أنه مستقر في موضعه، ولا أنه باد ؛ لكون الخروج هاهنا من موضعه والانتقال منه محسوساً ، فإنّه لو لم يخرج منه لم ير على رأس الجرح، مع أنه أيضاً غير ناقض عندنا، فيكون التقريب وهو سوق الدليل على وجه يثبت المدعى غير تام. وفيه بحث ؛ فإن الخروج عبارة عن التجاوز من موضع باطن إلى ظاهر ؛ ولذا قالوا : إن الخروج والسيلان متلازمان فلا يصح قوله ؛ فإن الخروج هناك محسوس»، وأيضاً المحسوس إنما هو الدم المرتقي لا نفس خروجه. والجواب عنه : أنه تسامح في العبارة، وجعل نفس الخروج محسوساً ؛ لكونه متيقناً بإعانة الحواس الظاهرة، والخروج مطلقاً عبارة عن تجاوز من موضعه إلى موضع آخر تجاوزاً ما سواء كان إلى الظاهر أو لا، وهو موجود هاهنا بلا ريب. وأما الذي حكموا بتلازمه مع السيلان فإنّما هو الخروج المعتبر في انتقاض الوضوء عند الفقهاء، والمقصود هاهنا مجرَّدُ منع قول المستدل : «إنَّ القليل بادٍ لا خارج»، مع قطع النظر عن كونه معتبراً أو لا. [١] قوله : عندنا ؛ هذا إنّما هو عند محمد له ؛ لأنه يُفسر السيلان بالعلو والانحدار من موضعه، وهو مفقود هاهنا، وعند أبي يوسف الله ينقض ؛ لأنه يفسر السيلان بالزوال عن مخرجه سواء انحدر أم لا. [٢]قوله : وجه حسن ؛ لهذا الكلام محملان : الأول : إنّه جواب عن الإيرادِ المصدر بقوله : «قلت»، وحينئذ فحاصله أنه لا يضر عدم جريان الدليل السابق في صورة غرز الإبرة، فإنّ انتقاض الوضوء فيها إنما هو لعدم تحقق خروج النجاسة، لا لعدم تحقق الخروج. الثاني : إنه جواب آخر مستقل عن قول زفر ، وحينئذ فحاصله : أنا سلمنا أنّ خروج النجاسة مؤثر، لكنَّ الدم الغير السائل سواء كان خارجاً – كما في صورة غرز الإبرة - أو مستقراً في معدنِهِ ليس بنجس ؛ لأنّ النجس هو الدم المسفوح لا غير، فلا يتحقق في جميع صور القليل خروج النجاسة، وإن تحقق الخروج في بعضها.
And we say: Yes; but the small amount is apparent (bad) not exiting, and the impurity settled in its place does not nullify. I said: This proof is incomplete because it does not cover the case where a needle is pricked and blood rises to the top of the wound but does not flow from the top of the wound, for the exit there is perceptible. First: The exit of impurity is the cause (illa) for the removal of purity, and whenever the cause is found the effect is found, so whenever the exit of impurity is found, the removal of purity is found; but the exit of impurity is present in our case, so it is necessary to rule nullification. Then the presentation of the answer: We do not accept the validity of the premise, for the small amount that does not flow is not exiting, but rather it is apparent (bad), i.e., visible; because the body is filled with impurities internally, so beneath every piece of skin there is impurity; when the outer layer is removed, that existing impurity appears; and exit is only judged when it moves from its original place, and that is only realized by flowing (sayalan), so exit and flowing are inseparable. Second: The small amount is impure and exiting, and every impure and exiting thing nullifies; thus it follows that the small amount also nullifies. Then the presentation of the answer: We also do not accept the minor premise, because the small amount is not exiting; and there are also other aspects we have explained in "al-Si'ayah." [1] His saying: "and the impurity" — this is a premise that is not needed in the answer, for the answer is already complete with his saying "not exiting"; it is only mentioned for emphasis and support, and it could be another independent answer to Zufar's inference by way of objection: that the small amount is impurity settled in its place (madin), and everything like that is not nullifying. [2] His saying: "this proof" — referring to his saying "the impurity settled in its place does not nullify" or to his saying "but the small amount is apparent" or to both. The gist is that this which they mentioned as an answer to Zufar's statement is an incomplete proof because it does not cover all forms of non-flowing (ghayr sa'il); for if a needle is pricked — that which is used for stitching — or its head — i.e., inserted and fixed in a place — and blood rises from its place and appears at the top of the pricked spot, yet it does not nullify according to us; and a good idea occurred to me: it is that the exit of impurity is not realized because this blood is not impure in it, and it did not flow beyond it, it cannot be described as settled in its place, nor as apparent; because the exit here from its place and the transition from it is perceptible; for if it did not exit from it, it would not be seen at the top of the wound, even though it is also non-nullifying according to us; so the approximation — i.e., presenting the evidence in a way that proves the claim — is incomplete. There is discussion in it: for exit (khuruj) is an expression of moving from an internal place to an external one; that is why they said: exit and flow are inseparable; so his statement "the exit there is perceptible" is not valid; also, what is perceptible is only the blood that has risen, not the exit itself. The answer to it: it is a license in expression, and he made the exit itself perceptible because it is certain with the aid of external senses; and exit in absolute terms is an expression of moving from its place to another place in some way, whether to the external or not, and it is present here without doubt. As for their ruling that it is inseparable from flow, that is the exit considered in the nullification of wudu according to the jurists; and the intent here is merely to refute the proponent's statement "the small amount is apparent not exiting", regardless of whether it is considered or not. [1] His saying: "according to us" — this is only according to Muhammad, because he interprets flow (sayalan) as rising and descending from its place, which is absent here; while according to Abu Yusuf it nullifies because he interprets flow as moving away from its exit whether it descends or not. [2] His saying: "a good idea" — this statement has two interpretations: First: it is an answer to the objection originating from his saying "I said", and then its gist is that the non-applicability of the previous proof in the case of the needle prick is harmless, because the nullification of wudu in that case is due to the non-realization of the exit of impurity, not due to the non-realization of exit. Second: it is another independent answer to Zufar's statement, and then its gist: we accept that the exit of impurity is effective, but the blood that does not flow, whether it is exiting — as in the case of the needle prick — or settled in its place, is not impure; because the impure (najis) is only shed blood (dam masfuh), nothing else; so in all cases of the small amount, the exit of impurity is not realized, even if exit is realized in some of them.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether a small amount of blood (ghayr sa'il) that appears but does not flow actually breaks wudu. The author first presents the standard Hanafi position: a small amount that does not flow is merely 'apparent' (bad) not 'exiting' (kharij), and impurity that remains in its place does not nullify wudu. Then an objection is raised: this reasoning fails for a needle prick where blood visibly rises to the surface but does not flow; here exit is perceptible, yet the Hanafis still say it does not nullify. The objector also gives two syllogisms: (1) exit of impurity is the cause (illa) for nullification, and in this case exit is present, so nullification must occur; (2) the small amount is impure and exiting, and everything impure and exiting nullifies. The answer is to deny the minor premise: the small amount that does not flow is not considered 'exiting' because true exit requires movement (tajaawuz) from its original place, and that only occurs with flow (sailan). Thus exit and flow are inseparable. For the needle prick case, the commentator notes that according to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, flow (sayalan) requires rising and descending (al-uluww wa al-inhidar), which is absent, so it does not nullify; but according to Abu Yusuf, flow means simply moving away from the exit (zualan 'an makhrajih), so such blood would nullify. The commentator then presents a 'good idea' (wajh hasan): either it is an answer to the objection that the previous proof's non-applicability is not problematic because the nullification is due to lack of impurity (since only shed blood is impure), or it is an independent answer that the blood in such cases is not impure at all, because only shed blood (dam masfuh) is najis, and non-flowing blood is not shed. Thus even if exit occurs, the najasah (impurity) is not present. This is an important qualification: the Hanafi position relies not only on the definition of exit but also on the nature of the blood—only flowing blood is considered najis for the purpose of nullification.

Key Terms
القليل (al-qalil): A small amount of blood (or other impurity) that does not flow (ghayr sa'il).
باد (بدا) (bad (bada)): Apparent; something that becomes visible on the surface but is not considered to have exited from its original place.
الخروج (al-khuruj): Exit; the legal concept that an impurity has moved from its internal location to the external surface. In Hanafi fiqh, true exit only occurs with flow (sailan).
السيلان (al-sayalan): Flowing; movement of blood from its site, either rising and descending (according to Muhammad) or simply moving away from the exit (according to Abu Yusuf).
النجاسة المستقرة (al-najasah al-mustaqirrah): Settled impurity; impurity that remains in its original place and has not moved.
الدم المسفوح (al-dam al-masfuh): Shed blood; flowing blood that is considered najis according to the Quran (al-An'am 6:145).
المعدن (al-madin): The place or origin of the impurity; the site where impurity resides.
المخرج (al-makhraj): The exit point; the place from which impurity emerges.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is required for a small amount of blood to nullify wudu?
A) The blood must be red in color.
B) The blood must flow (sail) from its place.
C) The blood must be more than a dirham in size.
D) The blood must exit from a wound.

Key Takeaway
Non-flowing blood does not nullify wudu because it is not considered exiting according to the Hanafi definition, and only shed blood (dam masfuh) is impure for nullification.

Vomiting and Its Effect on Wudu: Conditions and Rulings
Explaining types of vomit and conditions for wudu nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which vomiting nullifies wudu in the Hanafi school and apply the rule of predominance to mixed vomit cases.

Core Idea
Vomiting nullifies wudu only if the expelled substance is impure and fills the mouth.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains in detail the rulings on vomiting (qay') as a nullifier of wudu. The author distinguishes between different types of vomit—blood, food, water, bile, phlegm—and specifies the conditions that trigger nullification. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the ruling in daily worship.
