والقيء دماً رقيقاً إن ساوى البزاق
(والقيء) عطف على قوله : ما خرج ، فأراد أن يفصل أنواعَهُ لأنَّ الحكم مُخْتَلِفٌ فيها ، فقال : (دماً رقيقاً إن ساوى (البزاق) حتى إن كان البزاقُ أكثر لا ينقض"، ولما ذكر حكم المساواة ، عُلِمَ حكمُ الغلبة بالطريق الأولى، فقالوا : " إذا اصفر البزاق من الدم فلا يجب الوضوء، وإن احمر يجب.
[1] قوله : فأراد : الفاء : إما تعليلية : أي إنّما عطفه وأفرده بالذكر مع دخوله في قوله : «أو من غيره ؛ لأنه أراد أن يفصل أنواعه، وإما تفريعية : أي لما عطفه على «ما خرج»، وكان فيه تفصيل لا بُدَّ من ذكره فأراد أن يفصل أقسامه.
[٢]قوله : مختلف فيها ؛ بصيغة اسم الفاعل أو اسم المفعول، والمراد باختلاف الحكم في الأنواع كون بعضها ينقض وبعضها لا ينقض، واشتراط ملأ الفم في بعضها دون بعضها ، أو المراد اختلاف الأئمة فيها، فمنهم من يجعلُ بعضها ناقضاً ومنهم من لا يجعله.
[٣]قوله : لا ينقض ؛ ظاهره أنه لا فرق بين الدم الصاعد من الجوف، وبين الخارج من الفم، وظاهرُ كلام الزَّيْلَعِي في شرح الكنز » ) : إن الصاعد المختلط بالبزاق ينقض قليله وكثيره على المختار.
ووجهه : أنه يخرج بقوة نفسه، فإنّه لا يختلط بالبزاق إلا بعد صعودِهِ من الجوف، بخلاف المغلوب الخارج من الفم، فإنّه لا يخرجُ بقوَّةٍ نفسه، بل بقوة البزاق، فلا يكون ناقضاً ما لم يسل البزاق، أو يغلب عليه.
وإن كان الدم نازلاً من الرأس إلى الأنف نقض قليلاً كان أو كثيراً ؛ لوصوله إلى موضع يلحقه حكم التطهير.
[٤]قوله : فقالوا ؛ هذه علامة تعرف به مغلوبية الدم وغلبته.
(۱) تبیین الحقائق (۱ : ۸).

ثم عطف على قوله : دَمَاً ، قوله : (أو مرة أو طعاماً ، أو ماء [١] قوله : أو طعاماً (۱) ؛ سواء كان قاءه بعد زمان من الأكل أو في ساعته، وعن الحسن عن أبي حنيفة : إنه لا ينقض إن قاءَ من ساعته ما لم يتغير، وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبي إذا شرب اللبن وقاءً من ساعته ، والصحيح هو ظاهر الرواية : إنه نجس ناقض ؛ لتداخل النجاسة فيه، بخلاف قيء البلغم، وبخلاف قيء الدودة أو الحية ؛ فإنّه لا ينقض ، وإن كان ملأ الفم ؛ لأنه في نفسه طاهر، وما يتبعه من النجاسة قليل. كذا في «الغُنية شرح المنية»(٢).
وهذا كله إذا خرج الطعام بعد الوصول إلى الجوف، فإن كان بعد في المريء لا ينقض، كما في «القُنْية»، ولو قاء شيئين مختلفين دماً وطعاماً، أو دماً وبلغماً فالعبرة للغالب، ولو استويا يعتبر كل على حدة. كذا في «المجتبى شرح مختصر القدوري»(۳). [٢] قوله : أو ماء ; فلو شرب الماء وخرج بعد الوصول إلى الجوف نقض، وإن كان صافياً. كذا في «الظهيرية».
(۱) ومن الأدلة على نقض القيء :
١ . عن عائشة رضي الله عنها ، قال : «من أصابه قيء أو رعاف أو قلس أو مذي فلينصرف فليتوضأ ثم ليبن على صلاته وهو في ذلك لا يتكلم» في سنن ابن ماجة» (۱ : ٣٨٥)، قال التهانوي في «إعلاء السنن» (۱ : ۱۱۳) : والصحيح أنه مرسل صحيح الإسناد». وينظر : «الدراية» (۱ : ۳۱)، ونصب الراية (۱) : (۳۸) ، وتلخيص الحبير» (١ : ٢٧٤)، وغيرها. ٢ . عن أبي الدرداء : «إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قـاء فأفطر فتوضأ فلقيت ثوبان في مسجد دمشق فذكرت ذلك له فقال : صدق أنا صببت له الدفع في سنن الترمذي»(١: ١٤٣)، وقال: «قد جود حسين المعلم هذا الحديث وحديث حسين أصح شيء في هذا الباب وروى معمره الحديث». هذا . عن ابن عمر قال : إذا رعف الرجل في الصلاة، أو ذرعه القيء، أو وجـد مـذياً فإنـه ينصرف ويتوضأ ثم يرجع فيتم ما بقي على ما مضى ما لم يتكلم» في «مصنف عبد الرزاق»(۲) : ٣٣٩)، وإسناده صحيح كما في إعلاء السنن»(١ : ١١٤)، وغيره.
(۲) «غنية المستملي شرح منية المصلي»(ص۱۲۳).
(۳) «المجتبی شرح القدوري»(ق۸/أ).
And vomiting thin blood if it equals spittle. (And vomiting) is conjoined to his statement: 'what comes out', and he intended to detail its types because the ruling differs regarding them, so he said: 'thin blood if it equals spittle' until if the spittle is more, it does not nullify. And when he mentioned the ruling of equality, the ruling of predominance is understood by primary reasoning, so they said: 'If the spittle becomes yellow from blood, wudu is not obligatory, and if it becomes red, it is obligatory.' [1] His statement: 'he intended': the fa is either causal (i.e., he only conjoined it and mentioned it separately despite its inclusion in his statement 'or from other than it' because he intended to detail its types), or sequential (i.e., when he conjoined it to 'what came out', and there was a necessary detail, he intended to detail its divisions). [2] His statement: 'differing regarding them' in the form of active or passive participle, and the intended meaning of the difference of ruling in the types is that some of them nullify and some do not, and the condition of filling the mouth for some without others, or the intended meaning is the difference of the imams regarding them, some consider some nullifying and some do not. [3] His statement: 'does not nullify' – its apparent meaning is that there is no difference between blood rising from the stomach and that exiting from the mouth, and the apparent meaning of al-Zayla'i's statement in Sharh al-Kanz is that rising blood mixed with spittle nullifies whether little or much according to the chosen view. Its reasoning: it exits by its own force, as it does not mix with spittle except after rising from the stomach, unlike the dominated exiting from the mouth, for it does not exit by its own force but by the force of spittle, so it is not nullifying unless the spittle flows or overcomes it. And if the blood descends from the head to the nose, it nullifies whether little or much, because it reaches a place subject to the ruling of purification. [4] His statement: 'so they said' – this is a sign by which the domination of blood and its predominance are known. (1) Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (1:8). Then he conjoined to his statement: 'blood', his statement: 'or bile or food or water' [1] His statement: 'or food' (1); whether he vomited it after some time from eating or immediately. From al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: it does not nullify if he vomits immediately unless it changes; and accordingly is the difference regarding a child who drinks milk and vomits immediately. The correct view is the apparent narration: it is impure and nullifying, because impurity penetrates it, unlike vomiting phlegm, and unlike vomiting a worm or snake; for it does not nullify, even if it fills the mouth, because it is in itself pure, and what follows it from impurity is little. Thus in al-Ghunya Sharh al-Munya (2). All this is if the food exits after reaching the stomach; if it is still in the esophagus, it does not nullify, as in al-Qunyah. If he vomits two different things, blood and food, or blood and phlegm, then consideration is given to the predominant; if they are equal, each is considered separately. Thus in al-Mujtaba Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri (3). [2] His statement: 'or water'; if he drinks water and it exits after reaching the stomach, it nullifies, even if it is clear. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah. (1) Among the proofs for the nullification by vomiting: 1. From A'ishah, may Allah be pleased with her, who said: 'Whoever is afflicted with vomiting, nosebleed, regurgitation, or madhy, let him leave and perform wudu, then continue his prayer as long as he did not speak.' In Sunan Ibn Majah (1:385), al-Tahanawi said in I'la al-Sunan (1:113): 'The correct view is that it is mursal but with a sound chain.' And see: al-Dirayah (1:31), Nasb al-Rayah (1:38), Talkhis al-Habir (1:274), etc. 2. From Abu al-Darda': 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, vomited and broke his fast, then performed wudu. I met Thawban in the mosque of Damascus and mentioned that to him, and he said: 'He told the truth; I poured water for his ablution.' In Sunan al-Tirmidhi (1:143), and he said: 'Husayn al-Mu'allim authenticated this hadith, and the hadith of Husayn is the most authentic thing on this topic, and Ma'mar also narrated it.' This is from Ibn 'Umar who said: 'If a man has a nosebleed during prayer, or is overcome by vomiting, or finds madhy, then he leaves and performs wudu, then returns and completes what remains of his prayer as long as he did not speak.' In Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq (2:339), and its chain is sound as in I'la al-Sunan (1:114), etc. (2) Ghunyat al-Mustamli Sharh Munyat al-Musalli (p.123). (3) al-Mujtaba Sharh al-Quduri (folio 8a).
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rulings of vomiting (qay') as a nullifier of wudu. The author explains that the ruling differs depending on the substance vomited and its condition. The base text (matn) states: 'And vomiting thin blood if it equals spittle.' The commentator clarifies that this is conjoined to the earlier statement about what exits the body, and the reason for separate mention is to detail the types because the ruling varies. For thin blood, if the amount of blood equals the spittle (i.e., the mixture is half and half), then it nullifies wudu. If the spittle is more, it does not nullify. This is deduced by a fortiori reasoning: if equality nullifies, then predominance of blood also nullifies. The scholars said: if the spittle turns yellow from blood, wudu is not required; if it turns red, it is required.

The commentary then discusses the word 'he intended' and the grammatical analysis of the connecting particle 'fa'. It explains the phrase 'differing regarding them'—meaning either that the types differ in ruling (some nullify, some do not) or that the imams differ on which types nullify. 

On the phrase 'does not nullify', the commentator notes that it implies no difference between blood rising from the stomach and blood coming from the mouth. However, al-Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz states that blood rising from the stomach and mixing with spittle nullifies whether little or much, according to the chosen view, because it exits by its own force. In contrast, blood that is dominated by spittle (i.e., the spittle is more) does not nullify unless the spittle flows or overcomes. If blood descends from the head to the nose, it nullifies regardless of quantity because it reaches a place subject to purification (i.e., it is considered impurity). The phrase 'so they said' is a sign to identify whether blood is predominant or dominated.

Next, the text conjoins the rulings for bile (marrah), food, and water. For food vomit, it makes no difference whether it is vomited immediately after eating or after some time. There is a narration from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa that if one vomits immediately after eating without change, it does not nullify. The same applies to a child who drinks milk and vomits immediately. However, the correct view (sahih) is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah): the food is impure and nullifies because impurity mixes with it. This is different from vomiting phlegm (balgham), a worm, or a snake—these do not nullify even if they fill the mouth because they are pure in themselves, and any attached impurity is minimal. All these rulings apply only if the food reaches the stomach; if it is still in the esophagus (mari'), it does not nullify. If one vomits two different substances (e.g., blood and food, or blood and phlegm), consideration is given to the predominant; if equal, each is judged separately. For water, if one drinks water and it exits after reaching the stomach, it nullifies even if clear, as in al-Zahiriyyah.

The passage concludes with hadith proofs for the nullification of wudu by vomiting: from A'ishah (though it is mursal but with a sound chain), from Abu al-Darda' (the Prophet vomited and performed wudu), and from Ibn 'Umar (the one overcome by vomiting should leave and perform wudu, then continue prayer). These hadith support the ruling that vomiting nullifies wudu.

Key Terms
قيء (qay'): Vomiting; the expulsion of stomach contents through the mouth.
بزاق (buzāq): Spittle or saliva mixed with blood from vomiting.
ملأ الفم (mala' al-fam): Filling the mouth; a condition for nullification of wudu by vomiting.
نقض (naqḍ): Nullification of wudu; breaking the state of ritual purity.
غالب (ghālib): Predominant; when two substances are vomited, the ruling follows the one that is more in quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person vomits thin blood that is equal in amount to spittle, what is the ruling on his wudu?
A) Wudu is not nullified because spittle is pure.
B) Wudu is nullified because the blood equals the spittle.
C) Wudu is nullified only if the blood is thick.
D) Wudu is nullified only if the blood dominates the spittle.

Key Takeaway
Vomiting nullifies wudu only if the expelled substance is impure and fills the mouth; the predominance rule determines the effect of mixed vomit.

Vomiting and Nullification of Wudu in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling with proof on vomiting nullifying wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules on which types of vomit nullify wudu, apply the mouthful threshold, and identify the intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, only a mouthful of impure substances from the stomach nullifies wudu; phlegm and pure substances do not.

Vomiting is not always a nullifier of wudu according to the Hanafi school. The ruling depends on the type of substance, quantity, and origin. This lesson unpacks the detailed rules from classical Hanafi sources and the supporting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substances that nullify wudu
List the main categories: food, water, bile, thin blood, clotted blood. Note the exception for phlegm, worms, and snakes.
Each has a different condition.
2
Understand the mouthful threshold (mil’ al-fam)
A mouthful is defined as that which cannot be held back without effort. This threshold applies to most substances except thin blood from the stomach.
The majority of later scholars adopt this definition.
3
Differentiate between thin blood and clotted blood
Thin (liquid) blood nullifies even if little, whether from stomach or not (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Clotted blood is treated as burnt black bile and requires a mouthful.
Intra-Hanafi difference: Muhammad requires a mouthful for thin blood if not from stomach.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on immediate vomiting
According to a narration from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, immediate vomiting of unchanged food does not nullify, but the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that it does.
The Zahir al-Riwayah position is mufta bihi.
5
Examine the dalil (evidence) and cross-madhhab dispute
The Hanafis cite hadith from Aisha, Abu Darda, and Ibn Umar. The Shafi'is disagree, holding that vomiting does not nullify. The practical consequence (thamarah) is clear: Hanafis require wudu after a mouthful; Shafi'is do not.
The Hanafi response notes that the hadith are sound and supported by qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة الوضوء

ثم عطف على قوله : دَمَاً، قوله : (أو مرة أو طعاماً، أو ماء). قوله : أو طعاماً؛ سواء كان قاءه بعد زمان من الأكل أو في ساعته، وعن الحسن عن أبي حنيفة : إنه لا ينقض إن قاءَ من ساعته ما لم يتغير، وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبي إذا شرب اللبن وقاءً من ساعته، والصحيح هو ظاهر الرواية : إنه نجس ناقض؛ لتداخل النجاسة فيه، بخلاف قيء البلغم، وبخلاف قيء الدودة أو الحية؛ فإنّه لا ينقض، وإن كان ملأ الفم؛ لأنه في نفسه طاهر، وما يتبعه من النجاسة قليل. كذا في «الغُنية شرح المنية». وهذا كله إذا خرج الطعام بعد الوصول إلى الجوف، فإن كان بعد في المريء لا ينقض، كما في «القُنْية»، ولو قاء شيئين مختلفين دماً وطعاماً، أو دماً وبلغماً فالعبرة للغالب، ولو استويا يعتبر كل على حدة. كذا في «المجتبى شرح مختصر القدوري». قوله : أو ماء؛ فلو شرب الماء وخرج بعد الوصول إلى الجوف نقض، وإن كان صافياً. كذا في «الظهيرية».

ومن الأدلة على نقض القيء:
1. عن عائشة رضي الله عنها، قالت: «من أصابه قيء أو رعاف أو قلس أو مذي فلينصرف فليتوضأ ثم ليبن على صلاته وهو في ذلك لا يتكلم» في سنن ابن ماجة، قال التهانوي في «إعلاء السنن»: والصحيح أنه مرسل صحيح الإسناد.
2. عن أبي الدرداء: «إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قاء فأفطر فتوضأ» فلقيت ثوبان في مسجد دمشق فذكرت ذلك له فقال: صدق أنا صببت له الدفع في سنن الترمذي، وقال: «قد جود حسين المعلم هذا الحديث وحديث حسين أصح شيء في هذا الباب». 
3. عن ابن عمر قال: «إذا رعف الرجل في الصلاة، أو ذرعه القيء، أو وجد مذياً فإنه ينصرف ويتوضأ ثم يرجع فيتم ما بقي على ما مضى ما لم يتكلم» في «مصنف عبد الرزاق»، وإسناده صحيح.

أو عَلَقاً إن كان ملاً الفم لا بَلْغَماً أصلاً سواء كان نازلاً من الرأس، أو صاعداً من الجوف.

قوله : أو عَلَقاً؛ أي سوداء محترقة، وهو ما اشتدت حمرته وجمد، حتى لو كان سائلاً نقض، وإن قل. كذا في «النهر الفائق». وفي «الهداية»: «لو قاء دماً هو علق، يعتبر فيه ملأ الفم؛ لأنه سوداء محترقة». انتهى.
قال في «النهاية»: «قاء دماً منجمداً غير سائل كالعَلَقِ لم ينقض حتى يملأ الفم؛ لأن ذلك ليس بدم إنّما هي مرّة سوداء محترقة، والسوداء المحترقة تخرج من المعدة، وما يخرج منها لا يكون حدثاً ما لم يكن ملأ الفم». انتهى.

قوله : إن كان؛ أي قيء المرَّة والطعام والماء والعلق، بخلاف الدم الرقيق، فإنّه ناقض قليلاً كان أو كثيراً، إن كان من الجوف كما مر، ولا يشترط فيه كونه ملأ الفم عندهما خلافاً لمحمد.
وحد ملأ الفم اختلفوا فيه، والذي اختاره أكثر المشايخ أنه إن كان بحيث لا يمكن ضبطه وإمساكه إلا بتكلف فهو ملأ الفم، وما دونه قليل.
Book of Purity: Ablution. Then He conjoined to His saying: 'blood', His saying: 'or bile or food, or water'. His saying: 'or food'; whether he vomited it after some time from eating or immediately. And from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: that it does not nullify if he vomits immediately as long as it hasn't changed. And on this is the difference regarding a child who drinks milk and vomits immediately. The correct is the apparent transmission: that it is impure and nullifying due to the intermingling of impurity, unlike phlegm, and unlike vomit of a worm or snake; it does not nullify even if it fills the mouth because it is pure in itself, and the impurity accompanying it is little. This is in 'Ghunyat al-Munyah'. All this applies if the food exits after reaching the stomach; if it was still in the esophagus, it does not nullify, as in 'Qunyah'. If he vomited two different things, blood and food, or blood and phlegm, consideration is for the predominant; if equal, each is considered separately. This is in 'Mujtaba Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri'. His saying: 'or water'; if he drank water and it exited after reaching the stomach, it nullifies even if it is clear. This is in 'Zahiriyyah'. And among the proofs for nullification by vomiting: ... [hadith cited]. 'Or clotted blood if it fills the mouth, not phlegm at all, whether descending from the head or ascending from the stomach.' His saying: 'or clotted blood'; i.e., burnt black bile, which is what has become intensely red and congealed, so much so that if it were liquid it would nullify even if little. This is in 'Nahr al-Fa'iq'. And in 'Hidayah': 'If he vomited blood that is clotted, consider whether it fills the mouth because it is burnt black bile.' He said in 'Nihayah': 'He vomited congealed blood non-liquid like a clot does not nullify until it fills the mouth because that is not blood, rather it is burnt black bile, and burnt black bile emerges from the stomach, and what emerges from it is not a hadath unless it fills the mouth.' His saying: 'if it is'; i.e., vomit of bile, food, water, and clot, unlike thin blood, for it nullifies whether little or much, if from the stomach as mentioned, and its filling the mouth is not required according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Muhammad. And the definition of 'mouthful' they differed on, and what most mashayikh chose is that it is what cannot be held back except with effort; less than that is little.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the nullification of wudu by vomiting. The author is explaining the conditions under which various types of vomit break wudu. The types covered are food, water, bile, blood, clotted blood (علق), and phlegm. The general rule, based on the Zahir al-Riwayah, is that vomiting a mouthful (ملء الفم) of food, water, bile, or clotted blood from the stomach nullifies wudu. Clotted blood is considered burnt black bile, not true blood, so only a mouthful counts. True blood (thin, liquid) nullifies wudu regardless of quantity if it comes from the stomach, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; Muhammad holds that it must be a mouthful if not from the stomach. Phlegm, worms, and snakes do not nullify even if a mouthful because they are originally pure. The passage also provides hadith evidence from Aisha, Abu Darda, and Ibn Umar to support that vomiting breaks wudu. A key intra-Hanafi difference is mentioned: according to a narration from Abu Hanifa, if one vomits food immediately after eating without change, it may not nullify, but the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that it does nullify due to impurity intermingling. The definition of 'mouthful' is settled by the majority of mashayikh as that which cannot be retained without effort.

Key Terms
القيء (al-qay’): The expulsion of stomach contents through the mouth.
ملء الفم (mil’ al-fam): A mouthful; defined as that which cannot be held back without effort.
العلق (al-‘alaq): Clotted blood; congealed blood that is no longer liquid, considered burnt black bile.
البلغم (al-balgham): Phlegm; mucus from the respiratory system.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does vomiting a clot (علق) nullify wudu?
A) Always, regardless of quantity.
B) Only if it fills the mouth (ملء الفم).
C) Only if it comes from the stomach.
D) Never, because it is pure.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, wudu is nullified only by a mouthful of impure stomach contents; phlegm and pure substances do not break wudu.

Types of Vomit and Their Effect on Wudu: The Hanafi Position and Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling with dispute on cumulative vomit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for different types of vomit (thin blood, clotted blood, phlegm, bile/food/water) and apply the intra-Hanafi criteria for combining small amounts of vomit to determine nullification of wudu.

Core Idea
Not all vomit breaks wudu; thin blood always does, clotted blood only if mouthful, phlegm never.

In Hanafi fiqh, vomiting is not a simple nullifier of wudu. The ruling depends on the substance and quantity. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya details the types of vomit and highlights an important intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on how to count small amounts that together fill the mouth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of vomit
List the types mentioned: thin blood, clotted blood (burnt black bile), bile, food, water, and phlegm.
The text also mentions 'sawda' muhtariqa' for clotted blood.
2
Determine ruling for each type
Thin blood always nullifies wudu regardless of quantity if from stomach; clotted blood, bile, food, water only nullify if they fill the mouth; phlegm never nullifies.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Define 'mouthful' (mil' al-fam)
The preferred definition among later Hanafi scholars is what cannot be restrained without difficulty.
Other definitions mentioned but not adopted.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on cumulative vomit
Abu Yusuf considers unity of sitting (majlis): multiple small vomits in one sitting are combined. Muhammad considers unity of cause (sabab): multiple small vomits from the same cause are combined.
This applies only to substances that require the mouthful condition.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a case of repeated small vomits, state the ruling under each view and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
For example, if in one sitting but from different meals, Abu Yusuf would combine, Muhammad would not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عَلَقاً إن كان ملء الفم لا بَلْغَماً أصلاً سواء كان نازلاً من الرأس، أو صاعداً من الجوف. قوله : أو عَلَقاً؛ أي سوداء محترقة، وهو ما اشتدت حمرته وجمد، حتى لو كان سائلاً نقض، وإن قل. كذا في «النهر الفائق». وفي «الهداية»: «لو قاء دماً هو علق، يعتبر فيه ملء الفم؛ لأنه سوداء محترقة». انتهى. قال في «النهاية»: «قاء دماً منجمداً غير سائل كالعَلَقِ لم ينقض حتى يملأ الفم؛ لأن ذلك ليس بدم إنّما هي مرّة سوداء محترقة، والسوداء المحترقة تخرج من المعدة، وما يخرج منها لا يكون حدثاً ما لم يكن ملأ الفم». انتهى. قوله : إن كان؛ أي قيء المرة والطعام والماء والعلق، بخلاف الدم الرقيق، فإنّه ناقض قليلاً كان أو كثيراً، إن كان من الجوف كما مر، ولا يشترط فيه كونه ملأ الفم عندهما خلافاً لمحمد. وحد ملأ الفم اختلفوا فيه، والذي اختاره أكثر المشايخ أنه إن كان بحيث لا يمكن ضبطه وإمساكه إلا بتكلف فهو ملأ الفم، وما دونه قليل. وينقض صاعد ملء الفم عند أبي يوسف، وهو يعتبر الاتحاد في المجلس، وعند محمد : في السبب بجمع ما قاءَ قليلاً قليلاً. وسواء كان قليلاً أو كثيراً؛ لأنه للزوجته لا يتداخله النجاسة. وينقض صاعد ملء الفم عند أبي يوسف لكن النازل من الرأس لا ينقض عنده أيضاً. وهو يعتبر الاتحاد في المجلس، وعند محمد : في السبب بجمع ما قاءَ قليلاً قليلاً، فقوله : وهو يعتبر الضمير يرجع إلى أبي يوسف، وهذا ابتداء مسألة صورتها : إذا قاءَ قليلاً قليلاً بحيث لو جُمِعَ يبلغ ملأ الفم، فأبو يوسف يعتبر اتحاد المجلس، أي إذا كان في مجلس واحدٍ يُجْمَع، فيكون ناقضاً. قوله : للزوجته؛ اللزوجة مصدر، يقال : لَزِجَ الشيءُ لَزَجاً ولُزُوجة، من باب تَعِبَ : إذا كان فيه وَدَا يَعْلَقُ باليد ونحوها، كذا قال الفيومي في «المصباح». وحاصله : أنّ البَلْغَمَ بسبب كونه لَزَجاً لا تختلط معه النجاسة، وهو في نفسه ليس بنجس، فلا ينقض مطلقاً. فإن قلت : هذا منتقض ببلغم يقع في النجاسة ثم يرفع، فإنّه يحكم بنجاسته. قلت : البلغم ما دام في البطن يزدادُ ثخانة، فيزداد لُزُوجة، بخلاف ما إذا انفصل، فإنه حينئذ يرق فيتحمل النجاسة. كذا في «النهاية». قوله : عنده أيضاً؛ أي كما لا ينقض عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد فهو اتفاقي. قوله : وهو يعتبر... الخ؛ وجه قول أبي يوسف أن للمجلس أثراً في جميع المتفرقات، كما في «باب سجدة التلاوة» و«معاملات البيع والشراء»، وغير ذلك، فيعتبر المتعدد في مجلس واحد متحداً. ووجه قول محمد أن ثبوت الحكم على حسب ثبوت السبب، فيتحد باتحاده، ويتعدد بتعدده، ألا ترى أنه إذا جُرحَ جراحات كثيرة ومات منها قبل البرء يتحد.
Or [vomiting] clotted blood if it fills the mouth, not phlegm at all, whether it descends from the head or ascends from the stomach. His statement: 'or clotted blood' means burnt black bile, which is what has intensified in redness and solidified, such that if it were liquid it would nullify even if little. Thus in 'al-Nahr al-Fa'iq'. And in 'al-Hidayah': 'If he vomits blood that is clotted, the condition of filling the mouth is considered because it is burnt black bile.' End. He said in 'al-Nihayah': 'If he vomits congealed blood not flowing like clotted blood, it does not nullify until it fills the mouth because that is not blood but burnt black bile, and burnt black bile exits from the stomach, and what exits from it is not an event unless it fills the mouth.' End. His statement: 'if it is' meaning vomiting of bile, food, water, and clotted blood, unlike thin blood, for it nullifies whether little or much, if from the stomach as previously, and the condition of filling the mouth is not required according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Muhammad. And the definition of filling the mouth they differed on, and what most mashayikh chose is that it is whatever cannot be restrained and held except with difficulty, so it is filling the mouth, and less than that is little. And it nullifies [if] ascending filling the mouth according to Abu Yusuf, and he considers unity of the sitting, and according to Muhammad: in the cause, by collecting what he vomited little by little. And whether it is little or much; because due to its viscosity, impurity does not intermix with it. And it nullifies ascending filling the mouth according to Abu Yusuf, but descending from the head does not nullify according to him as well. And he considers unity of the sitting, and according to Muhammad: in the cause, by collecting what he vomited little by little. So his statement: 'and he considers' the pronoun refers to Abu Yusuf, and this is the beginning of a case whose form is: if he vomited little by little such that if collected it would amount to filling the mouth, then Abu Yusuf considers unity of the sitting, i.e., if it is in one sitting it is combined, so it is nullifying. His statement: 'due to its viscosity'; viscosity is a noun, it is said: the thing is viscous, viscosity, meaning it has stickiness that clings to the hand etc., thus al-Fayyumi said in 'al-Misbah'. And the summary: that phlegm due to its viscosity does not mix with impurity, and in itself it is not impure, so it does not nullify absolutely. If you say: this is contradicted by phlegm that falls into impurity then is lifted, for it is ruled impure. I say: phlegm as long as it is in the belly increases in thickness, so its viscosity increases, unlike when it separates, for then it becomes thin and can carry impurity. Thus in 'al-Nihayah'. His statement: 'according to him as well' means just as it does not nullify according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is unanimous. His statement: 'and he considers...' etc.; the basis of Abu Yusuf's statement is that the sitting has effect in gathering scattered things, as in the chapter of prostration of recitation and transactions of sale and purchase, etc., so multiple acts in one sitting are considered one. And the basis of Muhammad's statement is that the establishment of a ruling is according to the establishment of the cause, so it is united by its unity and multiple by its multiplicity; do you not see that if he is wounded multiple wounds and dies from them before healing, he is considered as having died from all?
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the types of vomit and their effect on wudu, with an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding how to count small amounts of vomit. The passage opens by stating the ruling: clotted blood ('alaq) only nullifies wudu if it fills the mouth, while phlegm (balgham) never nullifies wudu, regardless of whether it comes from the head or the stomach. The commentator explains that 'alaq here refers to burnt black bile (sawda' muhtariqa), not actual blood; if it were liquid blood, it would nullify wudu even if little. The Hidayah is quoted to confirm this. The Nihayah explains that congealed blood is not true blood but black bile, which only nullifies when it fills the mouth because it originates from the stomach. The passage then clarifies that the condition 'if it is' refers to vomit of bile, food, water, and clotted blood, as opposed to thin blood (dam raqiq) which nullifies whether little or much, provided it comes from the stomach. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, thin blood does not require the mouthful condition, but Muhammad holds a different view. The definition of 'mouthful' (mil' al-fam) is debated; the preferred view is that it is what cannot be held back without difficulty. The passage then addresses a scenario where someone vomits small amounts that would collectively fill the mouth. Abu Yusuf considers the unity of the sitting (majlis) — if the small emissions occur in one sitting, they are combined and nullify wudu. Muhammad considers the cause (sabab) — all emissions from the same cause are combined regardless of sitting. This is analogous to other legal chapters like prostration of recitation and transactions. Finally, the passage explains why phlegm never nullifies: due to its viscosity, it does not mix with impurity, and phlegm itself is not impure. A potential objection—that phlegm becomes impure after exiting the body—is answered: phlegm in the stomach is thicker and more viscous, so it does not carry impurity; upon exit, it thins and can carry impurity, but this does not affect the ruling of wudu.

Key Terms
القيء (al-qay'): Vomiting, the expulsion of stomach contents through the mouth.
العلَق (al-'alaq): Dried, clotted blood that is thick and not liquid; here it refers to burnt black bile (sawda' muhtariqa).
البَلْغَم (al-balgham): Phlegm, a viscous secretion from the respiratory tract. It never nullifies wudu regardless of quantity.
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): An amount that fills the mouth to the extent that it cannot be held back without difficulty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on vomiting pure phlegm (balgham) that does not fill the mouth?
A) It nullifies wudu regardless of quantity.
B) It nullifies wudu only if it fills the mouth.
C) It never nullifies wudu.
D) It nullifies wudu only if it comes from the stomach.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, only certain types of vomit nullify wudu, and the quantity matters except for thin blood; an intra-madhhab dispute exists on how to combine small amounts of vomit.

Combining Vomit: Session vs Cause
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining small vomit amounts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the criteria of unity of session and unity of cause to determine when small amounts of vomit combine to nullify wudu in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Small vomits combine only if session (Abu Yusuf) or cause (Muhammad) is unified — four scenarios determine nullification.

The Hanafi school agrees that vomiting a mouthful (mil' al-fam) nullifies wudu. But what if someone vomits small amounts repeatedly? Do they combine into a mouthful? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that unity of session (majlis) combines them, while Muhammad al-Shaybani insists on unity of cause (sabab/ghathayan). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate ikhtilaf within the madhhab and apply the four possible scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Recall that vomiting mouthful nullifies wudu; less than that does not. The dispute is only about combining small amounts.
Base ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Understand the two criteria
Abu Yusuf considers unity of session (majlis) – one continuous sitting. Muhammad considers unity of cause (sabab) – the nausea episode (ghathayan). Each cites analogies from other chapters.
Session: prostration of recitation, contracts. Cause: principle that rulings follow causes.
3
Work through the four scenarios
Combine the two binary criteria: session unified or not; cause unified or not. The text lists all four combinations and the resulting ruling for each Imam.
Scenarios: 1) both unified -> combine by agreement; 2) both differ -> no combination; 3) session unified, cause differs -> Abu Yusuf combines; 4) session differs, cause unified -> Muhammad combines.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentary notes that al-Kanz in al-Kafi prefers Muhammad's view as 'asahh' (most correct), because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes. However, some early scholars followed Abu Yusuf.
The mufta bihi may vary by context; today the preponderant view is Muhammad's.

[٢]قوله : عنده أيضاً ؛ أي كما لا ينقض عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد له فهو اتفاقي. [٣]قوله : وهو يعتبر... الخ؛ وجه قول أبي يوسف له أن للمجلس أثراً في جميع المتفرقات، كما في «باب سجدة التلاوة» و«معاملات البيع والشراء»، وغير ذلك، فيعتبر المتعدد في مجلس واحد متحداً.

الواجب، وإن تخلل البرء اختلف. كذا في «البحر الرائق»(۱).
[ 1 ] قوله : ومحمد يعتبر اتحاد السبب، قال صاحب «الكنز» في «الكافي» : «الأصح (۲) قول محمد ؛ لأنَّ الأصلَ إضافة الأحكام إلى الأسباب، وإنما ترك في بعض الصور للضرورة، كما في السجدة للتلاوة ؛ إذ لو اعتبر السبب لا يمكن التداخل ، وفي الأقارير يعتبر المجلس للعرف (۳) ، وفي الإيجاب والقبول ؛ لدفع الضرر». The Book of Purification: Wudu. 325. And ascending (vomit) that fills the mouth nullifies according to Abu Yusuf – may Allah have mercy on him – and he considers unity of session, while according to Muhammad: (it is) in the cause, combining what one vomited little by little. [1] And whether it is little or much; because it is viscous, impurity does not mix with it. (And ascending that fills the mouth nullifies according to Abu Yusuf) but descending from the head does not nullify according to him also. (And he considers unity of session, and according to Muhammad: in the cause, combining what one vomited little by little), so his statement: 'and he considers' – the pronoun refers to Abu Yusuf – and this is the beginning of a case whose description: if one vomited little by little such that if collected it would fill the mouth, then Abu Yusuf considers unity of session, i.e., if it was in one session it is combined, so it is a nullifier. [1] His statement: 'for its viscosity' – viscosity is a noun; it is said: the thing became sticky, stickiness and viscosity, from the door of ta'iba: if it has a sticky substance that clings to the hand and similar, as al-Fayyumi said in al-Misbah. Its summary: that phlegm, due to its being viscous, impurity does not mix with it, and it itself is not impure, so it does not nullify absolutely. If you say: this is contradicted by phlegm that falls into impurity then is removed, for it is judged impure. I say: phlegm as long as it is in the belly increases in thickness, so it increases in viscosity, unlike when it separates, then it becomes thin and bears impurity. Thus in al-Nihaya. [2] His statement: 'according to him also' – i.e., just as it does not nullify according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so it is agreed upon. [3] His statement: 'and he considers...' – the basis of Abu Yusuf's statement is that the session has an effect in all separate things, as in the chapter of prostration of recitation and transactions of sale and purchase, and other than that, so multiple (acts) in one session are considered united. And the basis of Muhammad's statement is that the establishment of the ruling is according to the establishment of the cause, so it is united with its unity and multiple with its multiplicity. Do you not see that if one is wounded with many wounds and dies from them before healing, it is united? And it is said: that which one cannot hold back. He said in al-Bada'i: upon it relied the Shaykh Abu Mansur, and it is the correct. See: Radd al-Muhtar (1:137) and others. And Muhammad considers unity of cause, which is nausea; if it is with one nausea it is combined, so it is a nullifier. Thus four scenarios arise: 1. Unity of session and nausea – it is combined by agreement. 2. Their difference – it is not combined by agreement. 3. Unity of session with difference of nausea – it is combined according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad. 4. Difference of session with unity of nausea – it is combined according to Muhammad contrary to Abu Yusuf. The obligatory (part), and if healing intervened it differs. Thus in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (1). [1] His statement: 'and Muhammad considers unity of cause' – the author of al-Kanz said in al-Kafi: 'The correct is the statement of Muhammad, because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes, and it is only abandoned in some cases due to necessity, as in the prostration of recitation; for if the cause were considered, overlapping would not be possible; and in confessions the session is considered due to custom; and in offer and acceptance to repel harm.' [2] His statement: 'which is nausea' – with fatha on the ghayn, tha, and ya, then alif and nun; and with dhamma on ghayn and sukun on tha; it is said: his soul became nauseous, i.e., it became agitated and disturbed, as said by al-Fayyumi in al-Misbah al-Munir. [3] His statement: 'it is combined' – as for Muhammad, due to unity of nausea; as for Abu Yusuf, due to unity of session.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the condition for vomiting to nullify wudu. The base ruling is that vomiting mouthful nullifies wudu. The dispute arises when someone vomits small amounts repeatedly: do they combine to count as a mouthful? Abu Yusuf holds that if the vomiting occurs in one continuous session (majlis), the amounts are combined even if due to multiple causes (nausea episodes). His evidence is that in other areas of fiqh (e.g., prostration of recitation, contracts), the session unifies separate acts. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the determining factor is the cause (sabab), i.e., the nausea (ghathayan). If the vomiting is from one episode of nausea, the amounts combine; if from separate episodes, they do not, even if in the same session. His evidence is that rulings are attached to causes, and unity of cause implies unity of ruling. The text presents four scenarios based on these two criteria: (1) both session and nausea are unified – combine by agreement; (2) both differ – do not combine by agreement; (3) session unified but nausea differs – combine according to Abu Yusuf only; (4) session differs but nausea unified – combine according to Muhammad only. The commentary also clarifies that phlegm (balgham) is viscous and does not mix with impurity while in the stomach, so it does not nullify wudu; but once it exits, it becomes thin and can carry impurity. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is considered, with some scholars preferring Muhammad's view because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes, and departure from this is only for necessity (e.g., in sujood al-tilawa, where if cause were considered, multiple prostrations could not be combined in one session). In confessions and contracts, the session is considered due to custom or necessity, but in vomiting, the cause is primary.

Key Terms
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): The amount of vomit that fills the mouth; the threshold for nullification of wudu.
الغثيان (al-ghathayan): Nausea; the cause of vomiting.
المجلس (al-majlis): The session or sitting; the time and place during which an action occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person vomits a small amount three times in one sitting, each from a separate episode of nausea (e.g., different nauseas), and the total would fill the mouth, does this nullify wudu according to Muhammad al-Shaybani?
A) Yes, because the session is unified.
B) No, because the cause (nausea) is not unified.
C) Yes, because the total is mouthful.
D) No, because vomiting never nullifies wudu.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes two criteria for combining small vomit amounts: unity of session (Abu Yusuf) and unity of cause (Muhammad), yielding four scenarios.

The Principle: What is Not Hadath is Not Necessarily Najis
Statement of a fiqh principle and its commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle 'ma laysa bi hadath laysa bi najis' and explain the distinction between hadath (ritual impurity) and najasah (physical impurity).

Core Idea
Hadath and najasah are separate: not every physical impurity nullifies wudu, and not every nullifier is physically impure.

This lesson introduces a crucial Hanafi principle that distinguishes between two types of impurity: ritual impurity (hadath) that requires ablution, and physical impurity (najasah) that must be washed off. Students often conflate the two, but this principle clarifies that they are independent categories. The principle is stated in the context of nullifiers of wudu, to prevent the mistaken assumption that if something does not nullify wudu, it is automatically pure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal meaning
Read the Arabic phrase: 'wa ma laysa bi hadath laysa bi najis' – 'And what is not a hadath is not najis.' Note the negation on both sides.
The principle is a negative correlation: the absence of hadath does not imply absence of najasah.
2
Distinguish hadath and najasah
Define each term: hadath is a state of ritual impurity requiring wudu or ghusl; najasah is physical impurity that must be removed from the body or clothing. They belong to different categories.
Hanafi school: hadath is a legal state, najasah is a tangible impurity.
3
Understand the context (istitrad)
The commentator notes that this principle is mentioned as a digression (istitrad) in the discussion of nullifiers of wudu, because the preceding talk about blood and vomit might lead one to think that if it doesn't nullify wudu, it's pure. This principle corrects that.
This shows the careful reasoning of the Hanafi fuqaha.
4
Apply to examples
Consider small amounts of blood: they do not nullify wudu (not hadath), but they are physically impure (najis) if from an impure source. The principle confirms they are not automatically pure.
Similarly, vomit, pus, etc. – not hadath but may be najis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ليس بحدث ليس بنجس
[٤] قوله : وما ليس بحدث ليس بنجس ; قد جرت عادتهم بذكر هذه القاعدة في بحث النواقض استطراداً، فإنه لَمَّا انجر الكلام إلى أن القليل من الدم والقيء ونحوهما لا
And what is not a hadath is not najis. [4] His statement: 'And what is not a hadath is not najis' – their custom has been to mention this rule in the discussion of nullifiers as a digression, for when the talk led to the point that a small amount of blood, vomit and the like does not...
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This is a basic usul al-fiqh principle: something that does not cause ritual impurity (hadath, i.e., requiring wudu/ghusl) is not inherently ritually impure (najis). For example, small amounts of blood or vomit do not nullify wudu (are not hadath), but they may be physically impure (najis) if they come from an impure source. However, here the principle is stated to clarify that if something is not a nullifier of wudu, it does not mean it is pure; it simply means it does not require wudu. The commentator says this rule is mentioned as an aside (istitrad) in the discussion of nullifiers, because the earlier discussion of blood and vomit might lead one to think that if it doesn't nullify, it must be pure, but that is not the case.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity that requires ablution (wudu or ghusl) to remove.
نجس (najis): Something that is physically impure and must be removed for prayer validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle stated, if a liquid does not nullify wudu (is not a hadath), does it automatically mean it is physically pure (not najis)?
A) Yes, the two categories are linked.
B) No, the categories are separate; a substance may be physically impure even if it does not nullify wudu.
C) Only if it is from a pure source.
D) It depends on the school.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle separates ritual impurity (hadath) from physical impurity (najasah); knowing this prevents the error of assuming all impurities affect wudu equivalently.

The Principle and the Vomiting Nullification Dispute
Commentary on a matn rule and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule 'what is not a hadath is not najis', explain the dispute on gathering vomiting instances, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Not every nullifier of wudu is impure; only hadath is najis. Vomiting gathers by cause, not sitting.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya' covers two interconnected topics: first, the general Hanafi principle that anything which does not nullify wudu (hadath) is not intrinsically impure (najis), and second, an internal Hanafi dispute on whether multiple instances of vomiting in different sittings should be considered a single nullifier. The commentary explains the four scenarios and provides the basis for the tarjih (preferred view).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
Locate the phrase 'wa mā laysa bi ḥadath laysa bi najis' and note its meaning: anything that is not a wudu nullifier is not impure. This is a general principle applied to substances exiting the body.
The rule is stated twice in the passage, first briefly and then expanded with commentary.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Focus on the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding how to gather multiple instances of vomiting. Abu Yusuf considers the sitting (majlis); Muhammad considers the cause (sabab, i.e., one bout of vomiting). Trace the four logical scenarios.
The passage numbers them explicitly: 1. same sitting & same vomiting → gather by agreement. 2. different sitting & different vomiting → no gather by agreement. 3. same sitting & different vomiting → Abu Yusuf says gather, Muhammad says no. 4. different sitting & same vomiting → Muhammad says gather, Abu Yusuf says no.
3
Examine the evidence for each view
Read the quote from Sahib al-Kanz in al-Kafi. Note that Muhammad's view is deemed 'al-asah' (most correct) because rulings are attached to causes, except where necessity dictates otherwise (e.g., prostration of recitation). Abu Yusuf analogizes to acknowledgments and contracts where sitting is considered due to custom.
The passage uses istihsan (juristic preference) as a method.
4
Apply the rule to the exception of istihadah
The commentary raises an objection: istihadah blood is not a hadath yet is najis. The response: it is a hadath but its effect is delayed to avoid hardship. This shows the rule is nuanced.
This is a common objection in Haramayn discussions.
5
Analyze the linguistic point of 'najis'
The author clarifies that 'najis' with kasra (adjective) and with fatha (noun) both apply. The negation of the general (kasra) necessarily negates the specific (fatha).
This linguistic precision avoids misinterpretation.

الواجب، وإن تخلل البرء اختلف. كذا في «البحر الرائق»(۱).
[ 1 ] قوله : ومحمد يعتبر اتحاد السبب، قال صاحب «الكنز» في «الكافي» : «الأصح (۲) قول محمد ؛ لأنَّ الأصلَ إضافة الأحكام إلى الأسباب، وإنما ترك في بعض الصور للضرورة، كما في السجدة للتلاوة ؛ إذ لو اعتبر السبب لا يمكن التداخل ، وفي الأقارير يعتبر المجلس للعرف (۳) ، وفي الإيجاب والقبول ؛ لدفع الضرر». [٢] قوله : وهو الغثيان ؛ بفتحات الغين المعجمة، والثاء المثلثة، والياء المثناة التحتية، ثم ألف ونون، وبضم الغين وسكون الثاء ، يقال : غنّت نفسه ؛ أي هاجت واضطربت، قاله الفيومي في «المصباح المنير» [٣] قوله : فيجمع ؛ أما عند محمد لهلهلهلهله فلاتحاد الغثيان، وأما عند أبي يوسف له فلاتحاد المجلس.
[٤] قوله : وما ليس بحدث ليس بنجس ؛ قد جرت عادتهم بذكر هذه القاعدة في بحث النواقض استطراداً، فإنه لَمَّا انجر الكلام إلى أن القليل من الدم والقيء ونحوهما لا ينقض الوضوء أن دوا ذكر حكمه في النجاسة والطهارة، فمنهم من يذكرها بلفظ : «كل»، ومنهم تالمصنّف من يصدّرها بـ«ما»، والمفاد واحد ؛ فإنّ ما للعموم. وحاصله : أن كل شيء ليس بحدث ؛ أي ناقض للوضوء ليس بنجس حتى لا يجب غسله، ولا يضرُّ أداء الصلاة معه.
فان قلت : هذا منقوض بدم الاستحاضة ؛ فإنّه ليس بحدث مع أ ع أنه نجس. فلت هو حدث، لكن لم يظهر أثره إلى خروج الوقت دفعاً للحرج. كذا في «البناية» (۱).
ثم المراد بما ليس بحدث ما يخرج من بدن الإنسان الحي، كما يقتضيه سوق المقام، فلا ينتقض بالنجاسات الخارجة من بدن الميت والحيوانات، وفي المقام تفصيل مذكور في «السعاية» (٢).
[١]قوله : بكسر الجيم ; فَهِمَ أكثرُ المحسين أنّ التصريح بالكسر ; لعدم استقامة الفتح، ووجهوه بتوجيهات كلّها باطلة، كما بسطناها في «السعاية»(۳). والحق أن الفتح أيضاً مستقيم، بل هو الأوفق بالمقام ; فإنه قد مر أن النجس بالكسر ما لا يكون طاهراً ، وبالفتح عين النجاسة ، والمقصود هاهنا إنما هو بيان أنّ ما ليس بحدث ليس بنجاسة، وإنّما نص على الكسر بناءً على أنه المنقولُ المسموع عن المصنف وغيره، ويلزم من نفي كونه نجساً بالكسر نفي كونه نجساً بالفتح أيضاً، فإنَّ نفي العام مستلزم لنفي الخاص. [٢] قوله : انتفاء كونه نجساً ; يحتمل أن يكون النجس هاهنا بالفتح ، والـفـاء تفريعية، يعني لما ذكرَ أنَّ ما ليس بحدث ليس نجساً بالكسر ، عُلِمَ منه أنَّ انتفاء كونه نجساً بالكسر لازم لانتفاء كونه حدثاً ، ومن المعلوم أن المفتوح أخص من المكسور
And what is not a hadath is not impure. And Muhammad considers unity of cause, which is vomiting: if the vomiting is one, it is gathered and thus a nullifier. So four scenarios arise: 1. Unity of sitting and vomiting – gathered by agreement. 2. Difference of both – not gathered by agreement. 3. Unity of sitting with difference of vomiting – gathered according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. 4. Difference of sitting with unity of vomiting – gathered according to Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. (And what is not a hadath is not impure) is obligatory, and if recovery intervenes, there is difference. So in 'Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq'. 1. His statement: 'And Muhammad considers unity of cause', the author of 'Al-Kanz' said in 'Al-Kafi': 'The most correct is the statement of Muhammad because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes, and it is abandoned in some cases due to necessity, as in the prostration of recitation; for if cause were considered, overlap would not be possible. And in acknowledgments, the sitting is considered due to custom, and in offer and acceptance to repel harm.' 2. His statement: 'which is vomiting': with fatḥa on ghayn, thā', yā', then alif and nūn, and with ḍamma on ghayn and sukūn on thā'. It is said: 'His soul vomited' meaning it became agitated and disturbed – al-Fayyūmī said it in 'Al-Miṣbāḥ al-Munīr'. 3. His statement: 'gathered': according to Muhammad due to unity of vomiting, and according to Abu Yusuf due to unity of sitting. 4. His statement: 'And what is not a hadath is not impure': their custom is to mention this rule in the discussion of nullifiers as a digression. Because when the discussion led to that a small amount of blood, vomit and the like do not nullify wudū', they mentioned its ruling on impurity and purity. Some mention it with the word 'kull', and the author initiates it with 'mā', and the meaning is one; for 'mā' indicates generality. Its summary: everything that is not a hadath, i.e. a nullifier of wudū', is not impure, so washing it is not obligatory and performing prayer with it does not harm. If you say: this is refuted by the blood of istiḥāḍa; for it is not a hadath while it is impure. So we say: it is a hadath, but its effect does not appear until the exit of the time to repel hardship. So in 'Al-Bināya'. Then what is meant by 'what is not a hadath' is what exits from the body of a living human, as the context requires; so it is not refuted by impurities exiting from the body of a dead person or animals. There is detail in 'Al-Si'āya'. 1. His statement: 'with kasra on jīm': most of the correct scholars understood that the specific mention of kasra is because fatḥa is not acceptable, and they explained it with explanations all invalid, as we elaborated in 'Al-Si'āya'. The truth is that fatḥa is also acceptable, rather it is more appropriate to the context; for it has passed that 'najis' with kasra means what is not ṭāhir, and with fatḥa means the essence of impurity. What is intended here is only to explain that what is not a hadath is not impurity, and he specifically mentioned kasra based on that it is the transmitted and heard from the author and others. And the negation of being 'najis' with kasra necessitates the negation of being 'najis' with fatḥa as well; for the negation of the general necessitates the negation of the specific. 2. His statement: 'negation of being impure': it is possible that 'najis' here is with fatḥa, and the fā' is consequential, meaning when he mentioned that what is not a hadath is not 'najis' with kasra, it is understood that the negation of being 'najis' with kasra is necessary for the negation of being a hadath, and it is known that the one with fatḥa is more specific than the one with kasra.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn: 'And what is not a hadath is not impure.' The author explains that this is a general rule: anything that does not nullify wudū' (hadath) is not impure (najis), so it does not need to be washed and does not invalidate prayer. However, an exception is made for istiḥāḍa blood which is a hadath but its effect is delayed. The passage also details an intra-Hanafī dispute between Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad regarding whether multiple instances of vomiting in different sittings gather to nullify wudū'. The rule is that vomiting nullifies wudū' if it is a mouthful. The dispute: Abū Yūsuf considers the unity of the sitting (majlis): if vomiting occurs in one sitting, it is gathered into one nullifier. Muḥammad considers the unity of the cause (sabab), i.e. the vomiting itself: if the vomiting is a single cause (one bout), it gathers even if in different sittings. The commentary mentions four scenarios and states that the relied-upon position (ṣaḥīḥ) is Muḥammad's, as in Al-Kāfī, because rulings are attached to causes. The author also discusses the word 'najis' with kasra (adjective) vs. fatḥa (noun) and shows that the rule covers both meanings.

Key Terms
حدث (ḥadath): In this passage, hadath refers to any nullifier of wudū' that also renders a person impure.
نجس (najis): Here it means impure substance that must be removed from the body or clothes for prayer validity.
غثيان (ghathayān): Vomiting or retching; the cause that nullifies wudū' when it reaches the mouth.
سبب (sabab): In this context, sabab refers to the cause of the vomiting (one bout) as opposed to the sitting (majlis).
مجلس (majlis): The sitting or session in which an action occurs; used to determine whether multiple instances of vomiting are gathered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when does vomiting nullify wudu?
A) Only if it occurs in the same sitting.
B) Only if it is a mouthful and from the same cause, even across different sittings.
C) Any amount of vomit nullifies wudu.
D) Vomiting never nullifies wudu.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only what nullifies wudu (hadath) is impure; vomiting nullifies when it reaches a mouthful, and the mufta bihi position considers the cause (bout) for gathering multiple instances, not the sitting.

Taharah — Purification

The General Rule: What is Not Hadath is Not Najis
Expanding the rule that non-hadath substances are not impure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that substances which are not hadath are not impure and apply it to non-flowing blood.

Core Idea
If it doesn't break wudu, it isn't impure – a key principle for purity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes a foundational rule: anything that is not a hadath (ritual impurity that breaks wudu) is not najis (impure). The commentary clarifies the scope and handles a notable counterexample – istihadah blood – to preserve the rule's universality. Understanding this rule simplifies taharah for everyday worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage begins by stating the logical entailment: 'From the negation of it being a hadath follows the negation of it being najis.' Understand that if something does not break wudu, it is not impure.
The author uses 'ma' (what) to indicate generality.
2
Recognize the Scope
The rule applies only to what exits from a living human body. It does not apply to dead bodies or animals.
3
Examine the Counterexample: Istihadah Blood
The passage addresses an objection: istihadah blood seems to be impure but not a hadath. The response is that istihadah is actually a hadath, but its effect is delayed until the end of prayer time to avoid hardship.
This preserves the universality of the rule.
4
Apply the Rule to Non-Flowing Blood
Blood that does not flow from a wound is not a hadath, thus it is not impure. Prayer with it is valid.
The condition is that the blood must not flow (i.e., no trickling).
5
Note the Linguistic Nuance
The commentator distinguishes between 'najis' (with kasra, meaning unclean) and 'najis' (with fat-ha, meaning impurity itself). Negating the general (kasra) automatically negates the specific (fat-ha).

Source Text

Book of Purification: Ablution. It is with kasra of the jim (najis). From the negation of it being a hadath follows the negation of it being najas. Thus, blood that does not flow from the head of a wound is pure. It breaks ablution? [Text unclear]... The summary: Everything that is not a hadath, i.e., a breaker of ablution, is not impure, so it is not obligatory to wash it, and performing prayer with it does not harm. If you say: This is contradicted by istihadah blood, for it is not a hadath while it is impure. I reply: It is a hadath, but its effect does not appear until the time exits to avoid difficulty. This is in "al-Binayah". Then what is intended by 'what is not a hadath' is what exits from the body of a living human, as the context requires. It does not apply to impurities exiting from a dead body or animals. Detail is in "al-Si'ayah". Comment: "His saying: with kasra of the jim"... The correct view is that the fat-h is also correct, and it is more appropriate here. For it has passed that 'najis' with kasra is what is not pure, and with fat-h is the essence of impurity. The intent here is to state that what is not a hadath is not impurity. Negating the general (kasra) necessitates negating the specific (fat-h). "His saying: negation of its being najis"... It may be with fat-h, and the fa' is explanatory. Likewise small vomit. And from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) in non-riwayah al-usul: it is impure because flow has no effect on impurity. So if the flowing is impure, then the non-flowing is also the same. Objection: then the rule is not universal. Reply: The meaning of its not being impure is that it does not acquire the attribute of impurity due to its exit. This is in "Radd al-Muhtar". It is the saying of al-Shafi'i, and some of our shaykhs gave fatwa with it, but the mufta bihi is the narration from Abu Yusuf that it is not impure, supported by what al-Bukhari narrated in a suspended form from al-Hasan: "The Muslims continued to pray with their wounds."
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary expanding on the rule that anything which is not a hadath (ritual impurity that breaks wudu) is not najis (impure). The commentator explains that the author, al-Marghinani, uses the word 'ma' (what) to indicate generality. The core ruling: if something is not a hadath, it is not impure, so it does not need to be washed before prayer and does not invalidate prayer if present. 

A counterexample is raised: istihadah blood (non-menstrual uterine bleeding) is not a hadath according to some interpretations, yet it is impure. The answer: istihadah blood is actually a hadath (it breaks wudu), but its legal effect (requiring renewal of wudu) is delayed until the end of the prayer time to avoid hardship—this is a special dispensation. So it does not break the rule. 

The passage then specifies that 'what is not a hadath' refers only to things exiting from a living human body, not from a dead body or animals. This limits the rule. 

Then the commentary delves into the linguistic point of 'najis' with kasra (unclean) vs. fat-h (impurity substance). The author clarifies that negating the state of being unclean (kasra) automatically negates the substance (fat-h) because the general negation includes the specific. 

The next section discusses small vomit (qay' qalil) that does not fill the mouth. According to Imam Muhammad in a non-riwayah al-usul transmission, it is impure because flow does not matter; if the flowing part is impure, the non-flowing part is also impure. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, as supported by Abu Yusuf and favored in al-Hidayah, is that it is not impure. Evidence: the practice of the early Muslims praying with wounds as cited by al-Bukhari from al-Hasan al-Basri. The commentary also notes that if the vomit is inherently impure (e.g., vomit of wine), it is impure by nature but does not break wudu due to its small quantity. 

Thus the passage balances the general rule with its practical exceptions and intra-madhhab differences, providing a nuanced understanding of purity in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): A state of ritual impurity that invalidates wudu and requires renewal of ablution.
نجس (najis): A substance that is intrinsically impure, such as blood, urine, wine, etc., that must be removed for prayer validity.
قيء قليل (qay' qalil): Vomit that does not fill the mouth. Considered a minor amount that does not break wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on blood that does not flow from a wound?
A) It is najis and must be washed before prayer.
B) It is not najis and prayer is valid.
C) It breaks wudu.
D) It is pure but still breaks wudu.

Key Takeaway
Non-flowing blood from a living human is not a hadath and thus not impure; prayer with it is valid.

Small Vomit: Is It Impure?
Expansion of the rule regarding small vomit and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on the impurity of small vomit and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Small vomit is not najis in the Hanafi mufta bihi, despite a minority opinion.

The second part of the passage addresses small vomit (qay' qalil) that does not fill the mouth. While some Hanafi scholars and Imam al-Shafi'i consider it impure, the relied-upon position is different. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi approach to purity and the weight given to ease and practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue: Small Vomit
Recognize that small vomit (not filling the mouth) is the subject. It does not break wudu but its impurity status is debated.
2
Identify the Differing Opinions
Abu Yusuf holds it is not najis. Muhammad (in a non-riwayah al-usul) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold it is najis.
3
Understand the Proofs
The mufta bihi relies on the hadith of Muslims praying with wounds. Opponents argue that impurity is intrinsic; flow is irrelevant.
4
Weigh the Positions
Al-Hidayah validates the view of Abu Yusuf, and later scholars follow this, making it the mufta bihi.
5
Apply the Rule
In practice, small vomit does not require washing for prayer, unless it comes from an inherently impure source (e.g., wine).
Exception: vomit of wine is impure by essence.

كتاب الطهارة الوضوء [۱] ۳۲۷ أي بكسر الجيم فيلز ) من انتفاء كونِهِ حَدَثاً انتفاء كونِهِ نَجا ، فالدَّمُ إذا لم يسل عن رأس الجرح طاهر ينقض الوضوء أن دوا ذكر حكمه في النجاسة والطهارة، فمنهم من يذكرها بلفظ : ومنهم تالمصنّف من يصدّرها بـ«ما»، والمفاد واحد ؛ فإنّ ما للعموم. وحاصله : أن كل شيء ليس بحدث ؛ أي ناقض للوضوء ليس بنجس حتى لا «كل»، يجب غسله، ولا يضرُّ أداء الصلاة معه.

ثم المراد بما ليس بحدث ما يخرج من بدن الإنسان الحي، كما يقتضيه سوق المقام، فلا ينتقض بالنجاسات الخارجة من بدن الميت والحيوانات، وفي المقام تفصيل مذكور في «السعاية» (٢).

(۲)

[١]قوله : بكسر الجيم ؛ فَهِمَ أكثرُ المحسين أنّ التصريح بالكسر ؛ لعدم استقامة الفتح، ووجهوه بتوجيهات كلّها باطلة، كما بسطناها في «السعاية»(۳). والحق أن الفتح أيضاً مستقيم، بل هو الأوفق بالمقام ؛ فإنه قد مر أن النجس بالكسر ما لا يكون طاهراً ، وبالفتح عين النجاسة ، والمقصود هاهنا إنما هو بيان أنّ ما ليس بحدث ليس بنجاسة، وإنّما نص على الكسر بناءً على أنه المنقولُ المسموع عن المصنف وغيره، ويلزم من نفي كونه نجساً بالكسر نفي كونه نجساً بالفتح أيضاً، فإنَّ نفي العام مستلزم لنفي الخاص. Book of Purification: Ablution. It is with kasra of the jim (najis). From the negation of it being a hadath follows the negation of it being najas. Thus, blood that does not flow from the head of a wound is pure. It breaks ablution? [Text unclear]... The summary: Everything that is not a hadath, i.e., a breaker of ablution, is not impure, so it is not obligatory to wash it, and performing prayer with it does not harm. If you say: This is contradicted by istihadah blood, for it is not a hadath while it is impure. I reply: It is a hadath, but its effect does not appear until the time exits to avoid difficulty. This is in "al-Binayah". Then what is intended by 'what is not a hadath' is what exits from the body of a living human, as the context requires. It does not apply to impurities exiting from a dead body or animals. Detail is in "al-Si'ayah". Comment: "His saying: with kasra of the jim"... The correct view is that the fat-h is also correct, and it is more appropriate here. For it has passed that 'najis' with kasra is what is not pure, and with fat-h is the essence of impurity. The intent here is to state that what is not a hadath is not impurity. Negating the general (kasra) necessitates negating the specific (fat-h). "His saying: negation of its being najis"... It may be with fat-h, and the fa' is explanatory. Likewise small vomit. And from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) in non-riwayah al-usul: it is impure because flow has no effect on impurity. So if the flowing is impure, then the non-flowing is also the same. Objection: then the rule is not universal. Reply: The meaning of its not being impure is that it does not acquire the attribute of impurity due to its exit. This is in "Radd al-Muhtar". It is the saying of al-Shafi'i, and some of our shaykhs gave fatwa with it, but the mufta bihi is the narration from Abu Yusuf that it is not impure, supported by what al-Bukhari narrated in a suspended form from al-Hasan: "The Muslims continued to pray with their wounds."
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary expanding on the rule that anything which is not a hadath (ritual impurity that breaks wudu) is not najis (impure). The commentator explains that the author, al-Marghinani, uses the word 'ma' (what) to indicate generality. The core ruling: if something is not a hadath, it is not impure, so it does not need to be washed before prayer and does not invalidate prayer if present. 

A counterexample is raised: istihadah blood (non-menstrual uterine bleeding) is not a hadath according to some interpretations, yet it is impure. The answer: istihadah blood is actually a hadath (it breaks wudu), but its legal effect (requiring renewal of wudu) is delayed until the end of the prayer time to avoid hardship—this is a special dispensation. So it does not break the rule. 

The passage then specifies that 'what is not a hadath' refers only to things exiting from a living human body, not from a dead body or animals. This limits the rule. 

Then the commentary delves into the linguistic point of 'najis' with kasra (unclean) vs. fat-h (impurity substance). The author clarifies that negating the state of being unclean (kasra) automatically negates the substance (fat-h) because the general negation includes the specific. 

The next section discusses small vomit (qay' qalil) that does not fill the mouth. According to Imam Muhammad in a non-riwayah al-usul transmission, it is impure because flow does not matter; if the flowing part is impure, the non-flowing part is also impure. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, as supported by Abu Yusuf and favored in al-Hidayah, is that it is not impure. Evidence: the practice of the early Muslims praying with wounds as cited by al-Bukhari from al-Hasan al-Basri. The commentary also notes that if the vomit is inherently impure (e.g., vomit of wine), it is impure by nature but does not break wudu due to its small quantity. 

Thus the passage balances the general rule with its practical exceptions and intra-madhhab differences, providing a nuanced understanding of purity in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): A state of ritual impurity that invalidates wudu and requires renewal of ablution.
نجس (najis): A substance that is intrinsically impure, such as blood, urine, wine, etc., that must be removed for prayer validity.
قيء قليل (qay' qalil): Vomit that does not fill the mouth. Considered a minor amount that does not break wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on small vomit (qay' qalil)?
A) It is najis and must be washed before prayer.
B) It is not najis, so prayer is valid without washing.
C) It breaks wudu but is not impure.
D) It is pure but breaks wudu.

Key Takeaway
Small vomit is not impure in the Hanafi mufta bihi, based on the ease of the early Muslims' practice.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Purity of Non-Flowing Blood: Hanafi Proof from Quran
Proof and defense of Hanafi position on non-flowing blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on non-flowing blood, cite the Quranic proof, and explain why the objection from Madani verses is rejected.

Core Idea
The Quran restricts prohibition to flowing blood; non-flowing blood remains pure and permissible.

This lesson examines the Hanafi proof that non-flowing blood (e.g., from a small cut) is pure (tahir) and does not break wudu. The central evidence is Surah al-An'am 6:145, which prohibits only 'flowing blood'. The passage also addresses objections, including an intra-Hanafi argument about possible abrogation by later Madani verses, and a second objection regarding human blood. Understanding this proof clarifies the unique Hanafi position on blood impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
State clearly: The Hanafi school holds that non-flowing blood (dam ghayr masfuh) is pure (tahir) and permissible (mubah). Identify the act or substance in question.
The subject is blood that does not flow, e.g., from a minor wound.
2
Locate the primary proof
Find the Quranic verse (Al-An'am 6:145) that the Hanafi author cites. Note that it prohibits 'flowing blood' (dam masfuh) specifically.
The verse is Makki; it lists four prohibited edibles.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
Explain why the Hanafi school uses this verse to prove non-flowing blood is pure. The key is that by specifying 'flowing' as the condition for prohibition, the verse implies that non-flowing is not prohibited. Since every impure thing is prohibited, non-flowing blood is therefore not impure.
This reasoning relies on the principle: 'if it were impure, it would be forbidden'.
4
Address the first objection (abrogation)
Ibrahim al-Halabi objects that later Madani verses (e.g., al-Baqarah, al-Ma'idah) mention 'blood' without the qualifier 'flowing', possibly abrogating the restriction. The Hanafi response is that the absolute mention is interpreted as flowing blood, as detailed in 'al-Sa'ayah'.
The restriction is not abrogated; the Madani verses are understood in light of the Makki verse.
5
Address the second objection (human blood)
A second objection argues that the proof only works for edible animals; for humans (whose flesh is not eaten), non-flowing blood is also forbidden, so purity cannot be deduced. The Hanafi response: the prohibition of flowing blood is general; non-flowing blood remains on its original ruling of permissibility (asl al-ibaha) because no text prohibits it.
This uses the usul principle that silence of the text does not negate the original permissibility.
