كتاب الطهارة الوضوء
ولنا : قوله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لَّا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرِّماً ﴾ إلى قوله : أَوْ دَمًا مسفوحا ، فغير المسفوح لا يكون محرَّماً ، فلا يكون نجساً، والدم الذي لم يسل عن رأس الجرح دم غير مسفوح ، فلا يكون نجساً.
وجوابه : إن الشرع اعتبر وصف السيلان في كون الشيء نجساً، حيث اشترطه في انتقاض الطهارة به.
[1]قوله : ولنا... الخ؛ حاصله : أنَّ الله تعالى قال في سورة الأنعام مخاطباً لنبيه : قُل لَّا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرَّمًا عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ إِلَّا أَن يَكُونَ مَيْتَةً أَوْ دَمًا مَّسْفُوحًا أَوْ لَحْمَ خِنْزِيرٍ فَإِنَّهُ رِجْسٌ أَوْ فِسْقًا أُهِلَّ لِغَيْرِ اللَّهِ بِهِ الآية ) ، فخص الحرمة في المطعومات بأربعة : الميتة والدم المسفوح أي السائل، ولحم الخنزير، وما ذبح بقصد التقرب إلى غير الله الله.
فعُلِمَ منه أن غير المسفوح ليس بحرام، وإذا ثبت ذلك ثبت أنه ليس بنجس ؛ لأنه لو كان نجساً كان حراماً، فإن كل نجس حرام. وأورد عليه إبراهيم الحلبي في «الغُنية شرح المنية» بأن الآية المذكورة مكية، وسورة البقرة والمائدة مدنيتان، وذكر فيهما حرمة الدم مطلقاً من غير قيد المسفوح ، فلِمَ لا يكون التقييد منسوخاً بالإطلاق.
والجواب عنه : أن الدم المطلق محمول على المسفوح ، كما فصلته في «السعاية».
فإن قيل : هذا فيما يؤكل لحمه، وأما فيما لا يؤكل لحمه كالآدمي فغيرُ المسفوح حرام أيضاً ، فلا يمكن الاستدلال بحله على طهارته. قلت : لما حكم بحرمة المسفوح بقى غير المسفوح على أصله، وهو الحل [1]قوله : فإن قيل ... الخ ؛ هذا إيراد على الاستدلال المذكور، وتوضيحه: أنّ ما ذكره المستدل من أنَّ المسفوح حرام، وغير المسفوح ليس بحرام، فلا يكون نجساً، إنما هو في الحيوانات التي تؤكل لحمها كالغنم والبقر وغيرهما، فيصح فيها الاستدلال بحله على طهارته ، فإنّه لو كان نجساً لكان حراماً.
وأما فيما لا يؤكل كالآدمي فغير المسفوح أيضاً حرام، يحرم أكله والانتفاع به فلا يتمشى الاستدلال المذكور هاهنا مع أن المقصود إنما في الدم الغير المسفوح الخارج من بدن الإنسان.
[2] قوله : قلت . الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ حرمة الدم المسفوح في الآية مطلقة، فيعلم منه أن الدم المسفوح سواء كان فيما يؤكل أو في غيره حرام، ويلزم منه حلّ غير المسفوح مطلقاً، فيلزم منه كونه طاهراً مطلقاً.
[3] قوله : بقي غير المسفوح على أصله ؛ إنّما لم يقل : عُلِمَ به حل غير المسفوح ؛ لأن تخصيص أمر بوصف والحكم عليه لا يدلُّ على نفي الحكم عما عداه عندنا، كما عرف في كتب الأصول. فلما حكم بحرمة الدم المقيَّدِ بالمسفوح لم يثبت منه إلا حرمته لأصل الدم الغير المسفوح، وعدم حله ؛ فإنّه مسكوت عنه في النص، لا يعلم حكمه به لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً ، وإنما يعلم حلّ غير المسفوح لبقائه على أصله وعدم ورود نص يحرمه.
[4]قوله : وهو الحل ؛ بكسر الحاء المهملة وتشديد اللام أشار به إلى أن الأصل في الأشياء الحل والإباحة إلى أن يدل دليل على عدم الإباحة، وهذا هو مذهب بعض الحنفية ومنهم الكرخي وهو الذي اختاره في «الهداية».
Book of Purification, Ablution. And we have: the statement of Allah the Exalted: 'Say, I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden...' up to His saying: 'or flowing blood.' So non-flowing is not forbidden, thus it is not impure. And blood that does not flow from the head of a wound is non-flowing blood, so it is not impure. And its answer: Indeed the Law considered the description of flow in a thing being impure, since it stipulated it in the nullification of purification by it. [1] His statement: 'and we have... etc.' Its summary: that Allah the Exalted said in Surah al-An'am addressing His Prophet: 'Say, I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden to one who eats it except that it be carrion or flowing blood or flesh of swine - for indeed it is abomination - or that it be slaughtered as a devotion to other than Allah.' So He specified prohibition in edibles to four: carrion, flowing blood i.e. liquid, swine flesh, and what is slaughtered with the intention of drawing near to other than Allah. So it is known from this that non-flowing is not forbidden, and when that is established, it is established that it is not impure; because if it were impure it would be forbidden, for every impure thing is forbidden. And Ibrahim al-Halabi objected to it in 'al-Ghunya Sharh al-Munya' that the mentioned verse is Makki, and Surah al-Baqarah and al-Ma'idah are Madani, and in them is mentioned the prohibition of blood absolutely without the restriction of flowing, so why should the restriction not be abrogated by the absolute? And the answer about it: that absolute blood is carried upon flowing blood, as I detailed it in 'al-Sa'ayah'. If it is said: This is regarding what is eaten (its meat), but as for what is not eaten like humans, non-flowing is also forbidden, so it is not possible to deduce its purity from its permissibility. I say: When the prohibition of flowing was ruled, non-flowing remained on its original ruling, which is permissibility. [1] His statement: 'if it is said... etc.' This is an objection to the mentioned argument, and its clarification: that what the arguer mentioned that flowing is forbidden and non-flowing is not forbidden, thus it is not impure, is only for animals whose meat is eaten like sheep, cows, and others, so in them the argument from its permissibility to its purity is valid, because if it were impure it would be forbidden. But as for what is not eaten like humans, non-flowing is also forbidden, it is forbidden to eat it and benefit from it, so the mentioned argument does not work here, while the intended is only non-flowing blood exiting from a human body. [2] His statement: 'I say... etc.' Its summary: that the prohibition of flowing blood in the verse is absolute, so it is known from it that flowing blood whether in what is eaten or in other is forbidden, and from it follows the permissibility of non-flowing absolutely, and from it follows its being pure absolutely. [3] His statement: 'non-flowing remained on its original ruling'; indeed he did not say: 'it is known by it the permissibility of non-flowing' because specifying a matter with a description and judging it does not indicate the negation of the judgment from what is other than it according to us, as is known in books of Usul. So when the prohibition of blood restricted by flowing was ruled, only its prohibition for the original non-flowing blood is established, and not its permissibility; for it is silent about it in the text, its ruling is not known by it either by negation or affirmation, but the permissibility of non-flowing is known because it remains on its original ruling and no text prohibiting it has come. [4] His statement: 'which is permissibility'; with a kasra on the ha and shadda on the lam, he pointed out to that the original in things is permissibility and permission until evidence indicating non-permissibility comes, and this is the school of some Hanafis, among them al-Karkhi, and it is what he chose in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the purity of non-flowing blood in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: non-flowing blood (such as blood that does not flow from a wound) is not impure (najis). The proof is from the Quranic verse (Al-An'am 6:145) which only prohibits 'flowing blood' (dam masfuh). Since the ruling of prohibition is limited to flowing blood, non-flowing blood remains permissible and therefore pure, as every impure thing is forbidden. A Hanafi scholar, Ibrahim al-Halabi, objects that the verse is Makki whereas later Madani verses (e.g., Surah al-Baqarah and al-Ma'idah) mention blood absolutely without the restriction 'flowing', suggesting abrogation. The author responds that the absolute mention is interpreted as flowing blood, as detailed in his work 'al-Sa'ayah'. A second objection follows: the argument about non-flowing blood being permissible and thus pure only applies to edible animals (like sheep and cows). In the case of humans (whose flesh is not edible), even non-flowing blood is forbidden to eat, so the analogy fails. The author replies that the prohibition of flowing blood is general for all, and non-flowing blood remains on its original ruling of permissibility (ibaha) because no text prohibits it. The commentary then explains that the ruling is based on the Hanafi usul principle that specifying a thing with a description does not negate the ruling from other things; the permissibility of non-flowing blood is derived from its remaining on the original ruling (asl al-ibaha). This principle is attributed to some Hanafis like al-Karkhi and is adopted in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
دم مسفوح (dam masfuh): Blood that flows or runs, such as blood from a slaughtered animal that gushes out.
دم غير مسفوح (dam ghayr masfuh): Blood that does not flow, such as blood that remains on the surface of a small wound without running.
نجس (najis): Ritually impure substance that prevents the validity of prayer unless removed or avoided.
طاهر (tahir): Ritually pure substance that does not invalidate prayer or require removal.
أصل الإباحة (asl al-ibaha): The principle that the original ruling for all things is permissibility (ibaha) unless proven otherwise by a text or evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on blood that comes out of a wound but does not flow (e.g., a small cut that oozes but does not run)?
A) It is impure and breaks wudu.
B) It is pure and does not break wudu.
C) It is impure but does not break wudu.
D) It is pure but breaks wudu.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on non-flowing blood being pure is derived from the Quranic restriction of prohibition to flowing blood, supported by the usul principle of original permissibility.

The Purity of Non-Flowing Blood and the Principle of Original Permissibility
Commentary defending the purity of non-flowing blood and the principle of original permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis rule non-flowing blood pure and apply the principle of original permissibility (al-asl fi al-ashya' al-ibahah) to similar cases.

Core Idea
Non-flowing blood is pure because prohibition can be for honor, not impurity — the origin of things is permissibility.

This passage defends the Hanafi ruling that non-flowing blood (ghayr al-masfuh) is pure. An objection is raised: the proof only works for animals whose meat is eaten; human non-flowing blood is prohibited too, so how can it be pure? The commentator responds by distinguishing between prohibition for impurity and prohibition for honor, and by invoking the principle that things remain permissible unless evidence of prohibition arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponent argues that the proof (flowing blood prohibited → non-flowing not prohibited → pure) only applies to edible animals. For humans, non-flowing blood is also prohibited, so the argument fails.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The Quranic prohibition of flowing blood is absolute; it does not restrict to edible animals. Therefore, non-flowing blood is permissible absolutely. For humans, the prohibition of non-flowing blood is based on honor (karamah), not impurity.
3
Apply the principle of original permissibility
The origin of all things is permissibility. Since no text prohibits non-flowing blood, it remains permissible and thus pure.
This principle is supported by Quran 2:29 and 2:168.
4
Distinguish prohibition types
Prohibition can be for impurity (najasah) or for honor (karamah). Impurity-based prohibition implies impurity; honor-based prohibition does not. Human non-flowing blood is prohibited for honor, so it remains pure.

[٢] قوله : قلت . الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ حرمة الدم المسفوح في الآية مطلقة، فيعلم منه أن الدم المسفوح سواء كان فيما يؤكل أو في غيره حرام، ويلزم منه حلّ غير المسفوح مطلقاً، فيلزم منه كونه طاهراً مطلقاً. [٣] قوله : بقي غير المسفوح على أصله ؛ إنّما لم يقل : عُلِمَ به حل غير المسفوح ؛ لأن تخصيص أمر بوصف والحكم عليه لا يدلُّ على نفي الحكم عما عداه عندنا، كما عرف في كتب الأصول. فلما حكم بحرمة الدم المقيَّدِ بالمسفوح لم يثبت منه إلا حرمته لأصل الدم الغير المسفوح، وعدم حله ؛ فإنّه مسكوت عنه في النص، لا يعلم حكمه به لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً ، وإنما يعلم حلّ غير المسفوح لبقائه على أصله وعدم ورود نص يحرمه. [٤]قوله : وهو الحل ؛ بكسر الحاء المهملة وتشديد اللام أشار به إلى أن الأصل في الأشياء الحل والإباحة إلى أن يدل دليل على عدم الإباحة)، وهذا هو مذهب بعض الحنفية ومنهم الكرخي وهو الذي اختاره في «الهداية». ويلزم منه الطهارة، سواءٌ كان فيما يؤكل لحمه ، أو لا ؛ لإطلاق النص". ثم حرمة غير المسفوح في الآدمي بناءً على حرمة لحمه ، لا توجب نجاسته إذ هذه الحرمة ؛ للكرامة لا للنجاسة، فغير المسفوح في الآدمي يكون على ومنهم من قال : إنّ الأصل في الأشياء الحرمة. ومال جماعة إلى التوقف، والمختار هو الأول. [١]قوله : لإطلاق النصّ ؛ أي إنّما فَهِمَ هذا التعميم الذي أشار إليه بقوله : سواء كان ... الخ ؛ لكون النص القرآني الحاكم بحرمة الدم المسفوح غير مقيد بدم ما يؤكل لحمه ، والمطلق يجري على إطلاقه. [١] قوله : ثم حرمة ... الخ ؛ دفع دخل مقدر ، تقرير الدخل : أن نـص الـدم المسفوح وإن كان مطلقاً لكن لا شبهة في أنَّ دم الآدمي حرام مطلقاً مسفوحاً كان أو غير مسفوح ، فكما أن المسفوح منه نجس يكون غير المسفوح أيضاً نجساً. وتقرير الدفع أنّ حرمته على نحوين : حرمة بسبب النجاسة، كحرمة الخمر والخنزير، ومثل هذه تدلُّ على النجاسة. وحرمة بسبب الكرامة والشرافة، وهذه لا تستلزم النجاسة، وحرمة غير المسفوح في الآدمي مبني على حرمة لحمه ، وحرمة لحمه ليست للنجاسة، فإنّ الآدمي ليس (الحظر والإباحة). وقال في شرح التحرير»: وهو قول معتزلة البصرة وكثير من الشافعية وأكثر الحنفية لا سيما العراقيين. قالوا : وإليه أشار محمد فيمن هدد بالقتل على أكل الميتة أو شرب الخمر فلم يفعل حتى قتل بقوله : خفت أن يكون آثماً ؛ لأن أكل الميتة وشرب الخمر لم يحرما إلا بالنهي عنهما ، فجعل الإباحة أصلاً والحرمة بعارض النهي .اهـ. ونقل أيضا أنه قول أكثر أصحابنا وأصحاب الشافعي للشيخ أكمل الدين في شرح أصول البزدوي». ينظر : «رد المحتار» (۱): ۱۰۵ ، ٤ : ١٦١، ٦ : ٤٥٨)، وغيره. ويستنثى منها الفروج إذ الأصل فيها التحريم ، قال العلامة شيخ زاده له في مجمع الأنهر» (٢ : ٥٦٨ : واعلم أن الأصل في الأشياء كلها سوى الفروج الإباحة، قال : هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ لَكُم مَّا فِي الْأَرْضِ جَمِيعًا ﴾ [البقرة : من الآية ٢٩]، وقال : كُلُوا مِمَّا فِي الْأَرْضِ حَلَلًا طَيِّبًا ﴾ [البقرة: من الآية١٦٨]، وإنما تثبت الحرمة بعارض نص مطلق أو خبر مروي فما لم يوجد شيء من الدلائل المحرمة فهي على الإباحة».
If it is said: This applies to what is eaten for its meat; as for what is not eaten for its meat, like a human, non-flowing blood is also prohibited, so its permissibility cannot be used as evidence for its purity. I say: When the prohibition of flowing blood was ruled, non-flowing blood remained on its original ruling, which is permissibility. [1] His statement: 'If it is said... etc.': This is an objection to the mentioned proof. Its clarification: That what the one proving mentioned, that flowing blood is prohibited and non-flowing blood is not prohibited, thus it is not impure, only applies to animals whose meat is eaten, like sheep and cattle and others. In them, the proof by its permissibility for its purity is valid, for if it were impure, it would be prohibited. As for what is not eaten, like a human, non-flowing blood is also prohibited; its consumption and benefit are prohibited, so the mentioned proof does not apply here, while the intent is regarding non-flowing blood exiting from a human body. [2] His statement: 'I say... etc.': Its summary: That the prohibition of flowing blood in the verse is absolute, so it is known from it that flowing blood, whether in what is eaten or not, is prohibited, and it entails the permissibility of non-flowing blood absolutely, and it entails it being pure absolutely. [3] His statement: 'Non-flowing blood remained on its original ruling': He did not say 'it is known by it the permissibility of non-flowing blood' because specifying a matter with a description and ruling upon it does not indicate negation of the ruling for what is other than it according to us, as known in books of Usul. So when the prohibition of blood restricted by 'flowing' was ruled, only its prohibition is established for the original non-flowing blood, and its non-permissibility; for it is passed over in silence in the text; its ruling is not known from it, neither negating nor affirming. Rather, the permissibility of non-flowing blood is known because it remains on its original ruling and no text has come to prohibit it. [4] His statement: 'which is permissibility' (al-hill, with kasr of the ha and tashdid of the lam): He indicated by it that the origin regarding things is permissibility and allowance until evidence indicates lack of permissibility. This is the madhhab of some Hanafis, including al-Karkhi, and it is what he chose in al-Hidayah. And it entails purity, whether in what is eaten for its meat or not, due to the absoluteness of the text. Then the prohibition of non-flowing blood in a human, based on the prohibition of his flesh, does not necessitate its impurity, for this prohibition is for honor, not for impurity. So non-flowing blood in a human is... And among them is one who said: The origin regarding things is prohibition. And a group inclined to suspension (tawaqquf), and the chosen is the first. [1] His statement: 'due to the absoluteness of the text': i.e., he understood this generalization that he indicated by his saying 'whether... etc.' because the Qur'anic text that rules the prohibition of flowing blood is not restricted to blood of what is eaten for its meat, and the absolute proceeds upon its absoluteness. [1] His statement: 'Then the prohibition... etc.': Repelling an anticipated objection. The objection's formulation: That the text on flowing blood, though absolute, there is no doubt that human blood is absolutely prohibited, whether flowing or non-flowing, so just as flowing blood is impure, non-flowing blood is also impure. And the formulation of the repelling: That its prohibition is of two types: Prohibition due to impurity, like the prohibition of wine and swine, and this kind indicates impurity. And prohibition due to honor and dignity, and this does not necessitate impurity. The prohibition of non-flowing blood in a human is based on the prohibition of his flesh, and the prohibition of his flesh is not for impurity, for the human is not (prohibition and permissibility). And it was said in Sharh al-Tahrir: It is the saying of the Mu'tazila of Basra and many of the Shafi'is and most Hanafis, especially the Iraqis. They said: And Muhammad indicated towards it regarding one who was threatened with death for eating carrion or drinking wine and did not do so until he was killed, by his saying: 'I fear he would be sinful' because eating carrion and drinking wine became prohibited only by the prohibition upon them, so he made permissibility the origin and prohibition by an accidental prohibition. End. And it was also transmitted that it is the saying of most of our scholars and the scholars of al-Shafi'i by al-Shaykh Akmal al-Din in Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi. Look at Radd al-Muhtar (1:105, 4:161, 6:458) and others. And private parts are excepted from them, for the origin regarding them is prohibition. Al-Allamah Shaykh Zadah said in Majma' al-Anhur (2:568): And know that the origin regarding all things besides private parts is permissibility. He said: 'It is He who created for you all that is on the earth' [Quran 2:29], and He said: 'Eat of what is on the earth, lawful and good' [Quran 2:168]. And prohibition is established only by an accidental absolute text or a narrated report; as long as nothing of the prohibiting evidences exists, they are upon permissibility.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, addressing a legal objection regarding the purity of non-flowing blood. The objection states that the proof linking permissibility to purity only works for animals whose meat is eaten; for humans, non-flowing blood is also prohibited (due to the sanctity of human flesh), so the argument fails. The commentator, following the Hidayah, responds that the Quranic verse prohibiting flowing blood is absolute and not restricted to eatable animals. Therefore, by the principle 'what is not explicitly prohibited remains on its original permissibility', non-flowing blood is ruled pure generally. However, for humans, the prohibition of non-flowing blood is due to honor (karamah), not impurity (najasah). Thus, it does not imply impurity. The passage then discusses the Hanafi position on the principle of original permissibility (asl al-ibahah) as opposed to prohibition. The relied-upon view is that the origin of things is permissibility, except for private parts (furuj), citing Quranic verses and the authority of Muhammad al-Shaybani. The commentator refutes the potential counterargument that human blood must be impure because all human blood is prohibited, by distinguishing the basis of prohibition (impurity vs. honor).

Key Terms
الدم المسفوح (al-dam al-masfuh): Blood that flows in a stream, explicitly prohibited in Quran 6:145.
غير المسفوح (ghayr al-masfuh): Blood that does not flow, e.g., blood remaining in meat or veins after slaughter.
الأصل في الأشياء الإباحة (al-asl fi al-ashya' al-ibahah): The legal principle that things are inherently permissible until evidence indicates prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules non-flowing blood pure based on the principle of original permissibility and the distinction between impurity-based and honor-based prohibition.

Non-Flowing Human Blood: Pure but Prohibited
Explaining why non-flowing human blood is tahir despite being haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on non-flowing human blood and explain why its prohibition does not imply impurity.

Core Idea
Non-flowing human blood is tahir because its prohibition is for dignity, not impurity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya tackles a subtle point: although human blood is haram to consume, not all of it is impure. The Quran restricts the prohibition of blood to 'flowing blood' (dam masfuh). The commentator explains that non-flowing blood, having undergone digestion and become organ-like, retains its original purity. The prohibition of such blood in humans is based on human dignity, not impurity. This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school and carries practical implications for purity in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic proof
Locate the Quranic verse cited (Q 6:145) that prohibits only 'flowing blood'. Note that the wording implies non-flowing blood is not covered by the prohibition of impurity.
The verse is used as a primary dalil: 'or flowing blood' limits the najis category.
2
Understand the distinction based on digestion
Follow the explanation that non-flowing blood has moved from veins into organs, undergone further digestion, and taken on the nature of the organ. Flowing blood is fresh from veins and retains its impure status.
The five stages of digestion are detailed to show transformation.
3
Apply the principle of dignity-based prohibition
Recognize that human blood is prohibited out of honor (karama) for the human being, not because it is inherently impure. The prohibition does not entail impurity.
This is a key Hanafi distinction: not every haram is najis.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Note that the Shafi'i school considers all blood, flowing or not, to be impure. The Hanafi position rests on the literal Quranic text and the digestion-based reasoning.
Practical consequence: in Hanafi, prayer is not invalidated by traces of non-flowing blood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزم منه الطهارة، سواءً كان فيما يؤكل لحمه، أو لا؛ لإطلاق النص. ثم حرمة غير المسفوح في الآدمي بناءً على حرمة لحمه، لا توجب نجاسته إذ هذه الحرمة؛ للكرامة لا للنجاسة، فغير المسفوح في الآدمي يكون على طهارته الأصلية مع كونه محرماً. والفرق بين المسفوح وغير المسفوح مبني على حكمة غامضة، وهي: أن غير المسفوح دم انتقل عن العروق، وانفصل عن النجاسات، وحصل له هضم آخر في الأعضاء فصار مستعداً لأن يصير عضواً، فأخذ طبيعة العضو، فأعطاه الشرع حكمه بخلاف دم العروق، فإذا سال عن رأس الجرح علم أنه دم انتقل من العروق في هذه الساعة، وهو الدم النجس، أما إذا لم يسل علم أنه دم العضو، هذا في الدم. [قوله: وهي أن غير المسفوح... الخ] اعلم أن الغذاء له هضوم خمسة: الأول: هضمة في الفم بالمضغ بإعانة الريق الذي فيه حرارة غريزية. الثاني: هضمة في المعدة، فإنه إذا دخل الغذاء من الفم إليها انهضم هناك هضماً تاماً، وعند ذلك يحصل منه ومما يخالطه من المشروبات جوهر شبيه بالكشك الثخين، ثم ينجذب الغذاء المنهضم من المعدة، أما لطيفه فإلى الكبد، وهو عن يمين المعدة، وأما ثفله فإلى الأمعاء، وهو الخارج من المبرز. فإذا دخل لطيفه في الكبد انهضم هناك هضماً ثالثاً، فيكون ألطف من الأول، وتتكون منه هناك أخلاط أربعة: الدم والبلغم والصفراء والسوداء، وفضلة هذا الهضم يندفع أكثره بالبول ثم يجري منه الدم مختلطاً بالأخلاط الباقية بقدر الحاجة إلى العروق. وهناك ينهضم هضماً رابعاً، يتميز حينئذ لطيفه من كثيفه ثم ينفصل اللطيف من العروق، ويتصل بالأعضاء، فيأخذ كل عضو منه حظه. بنجس، بل لما كان مكرماً بين المخلوقات حرم الانتفاع بجميع أجزائه تشريفاً، فإن في إباحة الانتفاع به إهانة وتذليلاً. ومن هاهنا يعلم أنه ليس كل حرام نجساً، فإن الحرمة قد تكون للكرامة، بل قد تكون للخباثة والمضرة أيضاً مع عدم النجاسة: كحرمة الطين والذباب، وأما النجس فكله حرام.
And necessarily from it is purity, whether in what is eaten of its meat or not; due to the generality of the text. Then the prohibition of non-flowing (blood) in the human is based on the prohibition of his meat; it does not necessitate its impurity, because this prohibition is for dignity, not for impurity. So non-flowing blood in the human remains on its original purity while being prohibited. And the difference between flowing and non-flowing is based on a subtle wisdom: that non-flowing blood is blood that has moved from the veins, separated from impurities, and undergone another digestion in the organs, so it becomes ready to become an organ, thus takes the nature of the organ, and the Lawgiver gave it its ruling unlike the blood of the veins. So if it flows from the head of a wound, it is known that it is blood that has moved from the veins at that moment, and it is impure blood; but if it does not flow, it is known that it is the blood of the organ. This is regarding blood. [His saying: and that non-flowing... etc.] Know that food has five digestions: First: digestion in the mouth by chewing with the help of saliva which has innate heat. Second: digestion in the stomach; when food enters from the mouth into it, it digests completely, and then from it and from the drinks mixed with it, a substance resembling thick kishk is obtained. Then the digested food is drawn from the stomach; its subtle part to the liver, which is on the right of the stomach; and its thick part to the intestines, which is what exits from the anus. When its subtle part enters the liver, it digests there a third digestion, becoming subtler than the first, and from it four humors are formed: blood, phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile. The waste of this digestion is mostly expelled through urine. Then blood mixed with the remaining humors runs from it as needed to the veins. There it digests a fourth digestion, where its subtle and thick parts are distinguished, then the subtle separates from the veins and connects to the organs, and each organ takes its share. Impure, but because he (the human) is honored among creatures, benefiting from all his parts is prohibited as an honor; for permitting benefit would be degradation and humiliation. From here it is known that not every prohibited thing is impure, for prohibition may be for dignity, and may be for foulness and harm also without impurity: like the prohibition of clay and flies. As for the impure, all of it is prohibited.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling of non-flowing blood in humans. It begins by affirming that the ruling of purity applies to both edible and non-edible animals due to the unqualified Quranic text. Then it addresses an objection about human blood: although it is prohibited, its prohibition is based on the dignity of humans, not on impurity. Therefore, non-flowing blood in humans remains pure, though it is haram to consume. The distinction between flowing (masfuh) and non-flowing blood is based on a subtle wisdom explained through digestion: non-flowing blood has been further processed in the organs and takes on the nature of the organ, whereas flowing blood is from the veins and is impure. The commentary then details the five stages of digestion to show how blood becomes organ-like. Finally, it generalizes: not every haram is najis; prohibition can be for dignity (like human blood) or harm (like clay or flies) without impurity, but everything najis is haram. This is a key Hanafi principle that separates the concepts of impurity and prohibition.

Key Terms
دم مسفوح (dam masfuh): Blood that flows out of the body from veins or wounds, considered impure.
دم غير مسفوح (dam ghayr masfuh): Blood that does not flow, such as that remaining in meat after slaughter, considered pure in Hanafi school.
حرمة الكرامة (hurmat al-karama): Prohibition based on dignity and honor, not on impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because its prohibition is for dignity, not impurity.
C) Because it is not from a living human.
D) Because it is found in halal animals.

Key Takeaway
Non-flowing human blood is tahir because its prohibition is based on human dignity, not impurity, a distinction unique to the Hanafi school.

The Purity of Non-Flowing Blood (Ghayr Masfuḥ)
Explaining the distinction between flowing and non-flowing blood regarding purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on non-flowing blood and explain the rational proof (digestion) that distinguishes it from flowing blood.

Core Idea
Non-flowing blood is pure because it has been digested and taken the nature of an organ.

Blood that flows from a wound is impure, but what about blood that remains in the liver or spleen after slaughter? This lesson unpacks the subtle wisdom behind the Hanafi distinction: the blood that does not flow has undergone a complete digestion, becoming organ-like, and thus takes the ruling of the organ itself. This rational proof, grounded in the five stages of digestion, reveals why not all blood is impure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on non-flowing blood
Start by stating the explicit Hanafi position: non-flowing blood (e.g., in liver or spleen) is pure (tahir), even though it may be prohibited to consume from an unlawfully slaughtered animal.
The passage opens with 'Its original purity while being prohibited' – purity is the default.
2
Understand the basis for the distinction
The distinction between flowing and non-flowing blood is based on a 'subtle wisdom' (hikma ghamida): non-flowing blood has separated from the veins, left impurities behind, and undergone an additional digestion in the organs.
Flowing blood comes directly from the veins and carries impurities; non-flowing blood is like the organ itself.
3
Follow the digestion argument (five stages)
The author details five stages of digestion: mouth, stomach, liver, veins, and final integration into organs. Non-flowing blood has completed all stages, including the final 'organ digestion' where it loses its blood nature and becomes part of the organ.
This rational proof shows that non-flowing blood is transformed and no longer retains its original impurity.
4
Connect digestion to the legal ruling
Because non-flowing blood has taken on the nature of the organ, it is given the same ruling as the organ – pure. Flowing blood has not undergone this transformation and remains impure.
The ruling is based on change of essence (istihala).
5
Note the broader principle: not everything haram is najis
The passage emphasizes that purity and permissibility are separate. Even if a substance is pure, it may be prohibited to consume due to an external reason (e.g., honor of the human body or harm).
This refutes a common misconception that all prohibited substances are impure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وهناك ينهضم هضماً خامساً حتى تستحيل صورته الدموية إلى الصور العضوية، فيلتصق به التصاقاً تاماً، وتفصيل هذه المباحث في الكتب الطبية. إذا عرفت هذا فحاصلُ الحكمة التي ذكرها الشارحُ : أن الدم السائل إنّما هو دمُ العروق، وهو ملتصق بالنجاسات فيكون نجساً لا محالة.

Its original purity while being prohibited. And the distinction between flowing and non-flowing blood is based on a subtle wisdom: that non-flowing blood has transferred from the veins, separated from impurities, and undergone another digestion in the organs, becoming ready to become a member, so it took the nature of the member, thus the Law gave it its ruling, unlike vein blood. So if it flows from the head of a wound, it is known that it is blood that has transferred from the veins at that moment, and it is impure blood. But if it does not flow, it is known that it is the blood of the organ. This regarding blood. ... (the rest of the long explanation) ... This is the secret: that flowing blood is impure and non-flowing is pure.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn statement 'Its original purity while being prohibited.' It addresses a subtle point: blood that does not flow (like liver or spleen blood) is pure despite coming from a prohibited animal. The wisdom is that such blood has undergone complete digestion and taken on the nature of the organ, so it is treated like the organ itself. The author then explains the five stages of digestion to support this ruling, concluding that flowing blood is from the veins and impure, while non-flowing blood is from the organs and pure. A side point: not everything haram is najis; purity and prohibition are separate issues.

Key Terms
دم مسفوح (dam masfūḥ): Blood that flows from veins when cut; considered impure in Hanafi fiqh.
دم غير مسفوح (dam ghayr masfūḥ): Blood that does not flow, like blood remaining in the liver or spleen after slaughter; considered pure in Hanafi fiqh.
هضم (haḍm): Digestion; the process by which food is broken down in the body, transforming blood into organ tissue.

Non-flowing blood is considered pure in Hanafi fiqh due to its complete digestion and transformation into organ tissue, even though it may be prohibited to consume from a haram animal.

Vomiting and Sleep: Subtle Nullifiers of Wudu
Clarifying the ruling on small vomit and sleep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a small amount of vomited water and on sleeping while lying down, and identify the hadith proof for sleep as a nullifier.

Core Idea
A small amount of pure vomited water does not break wudu, but sleep while lying down does.

This passage addresses two specific cases that often cause confusion in taharah: vomiting a small amount of pure water, and sleeping while lying down. The text clarifies that pure water from the upper stomach is like saliva and does not nullify wudu, while sleep in a reclining position nullifies it based on prophetic hadith. We will walk through the reasoning and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts and their rulings
Determine what the passage says about vomiting a small amount of water and about sleeping lying down. Note that the passage gives a ruling for each.
2
Analyze the ruling on vomiting
Understand why a small amount of water from the upper stomach is ruled like saliva. Consider the definition of 'small' and the significance of it being water, not mixed with impurities.
The passage explains that the upper stomach is not a place of impurity, so the water is pure.
3
Examine the objections and responses
The text records an objection: why specify water when other vomit can also be small? Study the three responses: (1) the passage aims to clarify a subtype of vomit, (2) water is a precursor to all vomit, and (3) it refutes a specific opinion (Hasan ibn Ziyad).
This shows scholarly discourse (ikhtilaf) even within the Hanafi school.
4
Turn to sleep: identify the proof
The passage states that the basis for the ruling on sleep is the hadith: 'The eyes are the drawstring of the anus, so whoever sleeps should perform wudu.' Analyze how this hadith indicates that sleep breaks wudu.
The hadith uses a metaphor; recognize the legal implication (amr -> wujub).
5
Apply the hadith to lying-down sleep
Understand why the ruling specifically mentions 'lying down': because in that position, the body is relaxed and control is lost, unlike sitting firmly. This is the mufta bihi position.
Sleep while sitting with the buttocks planted does not break wudu according to the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : الماء الذي كان ... الخ ؛ أوردَ عليه : أنّ القليل لا يختص بالماء، فإنّه هو قد يكون فيء الطعام والمرّة السوداء والبلغم أيضاً قليلاً لا يملأ الفم، فالتخصيص بالماء ليس في محله.
وأجيب عنه بوجوه :
الأول: إن معنى كلامه القليل من الماء هو الماء... الخ، والغرض منه بيان نوع القليل من قيء الماء لا من غيره.
الثاني : أنه ذكر بعضُهم أنّ الماء مقدّمة لكلِّ نوع من أنواع القيء ؛ فلذا أخذ الماء في تفسير القليل.
الثالث : إنما خص الماء بالذكرِ ردّاً لقول الحسن بن زياد له من أنه لا ينقض قيء الشارب عقيب شربه قبل المخالطة.

حديث: «وكاء الله العينان، فمن نام فليتوضأ ) ، أخرجه أبو داود وأحمد بسند حسن.
As for vomiting, the small amount is the water that was in the upper stomach, which is not a place of impurity, so its ruling is the ruling of saliva. He said: 'the water that was…' etc.; an objection was raised: that the small amount is not exclusive to water, for it may also be a small amount of food, black bile, and phlegm that does not fill the mouth, so the specification of water is not correct. And it was answered in several ways: First: the meaning of his statement 'the small amount of water' is water, and the purpose is to clarify the type of small vomit of water, not other types. Second: some mentioned that water is a precursor to every type of vomit, so water was taken in the interpretation of 'small amount'. Third: he specifically mentioned water in response to the statement of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad that the vomit of a drinker immediately after drinking before mixing does not break wudu. He said: 'and sleeping lying down'; the basis in this chapter is the hadith: 'The eyes are the drawstring of the anus, so whoever sleeps should perform wudu', narrated by Abu Dawud and Ahmad with a hasan chain.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage covers two matters that break wudu. First, vomiting: if someone vomits a small amount of pure water from the upper stomach, it does not break wudu because it is like saliva. The commentary then defends why the text specifically says 'water', giving three reasons: (1) the passage is discussing a subtype of vomit (water-vomit), (2) water is always present in any vomit, and (3) it refutes a view of Hasan ibn Ziyad that vomiting immediately after drinking does not break wudu. Second, sleep: sleeping lying down breaks wudu based on the hadith that sleep relaxes the sphincter muscles, potentially releasing wind. The hadith is cited as the primary evidence.

Key Terms
قيء (qay'): Vomiting; the expulsion of stomach contents through the mouth. In this context, the ruling depends on the amount and nature of what is expelled.
قليل (qalīl): A small amount; in vomiting, it refers to less than a mouthful, specifically water from the upper stomach.
نوم مضطجع (nawm muḍṭajiʿ): Sleep in a lying-down position; considered a nullifier of wudu because it relaxes the body and may lead to the release of wind.
وكاء (wikā'): Drawstring; used in the hadith metaphorically: the eyes are the drawstring of the anus, meaning that when awake, the anal muscles are controlled, but when asleep, they may relax.

A small amount of pure vomited water does not break wudu, while sleep lying down does, based on the hadith that sleep relaxes bodily control.

Vomit and Wudu: The Threshold of a Mouthful
Discussion of vomit's effect on wudu and definition of 'little'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vomit's effect on wudu and distinguish between a mouthful and less than a mouthful.

Core Idea
A mouthful of vomit breaks wudu; less than a mouthful does not.

The Hanafi school makes a precise distinction in the effect of vomiting on wudu. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains what constitutes 'little' vomit (water from the upper stomach, similar to saliva) and why a larger amount nullifies purity. It also addresses an objection about restricting 'little' to water and refutes an exceptional view attributed to Hasan ibn Ziyad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of vomit
Note that the text classifies vomit into 'little' (less than a mouthful) and, implicitly, 'much' (a mouthful or more). The 'little' is described as water from the upper stomach, which has not yet become impure, so its ruling resembles that of saliva.
The stomach has two parts: upper (near the esophagus, where water collects) and lower (where digestion and impurities occur).
2
Understand the ruling for 'little' vomit
The passage states that 'little' vomit has the same ruling as saliva – it does not nullify wudu. This is because it comes from a region not associated with impurity and has undergone only initial digestion.
Saliva does not break wudu by consensus.
3
Trace the objection about the definition of 'little'
An objector points out that 'little' vomit can include food, black bile, or phlegm, not just water, so specifying water seems arbitrary.
The objection challenges the commentator's initial definition.
4
Review the three answers to the objection
First, the commentator meant 'the little kind of water vomit' not all little vomit. Second, water is a precursor to all vomit (it helps expel other substances). Third, specifying water refutes the view of Hasan ibn Ziyad (who held that water vomit immediately after drinking does not nullify).
The third answer shows that the Shafi'i-like exception is rejected in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the general Hanafi rule
Regardless of the content, the Hanafi position is clear: any substance – water, food, bile, blood – if expelled in a mouthful or more, nullifies wudu. Less than a mouthful does not.
This is the muftā bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله : الماء الذي كان ... الخ ؛ أوردَ عليه : أنّ القليل لا يختص بالماء، فإنّه هو قد يكون فيء الطعام والمرّة السوداء والبلغم أيضاً قليلاً لا يملأ الفم، فالتخصيص بالماء ليس في محله. وأجيب عنه بوجوه : الأول: إن معنى كلامه القليل من الماء هو الماء... الخ، والغرض منه بيان نوع القليل من قيء الماء لا من غيره. الثاني : أنه ذكر بعضُهم أنّ الماء مقدّمة لكلِّ نوع من أنواع القيء ؛ فلذا أخذ الماء في تفسير القليل. الثالث : إنما خص الماء بالذكرِ ردّاً لقول الحسن بن زياد له من أنه لا ينقض قيء الشارب عقيب شربه قبل المخالطة.
As for vomiting, the little is water that was at the top of the stomach, and it is not a place of impurity, so its ruling is the ruling of saliva. And there it is digested a fifth digestion until its blood-like form transforms into organic form, and it attaches completely. Details of these matters are in medical books. If you know this, then the summary of the wisdom that the commentator mentioned is: that flowing blood is blood of the veins, and it is attached to impurities, so it is inevitably impure. As for non-flowing blood, it is blood that has been digested by venous digestion and separated from the veins, and moved away from impurities, and connected, and another digestion occurred by which it became ready to leave its form and take on organic form. When this is the case, the Lawgiver gave it the ruling of the limb. This is the secret of flowing being impure and non-flowing being pure. [1] His statement: 'water that was...' etc. An objection was raised: that little is not specific to water, because it may be vomit of food, black bile, and phlegm also in a little amount that does not fill the mouth, so specifying water is not appropriate. It was answered with several points: First: The meaning of his statement 'little of water' is water... etc., and the purpose is to clarify the type of little from water vomit, not from others. Second: Some mentioned that water is a precursor to every type of vomit, so water was taken in the interpretation of 'little'. Third: He specified water specifically to refute the statement of Hasan ibn Ziyad that the vomit of a drinker immediately after drinking before mixing does not nullify.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on vomiting as a nullifier of wudu. It begins by explaining that 'little' vomit refers to water from the upper stomach, which is not a place where impurities gather, so its ruling is like that of saliva—it does not break wudu. The commentator then explains the wisdom behind the impurity of flowing blood and purity of non-flowing blood: flowing blood is still in the veins and attached to impurities, whereas non-flowing blood has undergone digestion and separated from impurities, becoming akin to the limb itself. An objection is raised that 'little' should not be restricted to water, as vomit can also include food, bile, or phlegm in small amounts. The commentator provides three answers: first, the intended meaning is to specify the type of 'little' that is water vomit; second, water is a precursor to all types of vomit; third, the specification refutes Hasan ibn Ziyad's view that water vomit immediately after drinking does not nullify wudu. The Hanafi ruling is that any vomit filling the mouth breaks wudu, regardless of content; less than a mouthful does not. Hasan ibn Ziyad's view that water vomit before mixing does not break wudu is rejected.

Key Terms
القيء (al-qay'): Vomit; the act of expelling stomach contents through the mouth.
القليل (al-qalīl): A small amount of vomit, specifically water from the upper stomach that does not fill the mouth.
الماء (al-mā'): Water; in this context, the water present in the upper stomach before mixing with food.

C) His wudu is nullified only if the water is mixed with food.
D) His wudu is invalidated because water is pure.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a mouthful of vomit nullifies wudu, while less than a mouthful does not – a distinction grounded in the volume expelled, not the substance.

Sleep as a Nullifier of Wudu: The Lying Down Condition
Establishes that sleep only nullifies wudu when lying down due to joint relaxation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sleep as a nullifier of wudu and explain why only lying down sleep nullifies wudu based on the hadith.

Core Idea
Sleep nullifies wudu only when lying down because joint relaxation makes it a likely occasion for wind.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes one of the key distinctions of the Hanafi school regarding the nullifiers of wudu. While many Muslims assume any sleep breaks wudu, the Hanafi position relies on a careful analysis of prophetic hadith to restrict nullification to a specific posture. Let us examine the proofs and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and what the ruling is. Here, the act is 'sleep in a lying down posture' and the ruling is that it nullifies wudu (naqid).
The ruling is not about sleep per se but about a specific posture of sleep.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the proofs cited: three hadith that link sleep to wudu and specify the lying down posture. The first two hadith establish that sleep generally may require wudu; the third restricts it to lying down.
The third hadith explicitly mentions 'lying down' (mudtaji') and joint relaxation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the proofs indicate the ruling. The third hadith's wording 'innama al-wudu'' restricts wudu to lying down sleep. The mention of joint relaxation (istirkha' al-mafasil) provides the operative cause ('illa).
The imperative in the first hadith ('falyatawadda'') indicates obligation (wujub) of wudu upon sleeping, but the third hadith limits this to lying down.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that there is intra-Hanafi disagreement: Hasan ibn Ziyad held that sleep never nullifies wudu. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that lying down sleep nullifies. The text supports the majority with the hadith.
The cross-madhhab position of the Shafi'is (any deep sleep nullifies) is not discussed in the source text but is a common comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢) قوله : ونوم مضطجع؛ الأصل في هذا الباب : ۱. حديث: «وكاء الله العينان، فمن نام فليتوضأ ) ، أخرجه أبو داود وأحمد بسند حسن. وحديث : العين وكـاء السَّه – أي الدبر – فإذا نامتِ العين استطلق الوكاء» ، أخرجه الطبراني والدارمي. .. وحديث : «إنما الوضوء على من نام مضطجعاً، فإنه إذا نام استرخت مفاصله ) ، أخرجه الترمذي والدارقطني والبيهقي بألفاظ متقاربة، وفي الباب أحاديث أخر ) بسطناها في «السعاية»، ويعلم من هذه الأخبار أن كل نوم يسترخي فيه المفاصل ناقض، وإن نقضه ليس لذاته، بل لكونه مظنة خروج الحدث، فينتقض النوم على هيئة يكون فيها استرخاء المفاصل لا غير. [1]قوله : مضطجع ؛ هو أن ينام واضعاً جنبه على الأرض.
[2] His statement: 'And sleep lying down'; the basis in this chapter: 1. Hadith: 'The eyes are the drawstring of Allah, so whoever sleeps let him perform wudu.' Recorded by Abu Dawud and Ahmad with a good chain. And hadith: 'The eye is the drawstring of the anus, so when the eye sleeps the drawstring is released.' Recorded by al-Tabarani and al-Darimi. ... And hadith: 'Wudu is only upon one who sleeps lying down, for when he sleeps his joints relax.' Recorded by al-Tirmidhi, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bayhaqi with similar wordings. There are other hadith on the topic which we have expanded in al-Si'ayah. It is known from these reports that every sleep in which the joints relax is a nullifier, and its nullification is not intrinsic but because it is a likely occurrence of exiting of hadath. So sleep nullifies only in a posture where there is relaxation of the joints. [1] His statement: 'lying down' means he sleeps placing his side on the ground.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage deals with sleep as a nullifier of wudu. It begins by stating the basic principle: the basis for this ruling is a series of hadith. The first hadith states that the eyes are the drawstring (control) of the anus, and when one sleeps, wudu is required. The second hadith says that the eye is the drawstring of the anus, so when the eye sleeps, the drawstring is loosened, implying that sleep may lead to the release of wind. The third hadith explicitly states that wudu is only required for one who sleeps lying down, because when he sleeps his joints relax. The commentator notes that there are other hadith on this topic. From these reports, the ruling is derived: any sleep in which the joints relax nullifies wudu. The nullification is not because sleep itself is an impurity, but because it is a likely time for the exit of hadath (wind). Therefore, sleep only nullifies wudu when it is in a position that causes relaxation of the joints, i.e., lying down. The commentator then defines 'mudtaji'' as one who sleeps placing his side on the ground. According to the Hanafi school, sleep while sitting, kneeling, or standing does not nullify wudu because the joints remain tight and there is less likelihood of passing wind. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, based on the Zahir al-Riwayah. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Hasan ibn Ziyad held that sleep never nullifies wudu, but this is not followed. The passage implicitly refutes that view by citing the hadith.

Key Terms
النوم (al-nawm): Sleep; a state of rest where senses are inactive. In this context, it is discussed as a nullifier of wudu.
مضطجع (mudtaji'): Lying down on one's side; the posture that causes joint relaxation and likelihood of passing wind.
استرخاء المفاصل (istirkhā' al-mafāṣil): Relaxation of the joints; the operative cause ('illa) for nullification of wudu during sleep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in the text, which type of sleep nullifies wudu?
A) Any sleep, regardless of posture.
B) Sleep while sitting, because the person may lean.
C) Sleep while lying down on one's side.
D) Sleep while standing, as it is uncommon.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, sleep nullifies wudu only when the person lies down because this posture relaxes the joints and makes it likely for wind to escape, as per the prophetic hadith.

The Nullification of Wudu by Different Sleep Positions
Explanation of which sleep positions nullify wudu and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to: (1) state the Hanafi ruling on which sleep positions nullify wudu, (2) cite the two hadiths that form the primary evidence, and (3) explain the effective cause (illa) of relaxation of joints and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mustanid.

Core Idea
Only sleep that relaxes joints nullifies wudu; standing, sitting, and prostration do not.

Sleep is a well-known nullifier of wudu, but the Hanafi school does not consider all sleep equal. In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the author explains that wudu is only required when sleep reaches a depth where the joints relax, typically when lying down. Two prophetic hadiths establish this rule, and the commentary further clarifies the categories of sleep — from lying on the side to leaning on a support — along with a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement on the latter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relevant acts and rulings
List the different sleep positions mentioned (mudtaji', muttaki', mustanid, standing, sitting, etc.) and state which nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school.
Focus on the principle that only sleep with relaxation of joints nullifies.
2
Examine the primary evidence
The passage cites two hadiths. Identify each hadith, its narrator, and the key phrase that supports the ruling.
The first hadith uses the metaphor of the eye as a tie; the second specifically mentions mudtaji' and joint relaxation.
3
Determine the effective cause (illa)
The passage states that nullification is not due to sleep itself but to 'relaxation of joints' (istirkha' al-mafasil). Explain why this is the operative cause and how it is derived from the hadith.
This illa distinguishes Hanafi ruling from other schools.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on mustanid
The footnote details disagreement among Hanafi scholars on sleeping while leaning (mustanid). Summarize both positions, the evidence each side uses, and the condition that resolves the dispute (buttocks raised or not).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the mufta bihi is context-dependent.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Given a description of a sleep posture, determine whether it nullifies wudu based on the principle of joint relaxation. Use the condition for mustanid if relevant.
Consider modern contexts like sleeping on a bus or in a reclining chair.

Lying on side and leaning. And the hadith: 'The eye is the tie of the anus – so when the eye sleeps, the tie becomes loose' narrated by Tabarani and Darimi. And the hadith: 'Wudu is only required from one who sleeps lying on his side, because when he sleeps his joints relax' narrated by Tirmidhi, Daraqutni, and Bayhaqi with similar wordings, and there are other ahadith on the topic which we have expanded in 'al-Si'ayah'. It is known from these reports that every sleep in which joints relax is a nullifier, and its nullification is not due to its essence, but because it is a likely occasion for the emission of an event (hadath). So sleep nullifies only in a position that involves relaxation of joints. [1] His statement: 'lying on side' means he sleeps placing his side on the ground. [PAGE_333] 'And leaning on something such that if removed he would fall, nothing else' means: wudu is not nullified by sleep other than what is mentioned, i.e., sleep while standing, sitting, bowing, or prostrating, whether in prayer or otherwise. [1] His statement: 'nothing else': if he said 'and sleep of one whose joints relax, nothing else' it would be more appropriate, even more correct, because two objections arise against the chosen expression: first, sleep on one's back or face-down, or sitting in the posture of one defecating, and other positions involving relaxation are nullifiers; second, 'leaning on something such that if removed he would fall' is a type of leaning, and it is general, including leaning on the elbow and other things, so mentioning 'leaning' after 'leaning' is unnecessary, unless it is said that the intended meaning of 'leaning' is 'squatting' (al-mutawarrik), as chosen by many commentators of al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'or prostrating': the hadith: 'There is no wudu upon one who sleeps prostrating until he lies on his side' narrated by Ahmad in his Musnad; and the hadith: 'Wudu is not obligatory upon one who sleeps sitting, standing, or prostrating until he places his side, because when he lies down his joints relax' narrated by Bayhaqi, and Ibn al-Humam has declared its chain good due to multiple paths. In the absolute wording there is evidence that sleep in these postures does not nullify, whether in prayer or otherwise. (1) Meaning: if that thing (the support) is removed, the sleeper would fall. And there is disagreement on this: some hold it does not nullify, like the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:95), and it was authenticated by the author of al-Bada'i' (1:31), who said: 'and this is the view of the majority of scholars', and authenticated by al-Zayla'i in al-Tabyin (1:10), who said: 'Abu Yusuf narrated it from Abu Hanifa'; and others chose that it is a nullifier, like the author of al-Wiqayah, its commentator in al-Nuqayah, al-Halabi in Multaqa al-Abhur, al-Tahawi in his Mukhtasar, al-Quduri in his Mukhtasar, the author of al-Hidayah, the author of al-Ikhtiyar, and the author of al-Muhit. This is when his buttocks are not raised from the ground; otherwise, it nullifies by agreement. See Radd al-Muhtar (1:96).
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary explaining the ruling of sleep as a nullifier of wudu. The author begins by quoting two hadiths: one about the eye being the tie of the anus, and another that wudu is only required for one who sleeps lying on his side. From these, the principle is derived that sleep nullifies wudu only when it involves relaxation of joints, because that is a likely occasion for the emission of wind (hadath). The nullification is not due to sleep itself but to the high probability of breath escaping in that state. Then the author defines 'lying on side' (mudtaji') as sleeping with one's side on the ground. On the next page, he turns to the phrase 'and leaning on something such that if removed he would fall' (mustanid). The rule is that only sleep with relaxation of joints nullifies; thus sleep while standing, sitting, bowing, or prostrating does not nullify. The commentator then critiques the author's wording, noting that omitting 'sleep of one whose joints relax' leads to two problems: first, it fails to include other positions that also involve relaxation (like lying on back or face-down); second, the mention of 'leaning' (istinad) after 'leaning' (itika') is redundant, as mustanid is a type of muttaki. Some scholars interpret muttaki as 'squatting' (mutawarrik) to avoid redundancy. Next, the author addresses sleep in prostration, citing hadiths that explicitly exclude it from nullification. Finally, a lengthy footnote details the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling for one who sleeps leaning on something (mustanid). Some authorities like the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar deem it non-nullifying, considering it like sitting, while others including the author of al-Wiqayah and many jurists consider it nullifying. The decisive factor is whether the buttocks are raised from the ground; if they are, the position resembles sitting and does not nullify, otherwise it does nullify by agreement. This reflects the Hanafi commitment to the principle of 'relaxation of joints' as the effective cause.

Key Terms
مضطجع (muḍṭajiʿ): Sleeping on one's side with the body resting on the ground.
متكئ (muttakiʾ): Leaning on something while sleeping, such as a pillow or wall, without fully lying down.
مستند (mustanid): Sleeping while supported by something such that if the support is removed, the person would fall.
استرخاء المفاصل (istirkhāʾ al-mafāṣil): Relaxation of joints; the state when the body's joints loosen during deep sleep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes sleep that nullifies wudu by the criterion of joint relaxation, not by the fact of sleep itself, leading to detailed rulings on different postures.

The Hanafi Condition for Sleep Nullifying Wudu: Joint Relaxation (Istirkha')
Commentary on sleep postures and unconscious states nullifying ablution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for sleep nullifying wudu, apply the condition to different postures, and identify the relied-upon position on sleep in prostration among the five intra-Hanafi opinions.

Core Idea
Sleep only nullifies wudu when the joints relax — posture matters, not sleep itself.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on the Wiqaya's concise ruling on sleep and nullification. It clarifies that the key is not the act of sleeping but the state of the body: relaxation of joints (istirkha'). The author critiques the matn's phrasing and cites two hadiths as evidence. Then it delves into a rich intra-Hanafi debate about sleeping specifically in prostration, showing the depth of scholarly analysis. Finally, it distinguishes sleep from fainting and insanity, which always nullify. Let's walk through the proof and the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for nullification
What is the defining criterion for sleep to nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school? The passage insists on 'joint relaxation' (istirkha'). Find where this is stated.
The author criticizes the matn's phrase 'without exception' and says it should specify 'sleep with relaxation'.
2
Locate the hadith evidence
Two hadiths are cited: one from Ahmad and one from al-Bayhaqi. Identify the key phrase that exempts certain postures from nullification.
The hadiths mention 'until he lies down' (yadṭajiʿ) as the point when relaxation occurs.
3
Apply the condition to postures
The passage lists standing, sitting, bowing, and prostrating as postures where sleep does not nullify. Explain why each of these typically lacks joint relaxation.
In prostration, the body is in a controlled position; relaxation is minimal.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
List the five opinions on sleep in prostration. Which one is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) and why?
The first opinion (never nullifies) is the apparent position of the school.
5
Contrast with loss of consciousness
Why do fainting (ighmā') and insanity (junūn) always nullify wudu regardless of posture?
Because they involve total loss of control, unlike sleep where a person can be roused easily.

الطهارة الوضوء
ومستند إلى ما لو أزيل لسقط لا غير
ومستند إلى ما لو أزيل لسقط (۱) لا غير ) : أي لا ينقض الوضوء نوم غير ما ذكر، وهو النوم قائماً ، أو قاعداً ، أو راكعاً ، أو ساجداً في الصلاة وغيرها "
[١] قوله : لا غير؛ لو قال: ونوم المسترخي لا غير لكان أولى، بل أصوب، فإنّه يرد على عبارة اختارها إيرادان :
الأول أن نوم المستلقي على قفاه والمكبّ، وقاعداً على هيئة المتغوط وغير ذلك مما يكون فيه الاسترخاء ناقض
الثاني : إن الاستناد إلى ما لو أزيل سقط من صور الاتكاء، فإنّه عام يشمل الاتكاء على المرفق وغيره ، فَذِكرُ الاستناد بعد الاتكاء غير محتاج إليه، إلا أن يقال المراد بالمتكئ : المتورّك، كما اختاره كثير من شراح «الهداية».

أخذ
وقد وقع لأصحابنا في النوم ساجداً اختلاف على أقوال خمسة :
الأول : إنّه غير ناقض مطلقاً، وهو ظاهر المذهب على ما في «الخلاصة»(۱).
الثاني : إنّه إن تعمد النوم في الصلاة فهو حدث، وإلا فلا، وهو المروي عن أبي يوسف الله
الثالث : إنه حدث خارج الصلاة غير حدث فيها ، واختاره صاحب «المنية»(۳).
الرابع : إنّه ليس بحدث إذا كان على الهيئة المسنونة في الصلاة كان أو خارجها، وإن كان خارجها لا عليها فهو حدث، وكذا في الصلاة، واختاره الحلبي في «شرح المنية الصغير»، والشرنبلالي.
الخامس : إنه ليس بحدث في الصلاة مطلقاً، وخارج الصلاة إن كان على الهيئة المسنونة، وإليه مال الزيلعي).
[١]قوله : والإغماء ؛ هو ضرب من المرض يضعف القوى ولا يزيل العقل بل يستره ، بخلاف الجنون فإنّه يزيله، وهما كالنوم في فوتِ الاختيار، وفوت استعمال القدرة، بل أشدّ، فإنّ النائم إذا نبّه انتبه، بخلافِ المغمى عليه والمجنون ؛ فلذلك كان كل منهما حدثاً في جميع الأحوال، سواء كان مضطجعاً أو قاعداً أو ساجداً، بخلاف النوم، فإنّه لا يكون إلا إذا استرخت مفاصله. كذا في «البحر الرائق»).
[٢]قوله : ويدخل ... الخ ؛ وكذا يدخلُ فيه الصرع، وهو ما يكون بسبب مس الشياطين والجن) ، فإنّ المصروعَ إذا أفاقَ من صرعِهِ يجب عليه الوضوء . كما في
Purity: Ablution. And (sleep) leaning on something that if removed would cause (the sleeper) to fall, without exception. That is, sleep other than what was mentioned does not break ablution: sleep while standing, sitting, bowing, or prostrating, whether in prayer or otherwise. His saying: 'without exception' — if he had said: 'and sleep of one whose joints are relaxed without exception' it would have been better, indeed more correct, for two objections arise against the chosen expression: First, sleep while lying on the back, face down, or sitting in the posture of one relieving oneself, and other such cases where relaxation occurs, is a nullifier. Second, 'leaning on something that if removed would cause falling' includes forms of leaning, it is general and includes leaning on the elbow and other things, so mentioning 'leaning' after 'reclining' is unnecessary, unless it is said that what is meant by 'reclining' is 'sitting with the weight on one side', as chosen by many commentators of al-Hidayah. His saying: 'or prostrating' — the Hadith: 'There is no ablution upon one who sleeps in prostration until he lies down on his side' narrated by Ahmad in his Musnad, and the Hadith: 'Ablution is not obligatory upon one who sleeps sitting, standing, or prostrating until he places his side, for when he lies down his joints relax' narrated by al-Bayhaqi, and Ibn al-Humam considered its chain good due to multiple paths. And the absolute wording indicates that sleep in these postures does not nullify, whether in prayer or otherwise. There occurred among our companions (Hanafi scholars) regarding sleeping in prostration a disagreement into five opinions: First: It is absolutely not a nullifier, which is the apparent position of the school as in al-Khulasa. Second: If he intentionally sleeps in prayer, it is a hadath (impurity), otherwise not, and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Third: It is a hadath outside prayer but not inside, chosen by the author of al-Munya. Fourth: It is not a hadath if in the prescribed posture in prayer or outside, but if outside not in that posture it is a hadath, and similarly in prayer, chosen by al-Halabi in Sharh al-Munya al-Saghir and al-Shurunbulali. Fifth: It is not a hadath in prayer absolutely, and outside prayer only if in the prescribed posture, and al-Zayla'i inclined to this. His saying: 'and fainting' — it is a type of illness that weakens the faculties and does not remove the intellect but veils it, unlike insanity which removes it. Both are like sleep in loss of choice and loss of ability to use power, indeed more severe, for the sleeper when awakened awakens, unlike the fainting person and the insane; therefore each of them is a hadath in all conditions, whether lying down, sitting, or prostrating, unlike sleep, which is only (a nullifier) when the joints relax. So in al-Bahr al-Raiq. His saying: 'and enters...' — likewise enters under it epilepsy, which is caused by touching of devils and jinn, for if an epileptic regains consciousness from his epilepsy, ablution is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the text of al-Wiqaya concerning the nullifiers of wudu. It clarifies that sleep only breaks wudu when the sleeper's joints relax (istirkha'), which typically occurs when lying down or leaning on something that, if removed, would cause falling. The author notes that the matn's expression 'without exception' (la ghayr) is imprecise; it should specify 'sleep with relaxation' because any sleep involving relaxation, even while sitting or lying on one's back, is a nullifier. The condition of 'leaning' (istinad) is also explained: it refers to resting one's weight on something such that if removed, the sleeper would fall—this is the true criterion. Two hadiths are cited: one from Ahmad and one from al-Bayhaqi, showing that sleep in standing, sitting, bowing, or prostrating does not break wudu until one lies down (yadṭajiʿ). Ibn al-Humam declared the hadith chain good due to multiple routes. The passage then details five intra-Hanafi opinions on sleeping while prostrating (sajid). The first opinion, that it never nullifies, is the apparent position of the school. The second, attributed to Abu Yusuf, distinguishes intentional sleep in prayer. The third limits nullification to outside prayer. The fourth distinguishes based on posture (sunna posture vs. non-sunna). The fifth makes a distinction between inside and outside prayer with posture. This shows the richness of Hanafi scholarship. Finally, fainting (ighmāʾ) and insanity (junūn) are discussed: both always break wudu because they involve total loss of control, unlike sleep where a person can be easily roused. Epilepsy (ṣarʿ) is included under fainting.

Key Terms
الاسترخاء (al-istirkhā’): Relaxation of joints; the state where the body loses tension and support.
المستند (al-mustanad): One who leans on something such that if it were removed, he would fall.
الإغماء (al-ighmā’): Fainting; a temporary loss of consciousness due to illness, without removing intellect but covering it.
الجنون (al-junūn): Insanity; a permanent removal of intellect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which condition must be present for sleep to nullify wudu?
A) The sleeper must be lying down.
B) The sleeper's joints must be relaxed.
C) The sleep must be outside of prayer.
D) The sleeper must be unconscious.

Key Takeaway
Sleep nullifies wudu only when accompanied by joint relaxation (istirkha'), which typically occurs when lying down or leaning unsupported; fainting and insanity always nullify regardless of posture.

Ruling on Fainting and Madness Nullifying Wudu
Defines fainting and madness as wudu nullifiers with comparison to sleep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that fainting and madness nullify wudu in all circumstances and explain the reasoning (talil) that they are akin to deep sleep.

Core Idea
Fainting and madness always break wudu because they involve a greater loss of control than deep sleep.

The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines two important nullifiers of wudu: fainting (ighma') and madness (junun). While sleep only breaks wudu under specific conditions (deep sleep with relaxed joints), fainting and madness are considered events in every state. This lesson will clarify the definitions and the legal analogy (talil) that establishes their ruling.

Guided Path
1
Define the terms
Identify the definitions of 'ighma' and 'junun' as given in the text. 'Ighma' is an illness that weakens faculties and covers the intellect; 'junun' removes it entirely.
Focus on the distinction: covering vs removing intellect.
2
Identify the ruling
Note that both fainting and madness are always nullifiers (hadath) of wudu, regardless of the person's posture (lying, sitting, prostrating).
Absolute rule; no conditions.
3
Understand the talil (reasoning)
Grasp the analogy: these states involve a greater loss of control than sleep, which is the basis for sleep breaking wudu. Since fainting/madness are more severe, they are even more deserving of nullification.
The text says: 'They are like sleep but stronger.'
4
Contrast with sleep
Recognize that sleep only nullifies wudu when the joints relax (deep sleep), whereas fainting/madness always nullify. This is due to the inability to be easily woken or respond.
Other schools may differ, but Hanafis have this detailed view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإغماء، والجنون) على أي هيئة كانا، ويدخل في الإغماء وقد وقع لأصحابنا في النوم ساجداً اختلاف على أقوال خمسة : الأول : إنّه غير ناقض مطلقاً، وهو ظاهر المذهب على ما في «الخلاصة». الثاني : إنّه إن تعمد النوم في الصلاة فهو حدث، وإلا فلا، وهو المروي عن أبي يوسف. الثالث : إنه حدث خارج الصلاة غير حدث فيها ، واختاره صاحب «المنية». الرابع : إنّه ليس بحدث إذا كان على الهيئة المسنونة في الصلاة كان أو خارجها، وإن كان خارجها لا عليها فهو حدث، وكذا في الصلاة، واختاره الحلبي في «شرح المنية الصغير»، والشرنبلالي. الخامس : إنه ليس بحدث في الصلاة مطلقاً، وخارج الصلاة إن كان على الهيئة المسنونة، وإليه مال الزيلعي.

[٢]قوله : ويدخل ... الخ ؛ وكذا يدخلُ فيه الصرع، وهو ما يكون بسبب مس الشياطين والجن) ، فإنّ المصروعَ إذا أفاق من صرعِهِ يجب عليه الوضوء . كما في التاتارخانية عن فتاوی الحجة.
And fainting and madness (in any state they occur, and includes in fainting...). And there occurred among our scholars regarding sleep while prostrating a difference of five opinions: First: it is absolutely non-nullifying, and that is the apparent position of the madhhab according to al-Khulasah. Second: if he intentionally sleeps in prayer, it is an event, otherwise not, and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Third: it is an event outside prayer but not an event inside it, chosen by the author of al-Munyah. Fourth: it is not an event if it is in the sunnah posture whether inside or outside prayer, but if outside prayer not in that posture it is an event, and likewise in prayer, chosen by al-Halabi in Sharh al-Munyah al-Saghir and al-Shurunbulali. Fifth: it is not an event in prayer absolutely, and outside prayer if in the sunnah posture, and al-Zayla'i inclined to this. [1] His statement: al-ighma' is a type of illness that weakens the faculties and does not remove intellect but covers it, unlike madness for it removes it. Both are like sleep in the loss of choice and loss of use of ability, rather stronger, for the sleeper if woken wakes up, unlike the fainted and the mad; therefore each of them is an event in all circumstances, whether lying, sitting, or prostrating, unlike sleep, for it is only (an event) if his joints relax. As in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [2] His statement: 'and includes...' etc.; likewise includes epilepsy (sar'), which is due to the touching of devils and jinn, for the epileptic when he recovers from his epilepsy must perform wudu. As in al-Tatarkhaniyyah from Fatawa al-Hujjah.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It establishes that fainting (ighma') and madness (junun) are always nullifiers of wudu, regardless of the person's posture. The author defines ighma' as a disease that weakens the faculties and covers the intellect, while junun removes it entirely. Both states involve a greater loss of control than sleep, as a sleeper can be awakened but a fainted or mad person cannot. Therefore, they are always considered hadath (ritual impurity) requiring wudu. The passage also discusses five opinions regarding sleep during prostration, noting the varying conditions under which sleep breaks wudu. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to the commentary is that sleep only breaks wudu if the joints relax, indicating deep sleep. Additionally, epilepsy (sar') is included under fainting, and recovery from it necessitates wudu.

Key Terms
الإغماء (al-ighma'): A type of illness that weakens the faculties and covers the intellect without removing it.
الجنون (al-junun): Insanity that removes the intellect completely.
الصرع (al-sar'): Epilepsy caused by the touch of devils or jinn.
النوم (al-nawm): Sleep that breaks wudu only if the joints are relaxed (deep sleep).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Sleep while lying down

Key Takeaway
Fainting and madness are absolute nullifiers of wudu, unlike sleep which only breaks wudu when the joints are relaxed.
