Defining Intoxication for Nullification of Wudu
Establishes the correct definition of intoxication for wudu nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi threshold for intoxication that nullifies wudu and distinguish it from the threshold for hadd punishments.

Core Idea
Intoxication nullifies wudu when the person staggers, based on custom, not when totally unconscious.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: what degree of intoxication breaks wudu? The author of the commentary clarifies that the relied-upon view is based on customary understanding (staggering), not the extreme standard of hudud. We'll explore the proof and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested element
The definition of 'sukr' (intoxication) for wudu nullification. What exactly is being disputed?
The dispute is about the threshold, not whether intoxication nullifies (agreed).
2
State the author's position
The correct limit is when movement enters the walk (staggering). This is the 'sahih' view.
3
Identify the opposing view (Qadi Khan)
Qadi Khan uses the hudud standard: total loss of awareness (not knowing earth from sky).
This is a difference within the Hanafi school.
4
Examine the proof for each side
Author relies on 'urf (custom): people call such a person drunk. Qadi Khan relies on qiyas with hudud.
'Urf is a recognized source in Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
A person who staggers but is still aware breaks wudu according to the author, but not according to Qadi Khan. The relied-upon view is the author's.
Practical impact: many cases of mild intoxication nullify wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السكر، وحده هنا : أن يدخل في مشيته تحرُّك هو الصحيح"، وكذا في اليمين، حتى لو حلف أنه سكران ، يعتبر هذا الحدّ .
[١] قوله : السكر ؛ هو بالفتح ، مصدر لسكر يسكر ، كتعب، والكسر لغة، وأما بالضم فهو اسم منه. كذا في «المصباح المنير »، وهو حالة تعرّض من امتلاء دماغه من الأبخرة المتصاعدة من الخمر وغيره من المسكرات.
[٢]قوله : وهو الصحيح ) ؛ احتراز عما اختاره قاضي خان في «فتاواه» من حده هاهنا هو الحد المعتبر في «باب الحدّ : وهو أن يبلغ بسكره إلى أن لا يعرف شيئاً حتى الأرض من السماء.
[٣] قوله : يعتبر هذا الحدّ ؛ لأنّ مبنى الأيمان على العرف، ومن دخل في مشيه تحرك وتمايل يعد من السكارى عرفاً.
Intoxication; its limit here is that movement enters his walk – that is the correct (view). And likewise in oaths, so if he swears that he is drunk, this limit is considered. [1] His statement: al-sukr – with fatha, is a verbal noun of sakara yaskaru like ta'iba, and with kasra it is a language (variant), and with damm it is a noun from it. As in al-Misbah al-Munir, and it is a state that occurs from fullness of his brain from vapors rising from wine and other intoxicants. [2] His statement: 'that is the correct' – is a reservation against what Qadi Khan chose in his Fatawa that its limit here is the limit considered in the chapter of hudud: that he should reach in his intoxication to the point that he does not know anything, even the earth from the sky. [3] His statement: 'this limit is considered' – because the foundation of oaths is on custom, and one whose walk enters movement and swaying is considered among the drunkards customarily.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of intoxication (sukr) as a nullifier of wudu. The author clarifies that the correct threshold for intoxication in matters of wudu and oaths is when a person staggers in their walk (i.e., shows visible unsteadiness). This is based on common custom ('urf). The author distinguishes this from the definition used in hadd penalties, where a person must be completely unaware (not knowing earth from sky). The passage also notes a disagreement with Qadi Khan, who applied the stricter hudud definition here. The commentary explains the linguistic forms of 'sukr' and describes it physiologically as vapors from intoxicants filling the brain. Thus, the Hanafi position is that intoxication nullifies wudu when it affects walking, not necessarily total loss of consciousness.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sukr): Intoxication; a state of drunkenness from any intoxicant. The verbal noun form is 'sukr' (with fatha) as per the source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, intoxication nullifies wudu when the person visibly staggers, based on custom, not total unconsciousness.

Loud Laughter Nullifies Wudu in Prayer
States the ruling and conditions for loud laughter breaking wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which loud laughter nullifies wudu according to the Hanafi school and identify which prayers are excluded.

Core Idea
Loud laughter breaks wudu only in prayers with ruku' and sujud, and only for adults.

You may have heard that laughing during prayer invalidates it, but did you know it can also break your wudu? The Hanafi school has a specific ruling: loud laughter (qahqaha) during a prayer that includes bowing and prostration nullifies both the prayer and the wudu. This lesson unpacks the conditions, exceptions, and scope of this unique ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Identify the act being discussed (qahqaha during salah) and its two legal consequences: (1) the prayer becomes invalid, (2) wudu is nullified. Note that this is unique to the Hanafi school.
Other schools hold that only the prayer is invalidated, not wudu.
2
Isolate the conditions
From the text, extract the conditions for this ruling: the person must be an adult (baligh), and the prayer must be one that includes ruku' and sujud (i.e., not the funeral prayer).
The text explicitly excludes the child's laughter and the funeral prayer.
3
Apply the ruling to various scenarios
The text states the ruling applies equally to imam, individual, obligatory, voluntary, man, or woman. Test these: e.g., a woman laughing loudly during a nafl prayer breaks wudu.
There is no distinction in application beyond the two conditions.
4
Distinguish loud laughter from other laughter
Understand that only 'qahqaha' (audible, forced laughter) triggers the nullification. Smiling or slight laughter does not break wudu. The text implies this by specifying 'loud laughter'.
The nuance: laughter audible to others is what counts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقهقهة مصل بالغ يركع ويسجد) حتى لا ينقض الوضوء قهقهة الصبي. وشرطه : أن يكون في صلاة ذات ركوع وسجود ، حتى لو قهقه في صلاة الجنازة، التاتارخانية عن فتاوی الحجة.
[٤]قوله : وقهقهة مصل ؛ أشار بإطلاقه إلى أنها ناقضة في الصلاة إماماً كان أو منفرداً، مفترضاً كان أو متطوعاً، رجلاً كان أو امرأة، وفي التقييد بالمصلّي أشار إلى أنها لا تنقض الوضوء خارج الصلاة.

[4] His statement: 'and loud laughter of a praying person' – he indicated by its absolute expression that it is a nullifier in prayer whether imam or individual, obligatory or voluntary, man or woman, and by restricting it to the praying person he indicated that it does not break wudu outside prayer.

Commentary
This passage states that loud laughter (qahqaha) by an adult during a prayer that includes bowing (ruku') and prostration (sujud) nullifies both the prayer and wudu. The laughter of a child does not break wudu because a child is not fully accountable. The condition that the prayer must have ruku' and sujud excludes funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) because it has no such postures. The ruling applies to all types of obligatory and voluntary prayers, and to both men and women. Importantly, loud laughter only breaks wudu if it occurs during prayer; laughter outside prayer does not affect wudu. This is a well-established Hanafi ruling.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Loud laughter; audible laughter that can be heard by others.
مصل (musalli): One who is performing prayer.
بالغ (baligh): An adult who has reached puberty and is legally accountable.
ركوع (ruku'): Bowing in prayer.
سجود (sujud): Prostration in prayer.

Key Takeaway

Legal Definition of Sukr (Intoxication)
Defining the legal threshold for intoxication in fiqh contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi definition of sukr (intoxication) for oaths and distinguish it from the hudud definition.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, intoxication for oaths is defined by an unsteady gait, not by total loss of awareness.

The term 'sukr' (intoxication) appears in legal contexts like oaths and criminal penalties. However, its definition varies depending on the ruling. This lesson examines the correct legal threshold for intoxication in general fiqh matters, especially oaths, and why the Hanafi school distinguishes it from the severe intoxication required for hadd punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Recognize that the passage defines 'sukr' for legal purposes, specifically for oaths and similar contexts.
The term is analysed with its linguistic forms (fatha, kasra, damm).
2
Extract the core definition
The correct definition is that one's walking becomes unsteady (دخل في مشيته تحرك). This is stated as 'al-sahih' (the correct view).
This definition relies on observable physical change.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare with Qadi Khan's definition from the Book of Hudud: complete loss of cognition (does not know earth from sky). The text rejects this for oaths.
Qadi Khan's view is also Hanafi but not preferred for this context.
4
Understand the role of custom
The basis for oaths is custom ('urf). Customarily, a person with unsteady gait is considered intoxicated.
This principle ('mabna al-ayman 'ala al-urf') is key to the definition.
5
Apply the definition
In a hypothetical oath, if a person shows unsteady gait, he is legally intoxicated according to this view.
The definition must be used consistently in all non-hudud contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السكر، وحده هنا : أن يدخل في مشيته تحرك هو الصحيح، وكذا في اليمين، حتى لو حلف أنه سكران ، يعتبر هذا الحدّ . قوله : السكر هو بالفتح، مصدر لسكر يسكر، كتعب، والكسر لغة، وأما بالضم فهو اسم منه. كذا في «المصباح المنير» ، وهو حالة تعرّض من امتلاء دماغه من الأبخرة المتصاعدة من الخمر وغيره من المسكرات. قوله : وهو الصحيح احتراز عما اختاره قاضي خان في «فتاواه» من حده هاهنا هو الحد المعتبر في «باب الحدّ : وهو أن يبلغ بسكره إلى أن لا يعرف شيئاً حتى الأرض من السماء. قوله : يعتبر هذا الحدّ لأنّ مبنى الأيمان على العرف، ومن دخل في مشيه تحرك وتمايل يعد من السكارى عرفاً.
Intoxication: its definition here is that one's walking becomes unsteady. And that is the correct definition. Likewise in oaths, even if he swears that he is intoxicated, this definition is considered. Commentary: 'al-sukr' is with fatha, a verbal noun, as in 'sakara yaskaru' like 'ta'iba', and kasra is a dialect. As for damm, it is a noun from it. So in Al-Misbah al-Munir. It is a state arising from the filling of his brain with vapors rising from wine and other intoxicants. 'And that is the correct definition' is a precaution against what Qadi Khan chose in his Fatawa: that the definition here is the one considered in the Book of Hudud: that he reaches a state where he does not know anything, even the earth from the sky. 'This definition is considered' because the basis of oaths is custom, and whoever in his walking becomes unsteady and swaying is considered intoxicated customarily.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the legal meaning of intoxication (sukr) in Hanafi fiqh. The author states that the correct definition for legal purposes is when a person's walking becomes unsteady, not the extreme state of total unconsciousness used in criminal hudud. This is relevant in oaths where the term 'sukr' is used; the custom ('urf) determines the meaning. The commentary clarifies the linguistic forms and criticizes Qadi Khan's view that equates it with the severe intoxication of the fixed punishment, arguing that for oaths and similar matters, the common custom is what matters.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sukr): The state of intoxication where one's gait becomes unsteady, as per the correct legal definition in Hanafi fiqh for oaths and general contexts.
اليمين (al-yamin): Oath, particularly in the context of swearing about intoxication.

Ruling on Laughter Breaking Wudu During Prayer
Ruling that laughter invalidates wudu and prayer with conditions and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on laughter (qahqaha) during prayer, identify its conditions, and apply the rule to different prayer types (e.g., janazah, tilawah).

Core Idea
Audible laughter in a prayer with ruku and sujud breaks both wudu and prayer – but only for adults.

This passage from Al-Hidayah's commentary clarifies a unique Hanafi ruling: that audible laughter not only invalidates the prayer but also breaks wudu. This is an exception to the general principle that only exiting impurities break wudu. The ruling is based on a specific hadith and is strictly limited to its conditions: adult pray-ers in prayers that include bowing and prostration. Understanding this ruling helps you distinguish between different types of prayer and between laughter and smiling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qahqaha (audible laughter) during prayer. The ruling is that it breaks wudu and invalidates the prayer both must be repeated.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from al-Tabarani: people laughed during prayer; the Prophet ordered them to repeat wudu and prayer.
This is a single narration (ahad) and thus zanni in transmission.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The command to repeat wudu directly proves that laughter breaks wudu; the command to repeat prayer proves invalidity.
The causative link is the laughter itself.
4
Note the conditions
The ruling applies only to adults (baligh), only in prayers with ruku and sujud (not janazah or tilawah), and only to audible laughter (qahqaha).
Silent smiling (tabassum) does not break wudu.
5
Understand the principle of 'confined to its source'
Since the ruling is based on a dalil contrary to qiyas, it cannot be extended by analogy to other situations (e.g., laughter outside prayer or in other prayers).
This restricts the application to exactly what the hadith mentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقهقهة مصل بالغ يركع ويسجد) حتى لا ينقض الوضوء قهقهة الصبي. وشرطه : أن يكون في صلاة ذات ركوع وسجود، حتى لو قهقه في صلاة الجنازة أو سجدةِ التَّلاوة لا تنقض الوضوء، بل يبطل ما قهقه فيه ، وإِنَّما شَرَطَ ما ذُكِر ؛ لأن انتقاض الوضوء بها ثبت بالحديث على خلاف القياس) ، فيقتصر على مورده . قوله : ثبت بالحديث ؛ وهو ما أخرجه الطبراني عن أبي موسى الأشعري : بينما رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يُصلِّي بالناس إذ دخل رجل فتردّى في حفرة كانت في المسجد ، وكان في بصره ضرر، فضحك كثير من القوم وهم في الصلاة فأمر رسول الله مَن ضحك أن يعيد الوضوء والصلاة». قوله : فيقتصر على مورده حاصله : أن نقض الوضوء بالقهقهة مما لا مجال للقياس فيه، وإنما قلنا به لورودِ الحديث، وكلُّ ما ثبت بخلاف القياس لا يقاس عليه غيره، بل يقتصر على مورده، ومورد الحديث الذي نحن فيه إنما هو صلاة البالغين ذات الركوع والسجود، فلا يتعدى حكم النقض إلى خارج الصلاة، ولا إلى صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة، ولا إلى صلاة الصبي.
And laughter of a praying adult who bows and prostrates (so that the laughter of a child does not break wudu). Its condition: that it be in a prayer that has bowing and prostration, so if he laughs in the funeral prayer or prostrating of recitation, it does not break wudu, but rather invalidates what he laughed in. And he only stated what was mentioned because the breaking of wudu by it is established by hadith contrary to analogy, so it is confined to its source. Commentary: 'established by hadith' and it is what al-Tabarani narrated from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: While the Messenger of Allah was leading the people in prayer, a man entered and fell into a hole that was in the mosque, and his eyesight was weak, so many people laughed while in prayer, and the Messenger ordered those who laughed to repeat the wudu and the prayer. 'So it is confined to its source' means that breaking wudu by laughter is not subject to analogy, and we only say it due to the hadith, and everything established contrary to analogy is not analogized to others, but confined to its source, and the source of the hadith here is only the prayer of adults with bowing and prostration, so the ruling of breaking does not extend outside prayer, nor to funeral prayer or prostrations of recitation, nor to the prayer of a child.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the effect of laughter (qahqaha) during prayer. The Hanafi position is that audible laughter in a prayer that includes ruku and sujud breaks both the wudu and the prayer. The ruling is specific: it applies only to adults, only in prayers with bowing and prostration (so not janazah or tilawah), and only to laughter that is audible (qahqaha). The evidence is a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered those who laughed in prayer to repeat both wudu and prayer. The commentary emphasizes that this ruling is an exception to the general principle that only external impurities or exiting of the two passages break wudu; it is based solely on this specific narration and cannot be extended by analogy. Therefore, laughter outside prayer does not break wudu, and laughter in other forms of prayer (like janazah) does not break wudu, although it invalidates the prayer itself.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Audible laughter with a sound, as opposed to silent smile. Specifically, laughter that is heard by others.
صلاة ذات ركوع وسجود (salat dhat ruku' wa sujud): A prayer that includes bowing and prostration, i.e., the five daily prayers and other similar prayers, excluding funeral prayer and prostrations of recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Laughter in any religious gathering.
D) Only laughter in the Friday prayer.

Key Takeaway
Audible laughter (qahqaha) during a prayer with ruku and sujud breaks wudu and invalidates the prayer, but only for adults and only in those specific prayers.

Qahqaha in Prayer: When Does It Break Wudu?
Ruling on qahqaha breaking wudu and prayer with proof and limitations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha in prayer, apply the conditions of being awake and fard prayer with ruku' and sujud, and distinguish between qahqaha, dahiik, and tabassum.

Core Idea
Qahqaha in fard prayer breaks both wudu and prayer – but only because a specific hadith overrides qiyas.

The Hanafi school holds a unique position that loud laughter (qahqaha) during certain prayers invalidates both the prayer and the wudu. This ruling is not based on analogical reasoning but on a specific hadith that restricts the effect to the context of adult fard prayers with ruku' and sujud. In this lesson, we unpack the hadith evidence, the conditions, and the boundaries of this exceptional ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is qahqaha? It is loud laughter heard by the laugher and his neighbors. The ruling: it breaks both wudu and prayer, but only in adult fard prayers with ruku' and sujud, and only if the person is awake.
The act must occur within the prayer, not outside it.
2
Locate the Dalil
The proof is the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered those who laughed in prayer to repeat wudu and prayer. This is a zanni thubut hadith, but it is acted upon.
The hadith is from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari as narrated by al-Tabarani.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The command 'a'eedu al-wudu' indicates obligation (wujub) per the Hanafi usul principle that amr yufid al-wujub. The ruling is restricted to the hadith's context because it contradicts qiyas.
The restriction is key: qiyas would not break wudu by laughter, so the text is taken literally without extension.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i and others hold that qahqaha does not break wudu at all; it only invalidates the prayer itself. They argue the hadith is weak or interpret the command differently.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the apparent meaning of the hadith and considers it has shawahid (supporting narrations). Since it is a text contrary to qiyas, it is confined to its text.
The mufta bihi is that qahqaha breaks wudu only in the specific context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو سجدةِ التَّلاوة لا تنقض الوضوء، بل يبطل ما قهقه فيه ، وإِنَّما شَرَطَ ما ذُكِر ؛ لأن انتقاض الوضوء بها ثبت بالحديث على خلاف القياس) ، فيقتصر على مورده . . . . ثم القهقهة إنما تنقض الوضوء إذا كان يقظاناً حتى لو نامَ في الصَّلاةِ على أي هيئة فقهقه لا ينقض الوضوء . وعند الشافعي : لا ينقض الوضوء بالقهقهة. وحدها : أن تكون مسموعة له والجيرانه . والضحك : أن يكون مسموعاً لهُ لا لجيرانه، وهو يبطل الصَّلاةَ لا الوضوء . والتبسم : أن لا يكون مسموعاً أصلاً ، وهو لا يبطل شيئاً.
Or the prostration of recitation does not break wudu, rather it invalidates what he laughed in. And he only specified what was mentioned because the breaking of wudu by it is established by hadith contrary to qiyas, so it is restricted to its occurrence. . . . Then qahqaha only breaks wudu if he is awake, such that if he slept in prayer in any manner and laughed, it does not break wudu. And according to al-Shafi'i: it does not break wudu by qahqaha. And its definition: that it be heard by him and his neighbors. And dahiik: that it be heard by him but not by his neighbors, and it invalidates the prayer, not wudu. And tabassum: that it is not heard at all, and it invalidates nothing.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the nullification of wudu and prayer by qahqaha (loud audible laughter) during salah. The author first clarifies that qahqaha during a sajdah tilawah does not break wudu—instead it invalidates only the act of laughter in prayer (i.e., the prayer itself becomes invalid). The reason the ruling is restricted to adult prayers with ruku' and sujud is that the nullification of wudu by qahqaha is established by a specific hadith (narrated by al-Tabarani and others) that operates contrary to qiyas (analogical reasoning). Since it contravenes the general principle that only exiting impurities break wudu, it is confined to its precise textual occurrence (mawrid al-hadith) and cannot be extended by analogy. Thus, it does not apply outside salah, nor to janazah prayer, sajdah tilawah, or a child's prayer. The condition of being awake (yaqzān) is emphasized: if one is asleep and laughs, wudu is not broken because the ruling is a deterrent (zajr) and a sleeping person is not subject to deterrence. The author then notes the Shafi'i position: qahqaha does not break wudu at all. The passage concludes by defining three levels: qahqaha (heard by the laugher and his neighbors)—breaks wudu and prayer; dahiik (heard only by the laugher)—breaks prayer only; tabassum (not heard at all)—breaks nothing.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud laughter audible to the person and his neighbors, occurring during prayer.
الضحك (al-dahiik): Laughter audible to the person himself but not to his neighbors.
التبسم (al-tabassum): A smile that produces no sound; inaudible.
يقظان (yaqzān): Awake, as opposed to asleep. The condition for qahqaha to break wudu is that the person be awake.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on qahqaha (loud laughter) during a janazah prayer?
A) It breaks both wudu and prayer.
B) It breaks prayer but not wudu.
C) It breaks wudu but not prayer.
D) It breaks neither wudu nor prayer.

Key Takeaway
Qahqaha breaks wudu only in adult fard prayers with ruku' and sujud, while the person is awake – a strict restriction based on the hadith's text contrary to qiyas.

Laughter (Qahqaha) Nullifies Wudu If Awake in Prayer
Ruling on qahqaha nullifying wudu with definitions and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on loud laughter in prayer, distinguish it from ordinary laughter and smiling, and explain the evidence for the nullification of wudu.

Core Idea
Only loud laughter (qahqaha) nullifies wudu in prayer; ordinary laughter and smiling do not.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the Hanafi position on laughter during prayer, detailing three distinct categories: loud laughter (qahqaha), ordinary laughter (dahk), and smiling (tabassum). Each has a different legal effect on wudu and prayer. The text also addresses the Shafi'i disagreement and provides the methodological basis for the ruling through dalalat al-nass (indication of the text), not qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of laughter
Read the definitions of qahqaha, dahk, and tabassum. Note the audibility criterion: qahqaha is audible to the person and his neighbors, dahk is audible only to the person, tabassum is not audible.
The classification is based on sound, not content.
2
State the ruling for each type
Qahqaha nullifies wudu if the person is awake in prayer. Dahk invalidates the prayer but not wudu. Tabassum does not invalidate anything.
The ruling for qahqaha is conditioned on being awake; al-Karkhi differed on the sleeping case.
3
Analyze the condition of being awake
Understand why being awake is necessary: the nullification is a deterrent (zajr), and a sleeper is not subject to deterrence.
This is a key difference from al-Karkhi who held that qahqaha nullifies wudu even in sleep.
4
Examine the Shafi'i dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that qahqaha does not nullify wudu at all. The Hanafi school responds by distinguishing qahqaha through dalalat al-nass, arguing that the greater loss of composure requires a stricter ruling.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether one must repeat wudu after loud laughter in prayer.
5
Trace the evidence (dalil)
The proof for dahk not nullifying wudu is a hadith from al-Daraqutni (weak chain). The ruling for qahqaha is derived via dalalat al-nass, not qiyas, meaning it is attached by textual indication to the same category as intentional wudu nullifiers.
This shows the Hanafi use of usul al-fiqh to derive rulings beyond explicit texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القهقهة إنما تنقض الوضوء إذا كان يقظاناً حتى لو نامَ في الصَّلاةِ على أي هيئة فقهقه لا ينقض الوضوء (١٢). وعند الشافعي له : لا ينقض الوضوء بالقهقهة. وحدها : أن تكون مسموعة له والجيرانه (۳). والضحك : أن يكون مسموعاً لهُ لا لجيرانه، وهو يبطل الصَّلاةَ لا الوضوء ). والتبسم : أن لا يكون مسموعاً أصلاً ، وهو لا يبطل شيئاً). قلت : هما ملحقان بالوضوء القصدي بطريق دلالة النصّ لا بطريق القياس، وفي المقام أبحاث أخر مذكورة في «السعاية»، وفي «الهسهسة». [١] قوله : يقظان ؛ وهو بفتحات، خلاف النائم، وجه اشتراطه : أنّ انتقاض الوضوء بالقهقهة إنما هو زجراً ، والنائم ليس من أهله ، وفيه خلافُ الكرخي حكم بانتقاض وضوء النائم أيضاً بالقهقهة. [٢]قوله : لا الوضوء ؛ وهذا بالإجماع كما في «جامع المضمرات»، لما أخرجه الدارقطني بسند ضعيف مرفوعاً: «الضحك ينقض الصلاة ولا ينقض الوضوء
Then, loud laughter (al-qahqaha) nullifies wudu only if one is awake, such that if he sleeps in any posture in prayer and laughs loudly, it does not nullify wudu. According to al-Shafi'i, it does not nullify wudu by loud laughter. Its definition: it is audible to him and his neighbors. And al-dahk (laughter): it is audible to him but not to his neighbors, and it invalidates the prayer but not the wudu. And al-tabassum (smiling): it is not audible at all, and it does not invalidate anything. I say: Both are attached to intentional wudu by way of indication of the text (dalalat al-nass), not by way of analogy. And in this context there are other investigations mentioned in al-Si'ayah and al-Hashashi. [1] His statement 'yagzan' (awake): it is with fatha, opposite of sleeping. The reason for its condition is that the nullification of wudu by loud laughter is a deterrent, and the sleeper is not qualified for that. There is a difference from al-Karkhi: he judged that the wudu of the sleeper is also nullified by loud laughter. [2] His statement 'not the wudu': this is by consensus, as in Jami' al-Mudmarat, because al-Daraqutni reported with a weak chain marfu'an: 'Laughter invalidates the prayer but does not invalidate the wudu.'
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on different types of laughter in prayer: loud laughter (qahqaha) nullifies wudu if the person is awake; ordinary laughter (dahk) invalidates the prayer but not wudu; and smiling (tabassum) does nothing. The Shafi'i position is also noted: according to al-Shafi'i, loud laughter does not nullify wudu at all. The commentator clarifies that the condition of being awake is essential because the nullification of wudu by qahqaha is a deterrent (zajr), and a sleeper is not subject to this. The proof for dahk not nullifying wudu is a hadith from al-Daraqutni. The author (likely the commentator) states that the connection of qahqaha and dahk to wudu nullification is through dalalat al-nass (indication of the text), not qiyas. The marginal note mentions a difference from al-Karkhi, who held that a sleeping person's wudu is also nullified by qahqaha.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): A loud laughter that is audible to the person and his neighbors.
الضحك (al-dahk): Laughter that is audible to the person but not to others.
التبسم (al-tabassum): A smile that is not audible at all.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Loud laughter (qahqaha) in prayer nullifies wudu only when the person is awake; ordinary laughter (dahk) invalidates prayer but not wudu, and smiling (tabassum) does nothing.

Direct Intimate Contact and Worm Exit as Wudu Nullifiers
Ruling on direct skin contact and worm exit from anus as wudu nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on mubasharah fahishah and worm exit as wudu nullifiers, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between the Sahibayn and Muhammad, and apply the distinctions in practice.

Core Idea
Direct intimate contact nullifies wudu because the cause is treated as the effect; Muhammad requires actual impurity exit.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses two distinct nullifiers of wudu: direct intimate contact (mubasharah fahishah) and the exit of a worm from the anus. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the first, with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf considering it a nullifier, while Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees unless impurity actually exits. The second ruling is straightforward: a worm from the anus nullifies wudu due to its filthy origin, unlike a worm from a wound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Rulings
Read the passage and note that it covers two separate issues: (1) direct intimate contact and (2) worm exit. Each has its own ruling and reasoning.
2
Locate the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
For direct intimate contact, identify the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two) and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understand the basis of each position.
The text states: 'except according to Muhammad'.
3
Analyze the Reasoning (Talil)
Understand why the two (Sahibayn) consider mubasharah fahishah a nullifier: because it is a common cause for the exit of madhi (pre-ejaculate), so the cause is placed in the position of the effect (istihsan).
The Arabic phrase: 'فيقام السبب مقام المسبب'.
4
Examine the Nuances
Note additional points: the condition of genitals touching (al-Karkhi required it), the extension to women and beardless youth, and the different view of later scholars like al-Attabi who considered Muhammad's view mufta bihi.
The text mentions these in the marginal notes.
5
Distinguish the Worm Case
For the worm, note the key distinction: a worm from the anus nullifies because it comes from a filthy place; a worm from a wound does not because the wound is considered pure in terms of ruling.
The reason is given as 'because it is pure' (لأنها طاهرة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمباشرة الفاحشة إلا عند محمد ه ، ودودة خرجت من دُبُرٍ لا التي خرجت من جُرح) ؛ لأنها طاهرة) [١] قوله : والمباشرة الفاحشة ؛ من باشرَ الرجلُ المرأة : إذا أفضى بشرته إليها، والمراد بالفحش هاهنا الظهور، لا ما نهى عنه الشارع ، إذ قد تكون بين الرجل وزوجته، والوجه في كونها ناقضة على ما هو قولهما أن مثل هذه سبب غالب لخروج المذي ، فيقامُ السبب مقامَ المسبب وصحح قولهما في «التحفة» (١). [٢]قوله : إلا عند محمد له ؛ فإنّها ليست من النواقض عنده ما لم يخرج شيء من المذي ونحوه ، وصححه العتابي (٢) في «فتاواه» ، وصاحب «النصاب»، وجعله صاحب «حَلْبة المجلي» أوجه، وقال البرجندي : أكثر الكتب متظاهرة على أنه الصحيح المفتى به (۳). [٣] قوله : بدن المرأة ؛ وكذا المباشرة الفاحشة بين المرأتين، وبين الرجل والغلام الأمرد تنقض الوضوء عندهما، خلافاً لمحمد ه. كذا في «القُنية». [٤]قوله : وتماس ؛ ظاهرُ الرواية عدم اشتراط تماس الفرجين، وشرطه الكرخي، وصححه الإسبيجابي. كذا في «البحر»(4). [0] قوله : لأنها طاهرة ؛ أشار بذلك إلى وجهين للفرق بين دودة الجرح وبين دودة الدبر :
And direct intimate contact (al-mubasharah al-fahishah) [nullifies wudu] except according to Muhammad. And a worm that came out from the anus [nullifies], not one that came out from a wound, because it is pure. [1] His statement: 'al-mubasharah al-fahishah': from 'baashara' a man a woman: meaning he brought his skin to hers. What is meant by 'fahishah' here is apparent (zuhur), not what the Lawgiver prohibited, for it may occur between a man and his wife. The reason for it being a nullifier according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that such an act is a common cause for the exit of madhi, so the cause is placed in the position of the effect. And the view of the two was considered correct in al-Tuhfah. [2] His statement: 'except according to Muhammad': for it is not among the nullifiers according to him unless something like madhi actually comes out. And al-Attabi considered it correct in his Fatawa, and the author of al-Nisab, and the author of Hulbat al-Mujalli considered it possible, and al-Barjandi said: Most books agree that it is the correct and mufta bihi view. [3] His statement: 'body of the woman': and likewise, direct intimate contact between two women, and between a man and an adolescent boy, nullifies wudu according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. This is in al-Qunyah. [4] His statement: 'and the touching of the two genitals': the apparent narration is that touching of the two genitals is not a condition, but al-Karkhi conditioned it, and al-Isbijabi considered it correct. This is in al-Bahr. [0] His statement: 'because it is pure': he pointed by that to two aspects of the difference between the worm from a wound and the worm from the anus:
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage covers two wudu nullifiers: (1) direct intimate contact (mubasharah fahishah) between a man and a woman, and (2) a worm that exits from the anus. For (1), the Hanafi school has a dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that such contact nullifies wudu because it is a common cause for the exit of madhi (pre-ejaculate fluid). Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees; according to him, no wudu nullification occurs unless actual madhi or similar substance exits. The commentator notes that some later scholars (al-Attabi, etc.) consider Muhammad's view as the correct one and fatwa is given upon it, while many books uphold the view of the two. The ruling also applies to contact between women and between a man and a beardless youth. There is also a difference on whether touching of the two genitals is a condition; al-Karkhi conditioned it. For (2), a worm that comes out from the anus nullifies wudu because it comes from a filthy place (the anus is a source of impurity), while a worm from a wound does not nullify because the wound itself is considered pure in terms of the ruling.

Key Terms
المباشرة الفاحشة (al-mubasharah al-fahishah): Direct skin-to-skin contact between a man and a woman (or similar) with arousal, such that the two genitals may touch.
دودة (dudah): A worm that exits from the anus; it nullifies wudu because it comes from a filthy place. A worm from a wound does not nullify because the wound is pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a worm from a wound not nullify wudu?
A) Because the wound is considered pure (tahir) in terms of the ruling.
B) Because the worm is too small to cause impurity.
C) Because the wound is not a source of filth like the anus.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Direct intimate contact nullifies wudu according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) based on istihsan; a worm from the anus nullifies, but not from a wound.

Nullifiers of Wudu: Direct Intimate Contact, Worm Exit, Meat Fall, and Touching Women
Commentary on specific nawaqid al-wudu with intra-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on four specific nullifiers of wudu and identify the intra-madhab difference on mubasharah fahishah.

Core Idea
In taharah, the ruling changes when the cause is predominant—mubasharah nullifies, but ordinary touch does not.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details four specific nullifiers of wudu that often cause confusion. The commentary clarifies the conditions for each, especially the critical difference between direct intimate contact (mubasharah fahishah) and ordinary touching, and the unique rulings on worm exit and meat falling from a wound. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correct wudu maintenance in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Define mubasharah fahishah
Identify the precise conditions: nakedness, arousal, contact of private parts. Note that this is a specific type of touch, not general contact.
The term 'fahishah' here means 'manifest' not 'illicit'—it occurs between spouses too.
2
Analyze the intra-madhab dispute on mubasharah
Contrast the position of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (nullifies based on istihsan) with Imam Muhammad's view (requires actual emission). Understand the reasoning: treating the predominant cause as the effect.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view—nullification upon contact.
3
Explain the worm exit ruling
Distinguish between a worm from the anus (nullifies) and from a wound (does not). Learn the two reasons: (1) pure thing exiting the two passages nullifies; (2) the worm inevitably carries a small impurity, which from the two passages nullifies, but from elsewhere does not unless abundant.
The pure worm or small impurity from a wound is excusable (afw).
4
State the ruling on meat falling from a wound
Meat is pure and the moisture is minimal, so it does not nullify wudu. This is an extension of the principle that only exit from the two passages or significant impurity nullifies.
This ruling is straightforward but often confused.
5
Prove that touching a woman does not nullify
Cite the hadith of Aisha (radiyallahu anha) that the Prophet ﷺ kissed her and prayed without wudu. Understand that this establishes general touching does not nullify, even with desire.
This is the Shafi'i point of ikhtilaf, but Hanafis maintain the hadith overrides qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمباشرة الفاحشة إلا عند محمد ، ودودة خرجت من دبر لا التي خرجت من جرح (المباشرة الفاحشة) : وهي أن يماس بدنه بدن المرأة مجردين، وانتشار الته، وتماس الفرجان. ودودة خرجت من دبر لا التي خرجت من جرح؛ لأنها طاهرة [١] قوله : والمباشرة الفاحشة من باشر الرجل المرأة إذا أفضى بشرته إليها، والمراد بالفحش هاهنا الظهور لا ما نهى عنه الشارع إذ قد تكون بين الرجل وزوجته، والوجه في كونها ناقضة على ما هو قولهما أن مثل هذه سبب غالب لخروج المذي فيقام السبب مقام المسبب وصحح قولهما في «التحفة» [٢] قوله : إلا عند محمد فإنها ليست من النواقض عنده ما لم يخرج شيء من المذي ونحوه وصححه العتابي في «فتاواه» وصاحب «النصاب» وجعله صاحب «حلبة المجلي» أوجه وقال البرجندي أكثر الكتب متظاهرة على أنه الصحيح المفتى به [٣] قوله : بدن المرأة وكذا المباشرة الفاحشة بين المرأتين وبين الرجل والغلام الأمرد تنقض الوضوء عندهما خلافاً لمحمد كذا في «القنية» [٤] قوله : وتماس ظاهر الرواية عدم اشتراط تماس الفرجين وشرطه الكرخي وصححه الإسبيجابي كذا في «البحر» [٥] قوله : لأنها طاهرة أشار بذلك إلى وجهين للفرق بين دودة الجرح وبين دودة الدبر الأول إن الدودة حيوان طاهر في الأصل وإن كان أصله من النجس والطاهر إذا خرج من أحد السبيلين نقض الوضوء كالريح بخلاف غيرها فإن الخارج الطاهر منه لا ينقض كالدمع والعرق والثاني إن الدودة لا تخلو عن قليل نجاسة وقليل النجاسة إذا خرجت من أحد السبيلين نقض الوضوء ومن غيرهما لا ينقض إلا إذا كان كثيراً [٦] قوله : قليلة إطلاق النجاسة على القليل باعتبار اللغة وإلا فقد مر أن ما ليس بحدث ليس بنجس [٧] قوله : ولحم سقط منه لأن اللحم طاهر في نفسه وما يتصل به من البلة قليل [٨] قوله : ومس المرأة أي لا ينقض مس الرجل المرأة مطلقاً ولو كان بشهوة لحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها «إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قبلها ثم خرج إلى الصلاة ولم يتوضأ» أخرجه ابن ماجة والترمذي وأبو داود وابن أبي شيبة والنسائي وغيرهم وسنده لا بأس به ولحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها «كنت أنام بين يدي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم
And direct intimate contact except according to Muhammad, and a worm that comes out of the anus, not that which comes out of a wound. (Direct intimate contact): That is that his body touches the woman's body when both are naked, with erection, and the two private parts touch. And a worm that comes out of the anus, not that which comes out of a wound, because it is pure. [1] His saying: 'And direct intimate contact': from 'bashara' meaning a man touched a woman skin to skin. What is meant by 'fahishah' here is manifest, not what the Lawgiver prohibited, as it can be between a man and his wife. The reason it is a nullifier according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that such contact is a prevalent cause for the emission of madhiy, so the cause is treated as the effect. And the two's view is authenticated in al-Tuhfah. [2] His saying: 'Except according to Muhammad': For it is not a nullifier according to him unless something like madhiy actually emerges. And al-ʿAttabi authenticated this in his Fatawa, and the author of al-Nisab, and the author of Hilbat al-Mujalli considered it as a valid opinion. And al-Birjandi said: Most books indicate that this is the correct and fatwa-worthy opinion. [3] His saying: 'the woman's body': Likewise, direct intimate contact between two women, and between a man and a beardless boy, nullifies wudu according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad. Thus in al-Qunyah. [4] His saying: 'and contact': The apparent narration does not require the touching of the two private parts, though al-Karkhi conditioned it and al-Ispijabi authenticated that. Thus in al-Bahr. [5] His saying: 'because it is pure': He indicated two aspects for the difference between a worm from a wound and a worm from the anus. First: The worm is originally a pure animal, even if its origin is from impurity. When something pure exits from one of the two passages, it nullifies wudu, like wind, unlike other exits, for pure exit from other than the two passages does not nullify, like tears and sweat. Second: The worm is not free from a small amount of impurity. When a small amount of impurity exits from one of the two passages, it nullifies wudu; from other than them it does not nullify unless it is abundant. [6] His saying: 'a small amount': Calling it impurity with respect to small amount is linguistically, otherwise it has passed that what is not hadath is not impure. [7] His saying: 'and a piece of meat that fell from it': Because meat is pure in itself and the moisture attached to it is small. [8] His saying: 'and touching a woman': That is, a man's touching a woman does not nullify wudu at all, even if it is with desire, due to the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) kissed her, then went out to prayer and did not perform wudu.' It was narrated by Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Nasa'i and others, and its chain is acceptable. And due to the hadith of Aisha: 'I used to sleep in front of the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him)...'
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the nullifiers of wudu from the matn of Al-Wiqayah. It covers four issues: direct intimate contact (mubasharah fahishah), a worm exiting from the anus, a piece of meat falling from a wound, and touching a woman.

1. Direct intimate contact (mubasharah fahishah): This refers to skin-to-skin contact between a naked man and woman with arousal and contact of the private parts. According to Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), this nullifies wudu based on istihsan, because such contact is a predominant cause for the emission of madhiy (pre-seminal fluid). Even if no emission is certain, the cause is treated as the effect to avoid hardship. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, requiring actual emission of madhiy or similar; mere contact is not a nullifier. Later scholars like al-ʿAttabi and the author of al-Nisab supported Muhammad's view, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the two Sahibayn as authenticated in al-Tuhfah. The same ruling applies to contact between two women or a man and a beardless boy. There is also a difference on whether the touching of the two private parts is necessary; the apparent narration says no, but al-Karkhi and al-Ispijabi conditioned it.

2. A worm exiting from the anus nullifies wudu, but a worm exiting from a wound does not. Two reasons are given: (a) The worm is originally pure, and if a pure thing exits from one of the two passages (front or back), it nullifies wudu like wind. But if it exits from elsewhere, it does not. (b) The worm inevitably carries a small amount of impurity. A small impurity exiting from the two passages nullifies wudu, but from elsewhere it does not unless abundant. The small amount of impurity on the worm is excusable (ʿafw) when it exits from a wound.

3. A piece of meat that falls from a wound does not nullify wudu because meat is pure and the moisture on it is minimal.

4. Touching a woman does not nullify wudu, even with desire. This is proven by the hadith of Aisha (رضي الله عنها) that the Prophet (ﷺ) kissed her and then prayed without performing wudu. Another hadith mentions she slept in front of him. Thus, general touching of women is not a nullifier, unlike the specific case of direct intimate contact mentioned earlier.

Key Terms
مباشرة فاحشة (mubāsharah fāḥishah): Direct skin-to-skin contact between a naked man and woman with arousal and contact of the private parts.
نواقض الوضوء (nawāqiḍ al-wuḍūʾ): Actions or events that nullify the state of wuḍūʾ, requiring its renewal for acts of worship.
دودة (dūdah): A worm that exits from the body, ruling depends on the exit point: from anus nullifies wuḍūʾ, from a wound does not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements about the mufta bihi Hanafi position on nullifiers of wudu is correct?
A) Direct intimate contact (mubasharah fahishah) nullifies wudu even without emission of madhiy.
B) A worm exiting from a wound nullifies wudu because it carries impurity.
C) A piece of meat falling from a wound nullifies wudu because it is impure.
D) Touching a woman always nullifies wudu, as per the hadith of Aisha.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats predominant cause as effect in mubasharah; worm exit nullifies only from the two passages; ordinary touch does not nullify wudu.

Worm or Meat Falling from a Wound Does Not Break Wudu
Explaining the ruling and two justifications for meat/worm from a wound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a worm or meat falling from a wound and explain the two justifications for why it does not break wudu.

Core Idea
Exit from a wound does not break wudu; only exits from the two passages do.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding wudu. The source passage addresses a practical scenario: a worm or piece of meat falls from a wound. Does this break wudu? The author provides two justifications, both leading to the same conclusion. By understanding this, you will grasp a key principle: the location of exit matters for nullifying wudu.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The ruling is that a worm or meat falling from a wound does not break wudu. Confirm this from the passage.
2
Examine the first justification (pure origin)
The worm is considered pure in origin. If a pure substance exits from the two passages, it breaks wudu. But here it exits from a wound, not the passages, so it does not break wudu, just like tears.
This uses qiyas khafi: analogy with tears/sweat.
3
Examine the second justification (small impurity)
The worm may carry a small amount of impurity. A small impurity exiting from the two passages breaks wudu, but from elsewhere it does not unless substantial. Hence, this also does not break wudu.
The marginal note clarifies that calling it 'najasa' is linguistic; legally it's not najis.
4
Apply the same reasoning to meat
Meat from a wound is pure, and any moisture on it is negligible. Therefore, the same ruling applies: it does not break wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولحم سقط منه (1) : أي من جرح. الأول : إن الدودة حيوان طاهر في الأصل وإن كان أصله من النجس، والطاهر إذا خرج من أحد السبيلين نقض الوضوء كالريح، بخلاف غيرها، فإن الخارج الطاهر منه لا ينقض، كالدمع والعرق. والثاني : إن الدودة لا تخلو عن قليل نجاسة، وقليل النجاسة إذا خرجت من أحد السبيلين نقض الوضوء، ومن غيرهما لا ينقض إلا إذا كان كثيراً. [1] قوله : قليلة ; إطلاق النجاسةِ على القليل باعتبار اللغة، وإلا فقد مر أن ما ليس بحدث ليس بنجس. (2) قوله : ولحم سقط منه ; لأن اللحم طاهر في نفسه، وما يتصل به من البلة قليل.
And meat falling from it (a wound): it means from a wound. First: The worm is originally a pure animal even if its origin is from najas, and if a pure thing exits from one of the two passages, it breaks wudu like wind, contrary to other (passages), for a pure exit from them does not break wudu, like tears and sweat. Second: The worm is not free from a small amount of impurity, and a small amount of impurity when exiting from one of the two passages breaks wudu, but from other than them it does not break unless it is abundant. [1] His saying: 'a small amount' – the application of the term najas to a small amount is linguistic, otherwise it has passed that what is not hadath is not najas. (2) His saying: 'and meat falling from it' – because meat is pure in itself, and the moisture attached to it is small.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a worm or piece of meat that falls from a wound. It does not break wudu. The commentary presents two justifications. The first: the worm is a pure animal, and if a pure substance exits from the two passages (front and back), it breaks wudu; but here it exits from a wound, not from a passage, so it does not break wudu, just like tears or sweat. The second: the worm likely has a small amount of impurity, and a small impurity exiting from the two passages breaks wudu, but exiting from elsewhere does not unless it is substantial. The marginal note clarifies that the term 'small impurity' is used loosely; strictly speaking, what is not hadath is not impure. The commentary also explains that meat is pure and any moisture on it is negligible, so it does not break wudu.

Key Terms
دودة (dūdah): A worm that may emerge from a wound; considered pure in origin by some Hanafi scholars
نجاسة (najāsah): Impurity; here applied to a small amount on the worm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why does a worm falling from a wound not break wudu?
A) Because the worm is considered pure and the wound is not one of the two passages.
B) Because the worm is impure but the amount is large.
C) Because the worm is considered impure but exiting from a wound does not break wudu regardless.
D) Because the worm is considered pure but exiting from any opening breaks wudu.

Key Takeaway
A worm or meat falling from a wound does not break wudu because it exits from a non-passage and is either pure or carries negligible impurity.

Touching a Woman Does Not Break Wudu
Citing hadith to prove touching a woman does not nullify wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching a woman and cite the primary hadith proof.

Core Idea
Touching a woman does not break wudu, even with desire—based on the Prophet's actions.

This lesson examines a well-known point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether touching a woman nullifies wudu. The Hanafi school holds that it does not, relying on authentic hadith of the Prophet's actions. We will analyze the proof and understand the legal rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the Hanafi position: touching a woman does not break wudu, absolutely and even with desire.
2
Locate the primary hadith proof
The text cites the hadith of 'Aisha: the Prophet kissed her and then prayed without wudu.
This is a clear action (fi'l) of the Prophet, which is a source of law.
3
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling
The Prophet's action of kissing and then praying without renewing wudu indicates that touch does not nullify ablution.
The Hanafi school accepts ahad hadith as valid proof for rulings.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i interprets the Quranic verse 'aw lamastumu al-nisa' as referring to skin contact, requiring wudu.
The Hanafi response is that the verse refers to sexual intercourse, not mere touch.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, a Hanafi can shake hands or kiss without breaking wudu, while a Shafi'i would need a barrier or renew wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومس المرأة أي لا ينقض مس الرجل المرأة مطلقاً، ولو كان بشهوة ; لحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها : «إنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قبلها ثم خرج إلى الصلاة ولم يتوضأ»، أخرجه ابن ماجة والترمذي وأبو داود وابن أبي شيبة والنسائي وغيرهم، وسنده لا بأس به، ولحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها : «كنت أنام بين يدي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ورجلاي في قبلته، فإذا سجد غمزني فقبضتهما، فإذا قام بسطتهما)، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم والنسائي وغيرهم.
And touching a woman: meaning that a man touching a woman absolutely does not break wudu, even if with desire, based on the hadith of 'Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) kissed her and then went out to prayer and did not perform ablution,' narrated by Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Nasa'i and others, and its chain is acceptable; and based on the hadith of 'Aisha: 'I used to sleep in front of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) with my legs in his qibla, and when he prostrated he pressed me and I pulled them, and when he stood I stretched them,' narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, al-Nasa'i and others.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling that touching a woman, even with desire, does not break wudu. The proof is the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as reported by 'Aisha: he kissed her and then prayed without renewing wudu. Another hadith shows that 'Aisha would lie in front of the Prophet during prayer, and he would touch her feet when prostrating, yet he did not repeat wudu. These hadith serve as strong evidence that skin contact with a woman does not nullify ablution, contrary to the opinion of al-Shafi'i and others.

Key Terms
مس (mass): Physical contact; specifically touching a woman
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire; even if contact occurs with desire, it does not break wudu

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a man touching a woman with desire?
A) It breaks wudu.
B) It does not break wudu.
C) It breaks wudu only if the woman is a mahram.
D) It breaks wudu only if there is no barrier.

Key Takeaway
Touching a woman, even with desire, does not break wudu in the Hanafi school, based on the Prophet's actions.

Touching the Genital Does Not Break Wudu: Hanafi Position and Refutation of al-Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling on genital touching as a nullifier of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that touching the genital does not break wudu, explain the proof from the hadith of Talq, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Touching the genital does not break wudu — it is merely a piece of your body, as the Prophet said.

In this passage, the Hanafi school asserts that touching the penis, even without a barrier, does not invalidate wudu. This ruling is based on a direct prophetic statement. The passage then contrasts the view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who requires wudu after such touch, and provides the Hanafi rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: touching the penis does not break wudu, even without a barrier. This is the core claim of the passage.
2
Identify the Hanafi dalil
Locate the proof: the hadith of Talq where the Prophet said 'Is it but a piece of your body?' This hadith is recorded by multiple narrators and considered hasan.
The dalil is a direct negation of the need for wudu.
3
State the opposing view
Recognize the Shafi'i position: touching the penis (and touching a woman) breaks wudu. The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand why Hanafis reject the Shafi'i proof: they interpret Quranic 'lamastum' as intercourse, and consider the hadith on touching penis weaker than Talq's hadith.
The passage calls the discussion lengthy and refers to 'al-Si'ayah' for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذكر أي لا ينقض مس الذكر وإن كان بلا حائل ; لحديث طلق : «أنه سأل رسول الله عن رجل مس ذكره : أيتوضأ ؟ فقال : هل هو إلا بضعة منك» أخرجه الترمذي وأبو داود وابن ماجة والنسائي وغيرهم، وبعض طرقه حسن يحتج به. [2] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له ; فإنه ذهب إلى أن مس المرأة بلا حائل ثوب ناقض، وكذا عند مالك الله بشرط وجود اللذة والشهوة ; لقوله : أَوْ لَمَسْتُمُ النساء (4)، وأصحابنا جعلوا اللمس كناية عن الجماع. وكذا مس الذكر إذا كان بلا حائل ناقض الحديث : «من مس ذكره فليتوضأ)، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة، والكلام في هاتين المسألتين طويل الذيل، من شاء الاطلاع عليه فليرجع إلى «السعاية».
And the genitals (i.e., touching the penis does not break wudu even if without a barrier) based on the hadith of Talq: 'He asked the Messenger of Allah about a man who touched his penis: should he perform ablution? He said: Is it but a piece of your body?' narrated by al-Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Nasa'i and others, and some of its chains are hasan, acceptable as proof. [2] His saying: contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he held that touching a woman without a garment barrier breaks wudu, and likewise according to Malik with the condition of pleasure and desire, due to His saying: 'or if you have touched women' (Quran 4:43). And our scholars interpreted 'touch' as a metaphor for intercourse. Likewise, touching the penis without a barrier breaks wudu according to the hadith: 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform ablution,' narrated by the four Sunan. The discussion on these two issues is lengthy; whoever wishes to look into it should refer to 'al-Si'ayah'.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that touching the penis does not break wudu, even without a barrier, based on the hadith of Talq where the Prophet said it is just a piece of the body. Then it contrasts the position of al-Shafi'i, who holds that touching a woman without a barrier breaks wudu, and Malik, who requires pleasure. Shafi'i also holds that touching the penis breaks wudu. The Hanafi response is that the Quranic word 'lamastum' in verse 4:43 is interpreted metaphorically as intercourse, not simple touch. The hadith 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu' is weak in the Hanafi view or overridden by the stronger hadith of Talq. The text refers to 'al-Si'ayah' for a more detailed treatment.

Key Terms
بضعة (bid'ah): A piece or part of the body; here, the penis is called a piece of the body
لمس (lams): Touch; in Quranic context interpreted as intercourse by Hanafis

Checkpoint

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Touching the Penis and Women Does Not Nullify Wudu
Dispute on whether touching private parts or women breaks wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on touching the penis and women regarding wudu, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Touching your own penis or a woman does not break wudu in the Hanafi school — only intercourse does.

One of the most practical differences between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerns what nullifies wudu. In this passage, the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah states the Hanafi position clearly: touching the penis or a woman, even skin-to-skin, does not break wudu. He then contrasts this with the Shafi'i and Maliki views. The disagreement hinges on how we interpret the Quranic word 'lams' and which hadith to prioritize. Let's walk through the evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on touching the penis and women as stated in the text. The author says 'touching the penis does not break wudu even without a barrier'.
The Hanafi position is clearly summarised in the first sentence.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The text mentions al-Shafi'i and, with a condition, Malik. State their positions: touching a woman breaks wudu (Shafi'i), or if accompanied by desire (Malik). They also hold that touching the penis breaks wudu.
The opponents rely on a different set of evidences.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent cites Quran 4:43 'aw lamastumu al-nisa' (or you have touched women) and the hadith 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu'.
The word 'lams' is taken literally by the Shafi'i school.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis interpret 'lams' as a metaphor for intercourse, not literal touch. They also rely on the hadith of Talq, where the Prophet said the penis is 'just a piece of you' – implying that touching it does not require wudu. The hadith about touching the penis is considered weak or abrogated.
This is a classic example of tarjih (preference) based on interpretation and chain strength.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What changes in practice? Hanafis do not need to renew wudu after such touches; Shafi'is do. This affects daily acts like shaking hands, changing diapers, or marital intimacy without intercourse.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is the key reason this ikhtilaf matters.

والذكر
والذَّكَر ) خلافاً للشَّافِعِي
عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
ورجلاي في قبلته ، فإذا سجد غمزني فقبضتهما ، فإذا قام بسطتهما)، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم والنسائي وغيرهم. [1] قوله : والذكر ؛ أي لا ينقصُ مس الذكر وإن كان بلا حائل ؛ لحديث طلق : «أنه سأل رسول الله له عن رجل مس ذكره : أيتوضأ ؟ فقال : هل هو إلا بضعة منك (۳) أخرجه الترمذي وأبو داود وابن ماجة والنسائي وغيرهم، وبعض طرقه حسن يحتج به.
[٢] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي (۳) له ؛ فإنه ذهب إلى أن مس المرأة بلا حائل ثوب ناقض، وكذا عند مالك الله بشرط وجودِ اللذة والشهوة ؛ لقوله : أَوْ لَمَسْتُمُ النساء (4) ، وأصحابنا جعلوا اللمس كناية عن الجماع.
(٤)
وكذا مس الذكرِ إذا كان بلا حائل ناقض الحديث : «من مس ذكره فليتوضأ) ، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة، والكلام في هاتين المسألتين طويل الذيل، من شاءَ الاطلاع عليه فليرجع إلى «السعاية»(3).
And the penis. (And the penis) contrary to al-Shafi'i. 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. "And my two legs in his qiblah, so when he prostrated he pressed them, and when he stood he spread them" — narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, al-Nasa'i and others. [1] His statement: "And the penis" — meaning that touching the penis does not break wudu even if without a barrier, due to the hadith of Talq: "He asked the Messenger of Allah ﷺ about a man who touched his penis: should he perform wudu? He said: 'Is it but a piece of you'" — narrated by Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Nasa'i and others; some chains are hasan and can be used as proof. [2] His statement: "contrary to al-Shafi'i" — for he (al-Shafi'i) held that touching a woman without a barrier of clothing is a nullifier, and similarly with Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) with the condition of pleasure and desire, due to His statement: "or you have touched women" [4:43]; and our companions interpreted "touching" as a metaphor for intercourse. (4) Similarly, touching the penis without a barrier is a nullifier according to the hadith: "Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu" — narrated by the authors of the four Sunan; and the discussion on these two issues is lengthy; whoever wishes to study it should refer to "al-Sa'ayah" (3).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning whether touching the penis or a woman breaks wudu. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: touching the penis does not break wudu, even without a barrier. The proof is the hadith of Talq ibn 'Ali, where the Prophet ﷺ said it is just a piece of the body. The text notes that some chains of this hadith are hasan and acceptable as evidence. Then the author mentions that al-Shafi'i holds that touching a woman without a barrier does break wudu, and Malik holds the same but with the condition of pleasure or desire. Their proof is the verse 'aw lamastumu al-nisa' (or you have touched women). The Hanafis interpret 'lamastum' as a metaphor for intercourse, not literal touch, based on other evidence. The passage also notes that the hadith 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu' is used by the opposing school, but the Hanafi response is that this hadith is abrogated or weak; the author however does not elaborate here but refers to a detailed discussion in 'al-Sa'ayah'.

Key Terms
مس الذكر (mass al-dhakar): Touching the penis with the hand, with or without a barrier
لمس (lams): Touching (skin-to-skin contact)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does touching one's own penis without a barrier break wudu?
A) Yes, it breaks wudu
B) No, it does not break wudu
C) It breaks wudu only if done intentionally
D) It breaks wudu only if accompanied by desire

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, touching the penis or a woman does not break wudu; only actual intercourse does.
